THE RED PAGE.
SPRING ODE.
Spring days have come ! and a livelier iris
(Whatever that may be) shines oh the dove.
Spring time ! when all that we poets require is
Any fresh word that will rhyme with our Love.
Through the green woodlands, flower-burdened, fern-laden
Saunters to meet me, my rustic sweetheart :
How shall I rhyme her—shy, simple maiden ?
Cart ? dart ?—oh, hang it! here goes for a start 1
Fairest of dryads, with rose-blushes tinted,
Stanzas I’d pen to thee dozens of times ;
Passionate sapphics as ever were printed
But the devil’s own bother is—finding the rhymes !
N.S.W.
NORTON MALSEWARD.
The V a mpire.
HOW well I remember my first love,—with
w S v L ! fted busks and her stuffed hair!
We did not meet in the summer, nor in
« , green grass near running water, but in
winter,—under the pale glare of gas, in the wet
street and among people.
+1 her face so bleached
that her black eyes and red lips positively glared :
yet she had a silky fascination for me. And her
w 1 han , ds brought not only flesh into
contact, but mind.
8 a d > ® he WilS hungry. I had just drawn
my months earnings, and we went arm-in-arm
to a little green-curtained restaurant. It was
sufficiently wicked-looking, -the gas low, and the
solitary waiter unobstreperous.
I was drunk. The fumes of the cheap Bur-
gundy started an orchestra in my ears, and
?J, el l ed th ®. of my brain. The wind
lifted the blind and blew my cigarettes in a
wnr ring covey off the table. I stooped to
pick them up, but the blood-strings in my
throat stood out, and the room darkened to
m y eyes. The waiter noiselessly came to my
assistance, replacing them on a plate and retain-
ing a few slyly up his sleeve. But I said nothing,
fingers°° klng at Mar ? uei 'i te and her long, folded
She ordered some oysters, and, when they had
®° me : , watched her squeeze a lemon-quarter into
the shells and over the firm fish.
* * * *
I seemed to be in a garden with Marguerite.
Ihe garden was full of lilies—tall white lilies
without a speck or mark; and everywhere
amongst them were blue flies, trumpeting and
buzzing with pleasure. Here and there a rich
bee, with powdered legs, swayed on a flower, like
a jewel in snow. The air was warm and soft as
down while the rounded sound of bubbling water
poppled m the moss, between the bars of lily-
stalks. A delicious sweetness of earth and honey
mounted to my brain. I watched a butterfly
winged m old-gold and grey, as he flickered on a
red tile under the steady shadow of a fern.
Gradually I grew aware of the subtle electrical
hand upon my wrist—then of the eyes that made
my mind hers,—nay, my very soul. The small
piercing eyes, whose pupils diminished and en-
larged, and enlarged and diminished, like the
flame of a dying lanfp. And every time the pupils
diminished, they seemed to me two miser hands
that gripped my brain and squeezed myself from
me, like water from a cloth, opening only to grin
again. b 1
But the woman, in the body, was tall, and
breasted like a young girl; her back was straight
as an arrow, and her neck reared white as a rock-
wave carrying the magnificent head on its summit.
She had a broad brow, but somewhat slanting; a
sll &ht nose, an eagerly insolent mouth,
and the eyes I have spoken of. Her hair, where
it was loose, shimmered and shook as though over
a heat-mist.
Presently she lifted her hands from my wrists
and I felt her fingers thrill through my temples,
as she drew me towards her and kissed me on the
bp 3 - A. song seemed to set up in the garden, and
the lilies shot up like stars, swayed in the
sky, meeting in an arch, and crossed in stormy
rushes over our heads. The noise of the water
rose clamorously, and a flight of coloured birds
brushed my shoulder. A soft sensation went over
the whole of my skin, like the dropping of a
delicate veil.
And still she drew me closer.
I tried to resist, but, as in a nightmare, my
arms remained limp and paralysed. Neither
could I cry out. All at once, in. the midst of a
million kisses, she drew back her head, and with
a gasping laugh, pushed her red lips at my throat
and bit me deep, even to blood.
In vain I beat her about the face, and plucked at
her cheeks. She hung like a dog. With horrible
little laughs and gurgles she greeted my impotent
rage.
• I P ut , both bands to her forehead, and made to
thrust her from me ; but again my muscles failed.
And she bit deeper.
Then the lilies withered down from the skies
and lay stained and yellow on the earth The
butterfly lost its old-gold, and its wings and whole
form broadened into a bat’s. The soft ripple of
the water changed to the purring of a flame, and
bale-fires leapt from every corner.
Still the woman tore at my throat.
My breath shortened, and I felt as though mv
skull were contracting and injuring my brain.
Spasm after spasm shot through my head. Goaded
to madness, I hurled my tormentor away, and as
she returned at me, bloody-mouthed, I saw that
she too had changed. Her eyes had sunk, her
teeth were old and wasted to an appearance of
cloves; her nose seemed flattened, and her hair
thin Her face was almost simian.
My strength failed me, and I staggered on my
feet. “Marguerite,” I cried. “Woman!
Vampire!
And I fell clean to the ground, like a tree in a
storm.
* * * *
A great coldness rushed upon me, and an icv
breath fanned my forehead. I could feel a pair
of hands beneath my arm-pits. I was smothering
1 here was a bandage round my mouth.
I opened my eyes. There were stars-thou-
sands of them—blinking and blinking; but below,
turbid and swollen, lay the river without a light
for miles. Far away I could hear the platter of
horses on wet roads, and, nearer, the raucous
laugh of a woman.
The hands withdrew suddenly; and a man
darted from me, fleeing up the steep stoned bank.
It was the waiter of the restaurant. The bandage
dropped off my jaw, and I could now understand
the sweet scent of my garden dream.
My pockets were as empty as on the day when
3usffin theS made '' that WilS a fore &°* ie con -
Marguerite never again crossed my path. Yet
I know that green-curtained restaurant, and some
day I shall see her standing in the doorway. If
she beckons, I must go to her.
Because I dare not refuse.
SYD.
W.W. LAMBLE.
S. Gilbert Reminiscent.
IjN this fashion talked W. S. Gilbert when
recently interviewed by William Archer,
that leaden and conscientious dramatic
critic who has turned literary censor. Of
Gilbert’s first meeting with Sullivan :
Oddly enough, on our very first meeting, I posed
Sullivan with a musical problem. It was at the old
“ Gallery of Illustration,” then occupied by the German
Reeds, for whom I had written several short pieces.
Frederick Clay introduced me to Sullivan, and I deter-
mined to play off upon him a piece of musical clap-trap
which I happened to have in my mind. I had just com-
pleted a three-act blank-verse play called “ The Palace
of Truth” for the Haymarket Theatre. One of the
characters in that play is a musical pedant, and it
occurred to me to convert one of his speeches
into prose and to try its effect on Sullivan. So I
said to him : I’m very glad to have the pleasure
of meeting you, Mr. Sullivan ; for you will be able
to decide a question which has just arisen between my
friend Fred Clay and myself. I maintain that, if a com-
poser has a musical theme to express, he can express it as
perfectly upon the simple tetrachord of Mercury, in which
(as I need not tell you) there are no diatonic intervals
at all, as upon the much more complicated dis-
diapason (with the four tetrachords and the redun-
dant note), which embraces in its perfect consonance
all the simple, double, and inverted chords.” Sullivan
appeared to be impressed by the question, which,
he said, he could not answer off-hand. He said he would
take it away and think it over. He must have thought
it over for about thirty years, for I never received an
answer to the question. I obtained my musical facts
from the “ Encyclopaedia Britannica," under the head
Harmony." I took a sentence and put it into blank
verse without any idea as to what it may have meaht.
• * * *
As to which of the collaborators “ thought of it
first,”—says Gilbert:
When I collaborated with Sullivan, the verse always
preceded the music, or even any hint of it. Sometimes—
' er - v rarel y Sullivan would say of some song I had given
him, ‘My dear fellow, I can’t make anything of this’—
and then I would rewrite it entirely—never tinker at it.
But of course I don’t mean to say that I ‘ invented ’ all
the rhythms and stanzas in the operas. Often a rhythm
would be suggested by some old tune or other running in
my head, and I would fit my words to it more or less
exactly. When Sullivan knew I had done so, he would
say, * Don’t tell me what the tune is, or I sha’n’t be able
to get it out of my head.’ But once, I remember, I did
tell him. There is a duet in 4 The Yeoman of the Guard,’
beginning:
I have a song to sing, O 1
Sing me your song, O !
It was suggested to me by an old chantey I used to hear
the sailors on board my yacht singing in the ‘ dog watch ’
on Saturday evenings, beginning :
Gome and I will sing you.
What will you sing me ?
I will sing you one, O 1
What is your one, O !
and so on. Well, when I gave Sullivan the words of the
duet he found the utmost difficulty in setting it. He
tried hard for a fortnight, but in vain. I offered to recast
it in another mould, but he expressed himself so delighted
with it in its then form that he was determined to work
it out to a satisfactory issue. At last he came to me and
said : ‘ You often have some old air in your mind which
prompts the metre of your songs : if anything of the kind
prompted you in this case, hum it to me—it may help
me.’ Only a rash man ever asks me to hum, but the
situation was desperate, and I did my best to
convey to him the air of the chantey • that had
suggested the song to me. I was so far successful that
before I had hummed a dozen bars he exclaimed, ‘ That
will do—l’ve got it! ’ And in an hour he produced the
charming air as it appears in the opera. I have some-
times thought that he exclaimed, ‘ That will do—l’ve got
it,’because my humming was more than he could bear ;
but he always assured me that it had given him the
necessary clue to the proper setting of the song.”
* * * *
This of T. W. Robertson is to the point:
Robertson was an exceedingly skilful dramatic tailor.
He knew the stage perfectly, and he knew perfectly the
company he had to write for—the then Prince of Wales’s
stock company, which varied very little. He fitted each
character with the utmost nicety to the man or woman
wfio lyas to play it; and he was there to instruct them in
every movement, every emphasis. But when these parts
are transferred to other actors who knew not Robertson,
the very nicety of their adjustment to their original
performers is apt to render them misfits. I think"that
accounts in great measure for the comparative ineffec-
tiveness of his plays in revival—their charm was so largely
dependent on Robertson’s personal inspiration.
Then Robertson invented stage-management. It was
an unknown art before his time. Formerly, in a conver-
sation scene, for instance, you simply brought down two
or three chairs from the flat and placed them in a row
in the middle of the stage, and the people sat down and
talked, and, when the conversation was ended, the chairs
were replaced. Robertson showed how to give life and
variety and nature to the scene, by breaking it up with
all sorts of little incidents and delicate by-play. I have
been at many of his rehearsals, and learnt a great deal
from them.
Robertson knew “ Oaste ” was his masterpiece. I re-
member meeting him one day when he had just conceived
the idea of the play and was quite full of it. He poured
forth the whole story to me as we walked along, and I
told him how good I thought it. He was busy with some-
thing else at the moment, and could not settle down to
write it; but he said to me, “ I pant to begin that piece.”
+
“S.T.S.” writes:
Note the growing abuse of a useful phrase. People
are “ only too glad,” or “ only too willing,” to do every,
thing nowadays, making a kind of cheerful superlative of
it. What is overlooked is that the phrase always
implies an unworthy motive underlying conduct thus :
it is really' censure when used of others, confession when
used of one’s-self. “ The wounded man’s comrades were
only too willing to assist him to the rear ” apparently
means that they' had an eye to their own greater safety
“If someone better qualified would propose this toast, :
should be only too willing to make way for him ” is
semi-humourous admission of desire to shirk a duty
But the stereotyped, “ I shall be only too glad to contri
bute to this excellent cause,” “ only too willing to show
visitors over the property,” means nothing at all.
Grant Hervey—
I advocate knocking out the middle letter in all chain-
words that end and start with the same letter. Thus:
Penib, for pen-nib —people rarely pronounce the
second letter ■,—goldust, lampost, dressuit, etc. Dropping
the letter doesn’t alter the sense ; and it helps to drag
the language into some semblance of shape.
Eng. Institute of Journalists has resolved to
examine and certify the qualifications of “young
lions,” and Lond. Morning Leader drafts a Little-
Go exam, paper : whence—
I. Under what circumstances, if any, are the following
terms admissible : “ The devouring element,” “ felt his
position acutely',” “ Jupiter Pluvius,” “ with a sickening
thud,” “ the coign of vantage,” “ transpired,” “ the pro-
ceedings then terminated,” “ under the auspices of,”
“ the sacred edifice,” “ then, as the sun rose fully ” ?
4. Compare and contrast the following phrases : “ It is
alleged,” “it is stated,” “it is announced,” “it is re-
ported,” “it is affirmed.” Consider, with special refer-
ence to the law of libel, the utility of the term “ alleged.”
5. Make any repiarks you please (short of profanity)
upon the word “ anent.”
6. Explain and distinguish : “From a correspondent,”
“from our correspondent,” “from our own correspon-
dent,” “from a special correspondent," “from our
special correspondent,” “ from our own reporters.”

J. 0.: What a drab’s life is signified by the Aus-
tralian aversion from bright colours! In this
town (Harden, N.S.W.) verandahs, rich and poor,
display some hundreds of tin flowerpots, most of
which ate painted green, while not one has two
hues, or is aught but ugly. (Of course, white,
reds, and yellows are the harmonies with foliage.)
This throws light on the selfishness which now
curses the land. Squalid minds become insensible
to the simplest and purest of joys. I write in
cold blood.
Elsa Barker, in The Smart Set—
I think that when men die and go to hell
They do not burn in fire, as prophets tell;
But that they wait, and wait, and wait, and wait
For one who never comes, loved e’er so well.
“I am tired,”—says Eleanora Duse, perhaps the
world’s greatest actress, —“I am tired of the
theatre, but not of art; tired of the theatrical
part of stage life, tired of the necessary co-opera-
tion of stage artists, managers, scene-shifters, and
lamp-ligh ters. I must rid myself of the slavery
of stage life. That’s no life at all. I assure you
it’s hell, downright hell. Yes, of many of my
companions I cannot think but with feelings of
loathing and suspicion. Many of the actors and
actresses I have met are a contemptible lot.
Once let me get away from this hell, it will never
hold me in its clutches again. This is your queen
of the footlights, the premier actress of the cen-
tury, as some call me,” she said, with bitter
mockery. “Her kingdom a hotel parlour, her
frontier a steaming kettle. And the worst of it
all, I am getting old, my life is ebbing away, and
I see nothing and know nothing.” (Duse ends her
stage career after her approaching visit to
America.)
E.G.B.—
One hears little nowadays of the Victorian Review of
the Eighties—-Mortimer Franklyn’s solid literary periodi-
cal. I was in Franklyn’s employ when Marcus Clarke’s
“ Civilisation without Delusion ” was published. At the
time the Review, although often well written, and well
produced mechanically, had not succeeded, and its circu-
lation barely reached the thousands. When Clarke’s
attack on Christianity appeared, the issue of 2000 was
sold out in three days. I never saw so many black-coats
in my life before. Every minister in Vic. seemed to come
to the office, and to purchase a copy of that number.
Franklyn was overjoyed, and used to enter the office
with large, oily smiles, playing continually with the two
magnificent diamond rings he always WMje —a sure sign
of his exceeding self-content. Another faition of 3000
was quickly exhausted. The rush at the office was like a
scramble at the ticket-box on a first night. Altogether
fully 10,000 copies were sold, and so eager were pur-
chasers that I know of <€ 1 being paid for the number.
Bishop Moorhouse’s reply and Clarke’s rejoinder put the
Review fairly on the road to prosperity ; but its literary
quality grew poor, and it went the way of flesh, losing
for the unfortunate shareholders of the World company,
as the insolvency proceedings showed, 'thousands of
pounds.
THE DEVIL’S SHILLING.
My feet are tired with trudging,
My legs are bars of lead,
The whole world seems a-grudging
A corner for a bed.
I roam about and shiver,
And to me, as I go,
The voice of the grey river
Is wafted from below.
She does not spurn or scorn me,
But calls me to her breast,
She knows that none will mourn me,
And I ask only rest.
With all my senses willing,
With eyes for death alone,
I take the Devil’s Shilling
And join The Devil’s Own !
G.F.O.

A Letter from Archdeacon Lingard.
Christchurch, N.Z.,
February 25th, ’Ol.
Dear Sirs, —
I have much pleasure in testifying to the value
of Warner’s Safe Cure.
Nearly four years ago, after having tried several
remedies for Diabetes without success, my medical
man consented to my trying Warner’s Safe Cure.
At that time there was no prospect of my living
above a few months. At first I took six tablet
spoonfuls a day. I began to improve in health
immediately, and was soon able to undertake my
regular duties. During the last four years I have
never been without the Safe Cure in the house, at
times reducing the doses to one per diem, and occa-
sionally going without altogether for a month or so.
At the present time, though not altogether
cured (at my age I can hardly expect it), I am able
to undertake the arduous duties of a large parish
single-handed, and without much fatigue.
X have no hesitation in saying that my present
fairly good state of health is due to Warner’s Safe
Cure, and I shall ever feel grateful that it came my
way.
Yours, etc.,
EDW. ATH. LINGARD,
Archdeacon of Akaroa.
Messrs. H. H. Warner & Co., Limited.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A prominent English agricultural journal
advises its readers that when the pig falls sick it
is better to send for the butcher ere the animal
loses flesh. “ The pig is a most unkindly patient,
and seldom gets well,” so it doesn’t pay to try and
heal him. This item gives a fine relish to the
prime imported York ham.
The old story of fishing with dynamite.
Writes a South Sea correspondent
Dynamite kills cruelly sometimes. William Pitt was
dynamiting fish, Aug. 17th, on the island of Florida,
Solomon Group. He applied a fire-stick to a fuse, which
apparently did not light, and he started to blow it, when
the dynamite cartridge exploded, blew some of the fingers
and thumb off his right hand, destroyed his eye, aijd
blew his face and chest full of small holes. One hole
larger than the rest went right through the big bone of
the chest and was about three-quarters of an inch in
diameter. The Govt, yacht, Lalu, was sent to bring the
man to Tulegi. There was no doctor there, but some
traders did all they could for the patient; but he died
Aw. 31. An almost similar accident killed a man named
Sheridan at the same island some time ago, and Sheridan
also took 14 days to die.
Calling.
Painter, while the mood is on me,
(I, myself, will tell the story)
Seize your brush and paint a picture,
Paint a castle, old and hoary.
Let its towers be sunset-reddened,
With green ivy-tendrils twini ig
Round about them, and its windows
Richly, radiantly shining.
Paint a woman at a window,
Watching, while the red day closes;
Let her hands be as two li ies,
And her lips be like red roses.
Make her paler than a snow-flake
Under leaden heavens falling ;
And her lips—they must be parted,
So that one might hear her calling,
Calling, calling o’er the meadow,
Where the lark’s last song is ringing,
To a man, who nods and answers,
And comes near and nearer, singing,
Singing, singing sweet love’s praises—
What, 0 what were life without it ?
And the lark must pause and listen,
With the sunset light about it.
Paint a hundred lissome maidens
Round about him, laughing, dancing,
Hair down-fallen on their shoulders,
Burning mouths and forms entrancing.
Paint the maidens offering favors
To the minstrel—roses pelting ;
Fill their eyes with salutations,
Glances soft and sweet and melting.
But the minstrel must not heed them,
Though their beauty be a wonder ;
He sees only that pale woman
At the castle window yonder.
Yea, his heart goes out before him
To that pale, fair face enthralling,
As, through leagues and leagues of twilight,
Plaintively he hears her calling.
Calling, calling ! —ah, how sweetly,
While he hurries breathless, frantic,
Comes her voice across the distance
From that castle-place romantic.
Painter, he shall never reach her !
Skies shall darken, tempests thunder,
And the ground shall gape between them,
And a sullen gulf shall sunder.
And that gulf shall hold a torrent,
Leaping, swaying, roaring, falling ;
But, above its utmost tumult,
He shall hear her ever calling.
Yea, life-long he shall be haunted
With her beauty pale, unreal :
For the man is but a poet,
And the woman his ideal.
n.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.
Memories.
A little creek, and from its bending rushes
A bell-bird’s call;
A lovers’ kiss between the long, sweet hushes
Of twilight’s fall.
A gentle face and eyes a-full of dreaming,
And rich in lustrous shine,
Which underneath their long, dark lashes beaming
Appeared divine.
A rising moon and then, with steps of leisure,
Home through the ripening wheat;
And spirits glowing with that sense of pleasure
Which Love makes sweet.
A little grave amongst the churchyard grasses
Hidden in lilac spray,
Where the spring wind makes music as it passes
In wanton play.
A man’s sad heart grown weary through life’s
dangers,
And bitter with its gall;
A fading sunset o’er the distant ranges,
And twilight’s fall.

CIGARETTES
Green-Apple Wisdom.
THE accumulated wisdom of this generation can’t keep a boy from eating green apples.
You can talk to him till you are tired, but it will do no good. He simply can’t learn from
anything but hard, pit-of-the-stomach experience. He must have his own belly-ache before he
will believe what you tell him.
Now, men are simply boys grown up. It isn’t green apples any longer, but it’s the same
old comedy with new properties. It's the question, perhaps, of roofs. It’s the old chimera of
getting value, of getting full return for honest investment. There are plenty of cheap Roofs.
“ The Woods are full of them.”
In the Full Light of Experience.
“ When the Pan-American Exposition opened at Buffalo,
P & B Ruberoid Roofing was on eleven buildings, including the
United States Government Building, on which upwards of
20,000 square yards were used. They are constantly having
trouble with the cheaper Roofings. These are being torn off in
many instances, and Ruberoid substituted.
“Arthur W. Trench.
“New York, U.S.A., July 19th, 1901.”
Why not use the green-apple common sense that you learned as a boy, and invest in a
Ruberoid Roof? When 75 per cent, of the users of Ruberoid Roofing send us repeat orders, it
ought to mean something to you. How about a trial order ?

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 16, 1901.
More About State Audit.
Mr. Hardy, an English insurance magnate,
who came out here to do some actuarial
work for the Citizens’ Life Office, had a
festive welcome, the other day, and made
certain feeling remarks. He told the people
of Australia that State interference was a
bad thing; that Britain was full of large,
substantial life-assurance offices that had
grown up without Government supervision
or meddling ; that Australia was in the same
desirable situation ; and that, in view of
these excellent results, he hoped that Par-
liament would withhold its legislative hand
and its investigating eye, and leave matters
alone. And just at present Hume Cook, of
"Victoria, has a motion oti the business paper
of the House of Representatives to the effect
that the Commonwealth Government should
establish its own life-assurance department
under Government management and guaran-
tee. Which is, perhaps, the reason why
the private life-assurance man disparages
Government interference a little harder than
usual at the present moment.
• * * •
Of course, it is perfectly true that Britain
has many large, well-managed and prosperous
life offices that have grown up without State
supervision. It has one or two that might
possibly claim to be distinctly ahead even of
Australia’s great A.M.P. Society. These
institutions have been managed on the lines
that any law of State supervision would lay
down, and the State law would make no
change in their management. It would
merely be a question of the State auditor
coming round periodically, and compli-
menting them on being up to, or even ahead
of, the State standard of solvency and pre-
caution, and telling the management that it
and its institution were an honor to the
community. Even the most cantankerous
manager of the richest office could hardly
object to that. Then the functionary would
go away to hunt-up crooked and shady offices,
and make them imitate the ways of the
admirable institutions ; and imitation is the
sincerest form of flattery. Seeing that these
great assurance institutions are so sound and
well-managed, it is strange that a proposal to
make other institutions imitate them should
cause such indignation. A certain propor-
tion of life-assurance concerns are “cronk,”
and a certain proportion of persons commit
murder. Probably the proportion of crooked
life offices is larger than the proportion of
people who commit murder, and therefore
calls for more urgent attention. And a bank-
rupt life office is often a greater tragedy than
a murder. The latter, at the worst, is bub
the killing of a person who would have died
anyhow—possibly very soon, and in a much
more painful fashion. The latter may mean
years of after disappointment and misery,
and possibly disease and crime, caused by
that misery, to the widow and orphans. A
broken life-insurance office, or bank, or
building society is often the centre of a
thousand awful tragedies.
* * * *
And, despite all their successes, there is
not too much to boast of in the results of
Britain’s policy of non-interference—or of
Australia’s either. The record of dead
British offices isn’t nice reading; nor, in
some cases, that of the live offices either.
Britain owns a long and lamentable tail of
brassy life-institutions which would be a
disgrace to any country. Their balance-
sheets have to be published certainly, but
publicity doesn’t kill them, for the average
man doesn’t know one side of a balance-
sheet from the other, and they still collect
the premiums of the foolish and ignorant.
The Yorkshire Provident, after 28 years of
hard premium-collecting, with two-thirds of
its capital disappeared and all the premium
receipts spent—the Universal Insurance L.
and I. Co., after 31 years, with £25,860 lia-
bilities and £491 of assets (this isn’t the big
Universal, but a shabby concern which
apparently took the loan of its name with trim-
mings)—the sorrowful Pioneer Co., income
for 1899 about £ll,OOO, and outgoings about
£14,000 —the Law Property, with £72,000
liabilities and about £lO,OOO of assets —the
Methodist and General (said to belong to
the Salvation Army), and which certainly
looks like it—the Independent Order of
Foresters, and others. There is one Eng-
lish life office whose accounts frankly ad-
mitted, not long since, that it can only pay
2s. in the £, and it is getting steadily worse.
At a recent actuarial investigation it was
responsible for life-policies to the amount of
£111,860, and its life-assurance fund was
worth £l7O. It has been admitting things
like this for a dozen years. Still it gets pre-
miums—a good many premiums, in fact, and
its business is actually increasing. The
absolutely-bankrupt life offices of the United
Kingdom carry the hopes of a good many
thousand families that know no better. And
then, in addition, there are institutions that
are born and die without leaving any serious
trace behind them. Two or three pages of
the Insurance Blue Book are always
devoted to the names of recently closed-up
British insurance institutions—life and
other kinds. Some have transferred
their business; some are in liquidation ;
sometimes one has just gone away and left
no address, and not paid the rent. This
lamentable tail that hangs disgracefully on
to the far end of an honorable business exists
mainly for want of State supervision.
• * * •
Whatever Britain may think of the matter
Australia will almost certainly go in for
State interference before long. There are
two or three reasons why it should. The
Independent Order of Foresters is one ; a
legal system which allows that concern to
exist, despite the fact that, if its liabilities
are calculated according to the ordinary sys-
tem, they amount to a good many times its
funds, and which also allows it to swagger
insolently and blatantly in the land as the
expounder of a new and beautiful gospel in
life-assurance, can’t be too promptly sup-
pressed. The Provident and Industrial
of Maoriland is another good reason,
and one or two more concerns among
the horde that have sprung up in
late years might also be quoted. Austra-
lasia possesses one State-managed life-assur-
ance institution, and the Maoriland Govern-
ment Department, in addition to being the
only office which offers what can be fairly
called absolute security (i.e., the security of
the State), charges lower rates than any of
its rivals, and hopelessly beats every pri-
vately-managed institution of anything like
its own size in economy of management and
general excellence of results. If Maoriland’s
State Department is taken to represent
Government interference, and all the other
offices of Australasia (striking an average
with the A.M.P. at one end of the scale, and
the blatant Independent Order of Foresters
somewhere about the other) to represent the
policy of non-interference, then the case for
State control is overwhelming. If the
Maoriland Government Department is com-
pared to British companies, then, so far as
concerns security, lowness of premiums,
cheap management, and high bonuses, the re-
sult is again largely in favor of State control.
It is the only State life assurance scheme of
any size or standing in this world, and the
lofty British person who declares State
control to be a failure has seldom taken the
trouble to call upon and examine the only
specimen available. He is accustomed to
the privately-managed variety : it is British ;
and he gets his bread and butter by it; and
anything that is British, and that provides
him with his bread and butter, must be
good.
& * * * *
If there was a legal standard of solvency
as regards life-assurance offices, a State
investigation to ascertain that the assets are
sufficient under that standai’d to cover all
liabilities, and power to close up insolvent
offices, it is practically certain that several
institutions now doing business in Austra-
lasia would go out. Thereby, there would
be more room left for the sound offices, and
with less competition they could get business
at a cheaper rate, which would be better
both for themselves and the public. There-
fore, the sound offices would benefit largely
by the proceeding. Y-et even they are
mostly hostile to the idea. One reason is
that, as business is done in Australia, it is
almost certain that even large and solid-
looking offices have occasional intervals
of insolvency—pretty long intervals in some
cases, and pretty bad insolvency. When the
great fall in the value of property came in
the early Nineties, it found almost every
office loaded up with mortgage securities, an
enormous proportion of which ceased to be
worth the amount lent on them. And hardly
one office, by reason of the struggle after big
bonuses and the high expense-rate caused
by intense competition, had any reserves
worth mentioning. They wrote off the loss
by degrees, or it is still being written off by
degrees ; and the partial recovery in pro-
perty values helped them a little ; but there
was a period when some Australian life
offices of high standing might have had a
bad time if a genuine independent audit and
valuation of securities had taken place.
Some of them might have a bad time
even now—it is difficult to say. And
as property values are only being lifted
again by a violent return to the old
borrowing policy, it is quite possible that
another slump may attack the securities
before long. A mutual life office which has
no shareholders’ capital with which to fill a
temporary shortage, which does business in
a land of inflation and violent fluctuations,
and invests most of its funds for the sake of
high interest in securities that are specially
liable to fluctuate, and which spends almost
all its profits in sensational bonuses as it
goes along, finds difficulties in a system
which would require it to show itself solvent
all the time. A really efficient State audit
and valuation would make it necessary to
sacrifice a lot of the bonuses for a number of
years, in order to build up large permanent
reserves and provide for the next slump in
advance. That would be unpleasant for the
management, but good—very good—in the
end for the public. Unfortunately, the State
audit idea only began to take serious hold in
Australia about the time when some even of
the big life offices of this country could least
stand auditing.
• • • •
Also, the State audit idea began to grow
up in Australia just about the time that its
banks and building societies were least
anxious to get into the limelight and demon-
strate their solvency. The State audited the
Q.N. Bank a little and the Bank of New
Zealand a good deal, and the things it found
out about private enterprise were painful to
mention. In New South Wales the State
didn’t audit—it only interfered. It saved
the surviving banks in ’93 by coming to their
assistance without asking questions about
their standing. That proceeding was cor-
dially admired ; private enterprise mostly
rushes to the State for aid in bad times. It
highly approves of State interference so long
as the interference consists of assistance
with no questions asked. In Victoria the
State didn’t interfere, and for that reason
only one Victorian bank escaped suspension.
And the Victorian capitalist, though he ob-
jects to State interference as much as any •
other capitalist, has never ceased cursing to
this day because the State didn’t interfere. If
the Australian life-assurance offices strike a
serious disaster some day, they will also, it
is quite probable, come to the State for aid
with no questions asked. A rigid system of
State inspection now might avert that catas-
trophe. It would almost certainly make
these institutions put more energy into the
work of writing off old losses, and it might
cause them to make solid provision in ad-
vance for future losses. If it achieved either
of these results it would have a good work
to its credit.
The Federal Labor Party and the Tariff.
The Labor party having considered the Barton
tariff in detail has decided to call for its reduc-
tion in committee by something like a million
sterling. George Turner, the most careful and
grubbing Treasurer who ever blessed any Austra-
lian State, calculates that the Commonwealth
requires to raise £8,900,000 a year for its
own and the States’ uses. The Labor
party calculates that £7,900,000 will be enough.
The Bulletin would like to believe that the
Labor party is correct, for paying taxes is not
a pleasant thing ; but, judging by the public
performances of the two financial authorities
—Turner and the Labor party—this paper
is inclined to back the former, and to
forecast that any such severe trimming of the
Federal income as is proposed would only result
in a further resort to the dishonest tricks of public
finance. The system of charging current expendi-
ture to loan accounts has already brought the
finances of the States to a parlous condition, and
threatens to lead to absolute disaster. So, whilst
it would be a joyful circumstance to be able to dis-
pense with £1,000,000 of taxes —since, even with
that million, all the States will continue to resort
to the London pawnshop, and the Federa-
tion will go to the same disreputable place to get
money to put clock-towers on post-offices IHE
Bulletin would be more inclined to welcome a
proposal to gather in yet another £1,000,000 or
so a year, and thus start Australia on a serious
attempt to pay its way. Paying one’s way is
travelling on a rugged and thorny path, but the
journey will have to be made some day, and the
younger our feet the less painful the blisters.
* * * *
One notable fact is that the Labor party’s list
of suggested reductions touches practically no
Protective duty. It is the revenue part, the Free-
trade part of the tariff, to which exception is
chiefly taken. The duties on tea, cocoa, mustard,
kerosene, infants’ food, undressed timber, water-
proof cloth, flannelette and other cotton goods the
party proposes to abolish; thetax on rice to decrease.
The only Protective duties which it is proposed to
interfere with are those on condensed milk (re-
duction from Hd. to Id. per lb.), galvanised iron
(abolition), and ammunition (abolition). It is
satisfactory to note that the Australian Labor
party, as a party, unanimously endorses nearly all
the Protective portion of the Barton National
policy, and finds ground for objection only to the
Freetrade revenue duties. This objection The
Bulletin would, in the main, cordially endorse if
the financial position of Australia were different.
But when the nonest meetingof current obligations
is practically impossible with a Customs and Excise
revenue of £8,900,000, there can be no other result
of reducing that sum by £1,000,000 than an in-
crease of national dishonesty. On some details,
too, the suggestions of the Labor party are open to
criticism. Experience has shown that a tea duty
does not interfere with the cheap brealcfast-table,
whilst it has a value outside of its revenue-raising
properties in keeping rubbish out of the country.
When G. H. Reid canvassed for the gratitude of
the N.S.W. people by repealing the I)ibbs duty
on tea, householders went on paying the usual
price of one shilling, or one shilling and sixpence,
or two shillings a pound for their blend, and
were not aware of getting any better value
for the money than before. The money pre-
viously paid in duty did not go to the
consumer, but was partly pocketed by the
importer, partly distributed among newspapers,
hoarding fiends, and house-to-house tea canvassers,
in the effort to push the consumption of stuff
worth fourpence a lb. as an extra choice eighteen-
penny blend. In proposing to remove the tax
from the murderous flannelette the Labor party
makes another mistake. That shoddy, inflam-
mable material causes scores of deaths from
burning of the children of the poor yeai'ly, and if
it were to be kept out of the country by a 1000
per cent, ad valorem impost, the tax would be a
blessing and not a burden.
* » * *
On the broad principle of having less by
£1,000,000 of the Freetrade brand of taxation,
The B. is, however, cordially with the Labor
party, if it can be done honestly, and would just
as enthusiastically endorse a call for £5,000,000
less—with the same reservation. Unfortunately,
the reservation is a fatal one.
It will be interesting to see if Mr. G. H. Reid
has the unutterable gall to support the Labor
party in this attack on his pet duties. A Reid
tariff would certainly have agreed with the
Barton tariff on the points singled out by
the Labor party ; in fact, Sydney D.T., which
professes to be the organ of Reid, and
is certainly the organ of the importers, called
for even higher revenue taxes than those proposed
by Barton. If Mr. Reid now rallies to the
standard of Mr. Watson and assists his efforts to
do away with just the sort of duties that Mr.
Reid has lately been crying out for, it will show
that the only principle in his political pro-
gramme is office-seeking, and a low-down
desire to embarrass the Government at any cost.
An honest attitude on the part of the leader of
the Opposition on this point would do something
to rehabilitate him in the eyes of Australia ; if he
follows at the Labor party’s tail in an effort to
destroy the very part of the tariff that he himself
favors, it will never be possible to regard him in
any other light than that of a humorous person
with no political moral sense, and no guiding
principle except a greed for office.
Sweater and Co.
“ Manufacturers of clothing, poverty, and im-
morality,” is the sign that should be displayed by
some of the Sydney ‘ ‘ slop ” firms. Hitherto, the
wages paid in their factories have been fixed in
the face of unrestricted competition with the
world, and this has brought the trade in Sydney
down to such a point that only women who had
husbands working, who had homes with relatives,
or who had “ other sources of income” could really
live. But with the passage of the Federal tariff,
and the consequent barring out of European cheap
labor competition, the women have made an effort
to improve their position by means of organisation.
The need for it was very great. For making
trousers, women used to be paid by some manu-
facturers fivepence per pair—some were only
paid fourpence—for which sum the trousers had
to be seamed down the sides, round the bottoms,
sewn round the body, fitted with pockets and
buttons. Vests were paid for at the rate of 6d.
for men’s, sd. for youths’, and for boys’, the
workers finding all the silk and cotton thread;
for Govt, vests they received 7d. Coat machin-
ists were paid anything from 2s. 6d. to £1 per
week, but the average was far below 10s. per week.
In one factory writer took the wages of the eight
best-paid women, and the total was £6 13s. 6d.,
or an average of 16s. each for 48 hours ! This
is the wage of people who have served their time,
not averaged with the swarm of apprentices
working for 2s. 6d. per week. As to the Victorian
rates, the lowest paid for making trousers in that
State is Is. per pair, under the Minimum Wage
law. It is in an effort to raise Sydney rates
towards the level of those of Melbourne that the
tailoresses on strike are now engaged.
* * * *
The demands of the strikers are exceedingly
moderate: for trousers, eigbtpenee per pair for
plain seams and ninepence for raised seams; for
men’s vests eightpence; minimum wage for
machinists 30s. per week. These are the terms
Sweater and Co. are fighting, and in the interests
of the whole community Sweater and Co. must be
smashed. The worst sweaters are not manufactu-
rers in the proper sense of the terra. They are
mere middlemen parasites. The warehouses im-
port the cloth, and the so-called manufacturers fit
up a room with a few machines and tender for the
work of making the cloth up into garments for the
warehouses. Their only raw material is woman’s
labor. They compete with one another, and
the warehouses pit them against each other,
and the more they can beat down wages the
greater the margin of profit. The warehouse-
men calculate to make one profit on the cost of
selling the raw material, another profit on the
cost of making it up; in addition they have to
pay travellers, advertising and other expenses,
and pass the total sum on to the retailer, who in
turn has to get his 30 or 40 per cent, profit. The


firms which imported the cloth and made it up
themselves, and sold it direct to the retailers or
the public, settled the difficulty right away, as far
as they were concerned. It was the middle-man
parasite who stood out. He says he cannot afford
to pay the increased rates, as the aristocratic
warehouseman has got him sweated right down to
the last cent. The middle-man does indeed look
as though someone had got him down. What he
says may be true. He may not be able to
pay the higher wages and live, and if so,
the sooner he is commercially dead the better. At
present he seems to be able to live only by grind-
ing down the worker, and taking from the “big
man” the reproach of sweating.
* * * *
It was not a strike that was wanted. It was a
case to go before an Arbitration Court, which
could have found out if the real sweater was the
poor-looking middle-man manufacturer, or the
eminently respectable warehouseman, who sits in
the legislature and drives to church on Sundays.
As the strike has occurred, the only thing is to
level up the wages—even if it sweeps the middle-
man right out the business. He has no right to
live if he cannot live honestly and without killing
others. For the future the matter is largely
mixed up with the tariff. The big warehouses
who import the cloth would just as soon
import the made-up article. The duty on
made-up clothing is 25 per cent. Add to that
about 8 per cent, for landing charges, and you get
a total duty of 33 per cent. On raw material,
woollens, and worsteds, the duty is 20 per cent.,
and the landing charges are from 5 to 1\ per cent.,
which gives the local manufacturer only from 5 to
74 per cent, protection. In addition to this he
has outside advantages which probably increase
his actual protection to about 12 per cent, at the
outside. These advantages are those of distance,
freshness of design and cut, sorting up
of sizes, and ability to complete small
orders in full range of sizes. The English
or foreign manufacturer has always to make
goods for the Australian market about a
season in advance. Australia is a long way off, and
fashions change suddenly as to width of trousers
and cut of vest. Small men cannot send for a
dozen of assorted suits half-way round the world,
but they can send to the local manufacturer.
Against all these advantages is the superior organi-
sation of the European factories. They work longer
hours, with better machinery and lower wages, and
on many classes of goods they can pay all our cus-
toms duties and still undersell the local product. If
Australia is to have its store-clothing hands paid
a decent living wage, the tariff on manufactured
rags must be increased to at least 35 or 45 per
cent. Local competition will keep the price
down ; so, while the higher tariff won’t increase the
price of clothes, it will set a standard below which
the cheap and nasty foreign exporter cannot cut
rates by his cheaper labor. Then, with a Mini-
mum Wage law and an Industrial Arbitration
Court, the sweater can be kent out of the trade.
The Chinese Egg.
The Bulletin still awaits with anxiety the pro-
test of the Australian Freetrade party against
the protection of the Australian hen proposed by
the Barton tariff. The Customs tax of sixpence
per dozen on imported eggs, now liable to be col-
lected, amounts to 300 per cent, on the Chinese
egg which lias lately been arriving at our ports,
and will prove practically prohibitive. The bless-
ings of the Hong Kong hen’s cheap labor product
will thus be. entirely lost to the Australian citizen.
The issue is such an important one, from the
Cobden point of view, that George Reid, Sydney
Smith and Joseph Cook should for the while
abandon their sorrowful contemplation of the
washerwoman’s taxed starch and rally to the de-
fence of the cheap Chinese egg.
• * * *
The egg, it is perhaps hardly necessary to re-
mind Mr. Reid, is a staple article of food alike
for poor and rich. It graces every breakfast-table,
enters into the composition of the Wolloomooloo
baby’s milk-pudding and the Toorak magnate’s
lobster mayonnaise. From Kalgoorlie to Cook-
town its use is universal. The miner, the
farmer, the laborer, the struggling city clerk,
the sweated laundress, all trust for nutri-
ment largely to the egg. A far-seeing Providence
has seemed to design it, not so much for the per-
petuation of the fowl species as for the provision
of an easily-portable, naturally-canned so to
speak, and quickly-cooked article of human food.
A falling price - barometer in the egg market
indicates happiness and peace for mankind ; a rise
in value pinches every household. For many
years the Australian hen has coped with the
Australian demand for eggs, providing the “laid
to-dav,” the “new-laid,” and the “quite fresh”
in quantities apparently sufficient for all demands.
But, with the beginning of the new century, there
dawned on N.S.W. the blessing of the Chinese
egg.
* * * •
The reason of the extraordinary cheapness of the
Chinese egg, the researches of political economists
have so far failed to reveal, though it is probably ex-
plainableon the ground that the Chinese hen thrives
on a diet of fish-offal, whilst the Australian hen will
not work without the stimulus of a grass run and
two feeds a day of grain food. At Hong kong
eggs cost one penny per dozen packed: they can
easily be landed in Sydney at threepence
dozen and retailed for fivepence, at a big profit.
The Australian hen refuses to work under any
conditions which will enable her product to be
sold at less than sixpence a dozen, even in its
“ quite fresh ” and intensely doubtful state.
* * * *
Now, it must be obvious to the meanest free-
trade intellect that a tariff which proposes to
protect the Australian hen at the expense of the
bone and sinew of the heavily-burdened washer-
woman of the community, is a relic of barbarism,
a prop of monopoly, an attempt on the part of
bloated privilege to suck the blood of the poor.
What claim has that bird, or the mercenary mono-
polists who seek to pile up fortunes out of its
labors, on the goodwill of the Australian com-
munity, that we should be taxed heavily to main-
tain for her easy conditions of work and
plentiful supply of food ? Why should she not
be prepared to compete in the open markets of
the world, and live on fish offal too ? Her industry
is surely long enough established now. Why
should it require more coddling ; still weakly seek
shelter from the State? If the Chinese hen can
lay eggs in China which can be laid down in
Sydney at threepence per dozen, is it not a posi-
tive advantage to every section of the community
in giving them a cheap food-supply ? And why
should the Australian hen object ? Far better
for the taxpayers to agree to pension every Aus-
tralian hen and allow it to live in opulent and
eggless ease than to submit to this burden of
sixpence per dozen on Chinese eggs.
* * * »
These are the arguments on the subject which
will readily suggest themselves to every Free-
trader. They are precisely similar to the argu-
ments used on the question of boots, and woollen
clothing, and furniture, and grain, and a dozen
other things which the Bakton tariff proposes to
tax for the protection of Australian industry.
The Bulletin awaits anxiously, then, their
flourishing forth in the loud squeakof G-. H. Reid —
awaits anxiously, but to an extent, hopelessly, for
the Cobdenites seem to wish to avoid this egg-
question as the politician seeks to avoid the
feculent egg of the platform. Yet, consistency
demands of the great Freetrade party some firm
utterance on the subject, else it will incur the
ignominy of quietly consenting to give to the
Australian lien the protection which it wishes to
take away from the Australian working-man.
The defence of the Chinese egg against its local
enemies is the duty of pressing moment for the
Foreign-1 raders.
The Child-Blood-Griutiness of New
South Wales.
It is well for the average man’s peace of mind
that statistics are dry reading, otherwise the
terrific waste of infant life in Australia would
darken his days and haunt his nights. Listen to
the death wail of the children echoing through
Coghlan’s statistics. In N.S.W. alone, between
the years 1895 and 1900, inclusive, N.S.W. lost by
death 38,258 children under the age of 15 years.
During the same period 15,140 babies were born
out of wedlock, of whom 4212 perished in their
first year ; 373 reached their first birthday only to
die; and the death total was swelled by 289
others, born before 1895. Altogether, during the
six years, 4874 illegitimate children died before
their 15th year.
-* • * *
Probably numbers of these were wanted to live,
but were slain by the ignorance and carelessness
of their mothers. The dirty feeding-bottle, with
the rubber tube poison-bed ; and the infected sink
that cries aloud in vain for carbolic acid, accounted
for some of the little illegitimates without the
consent of their mothers ; but the vast majority
were, no doubt, simply treated as undesirable
planetary immigrants and shoved over the bourn
as quickly as possible by neglect, brutality, and
straight-out murder. There can be no question of
this : the way the illegitimate dies off compared
with the wedlock child proves it. This table is
supplied by statistician Coghlan, and shows the
comparative death-rates of legitimate and illegiti-
mate children for the years mentioned
DEATHS'per THOUSAND OP LEGITIMATE AND ILLEGITIMATE
CHILDREN UNDER 15 EACH YEAR.
1895. 1896. 1897. 1893. 1899. 1900.
Legitimate .. 106 109 91 109 106 89
Illegitimate .. 245 294 260 297 286 288
Excess of illegi-
timate deaths
per 1000 .. 139 185 169 188 180 199
For 1901, as far as the figures are available, the
ratio stands :
DEATHS PER THOUSAND OF EACH CLASS LIVING.
Under one year—Legitimate, 90 - 3 ; illegitimate, 305 - 7
Under five years— „ 27‘8 ; „ 91'5
Which means that the poor little illegitimates died
off about 34 times as fast as those born in wedlock,
and a large proportion of them died in their first
year, when they were utterly helpless, when their
only protest was a wail, and the fiendish baby-
farmer could murder them with comparative
safety.
* * • *
N.S.W. has a Children’s Protection Act, and a
Children’s Protection Department to administer
it. The Act was largely the outcome of the ex-
posure of the wholesale Marin murders in 1892,
but it is crippled by its undue respect for parental
rights, whereas any Act for the proper protection
of children must lay it down as a fundamental law
that all parental rights are based upon the faithful
performance of parental duties. N.S.W. Act
makes it necessary for the Children’s Protection
Department to prove the parent or guardian
guilty of some specific action, which, in the sworn
opinion of a medical man, “has resulted, or
appears likely to result in bodily suffering or
permanent or serious injury to the health of the
child,” before it can take charge of it. What is
needed is an absolutely new Act, drawn on the
lines of the recent English legislation on the sub-
ject, giving the department power to move on the
grounds of general neglect and insufficient care,
and leave it to the court to say whether the neg-
lect justifies the adoption of the child by the State.
* * * •
To provide proper protection for the illegiti-
mate, it is absolutely necessary that the powers
of the Children’s Protection Department be
largely extended. It must have machinery by
which it can have some chance to get to know of the
existence of the illegitimate infant within hours
of its birth. At present, it never hears of him till
60 days afterwards, if it does hear of him at
all ; and by that time the little waif, if not already
dead, is doomed. Every charitable or other
lying-in hospital or home should be compelled to
notify the department within 24 hours of any
birth. Every doctor, or nurse, or other person
acting in his place (the householder being respon-
sible for the carrying out of the law) should be
required to notify the nearest officer of the
C.P.D., or, where there is no officer, the nearest
stipendiary magistrate, of the birth of any child.
If it was described as an illegitimate, immediately
the department received the news it could take
steps to notify the mother in a tactful and discreet
manner that it is prepared, if she were unable to do
so, to find a home for her infant, without exposing
“ her shame” in any way. If this were done the
helpless, deserted mother would turn instinctively
to the department for succour and assistance;
whereas now she turns to the “kind, motherly
person ” who will adopt a pretty baby for a pre-
mium of £5. Mostly the poor girl has to go to
work to earn the £5, and the baby is dead before
it is paid.
• • * *
A searching investigation into the cause of
death should be made in the cases of all boarded-
out children, and a record kept of the people with
whom boarded-out illegitimates die, so that the
Department could get on the track of all people
whose record would cause suspicions of trafficking
in infantile death.
* » • *
To properly cope with the problem, the Depart-
ment should have thoroughly-equipped maternity
hospitals and foundling homes. The mother could
be admitted to the maternity hospital till “her
trouble” was over, when she could be drafted on
to the foundling home to nourish her child for
about twelve weeks or so while it was being inured
to artificial food. Private foundling-homes, such
as that at Waitara, near Sydney, run by R. C.
nuns, can do this; so it should not be beyond
the power or the purse of the State. The
present lack of such institutions absolutely
drives the unmarried mother to the abortionist
and the baby-farmer. N.S.W. has, in the
past, gaoled individuals following these occu-
pations, and when gaol failed to suppress
them, it has tried hanging them; but,
judging by the numerous trials for illegal
practice, that, too, has failed to suppress
the abortionist, and the previously - given
statistics prove that the baby-murderer is
rampant in the land. If State maternity hospi-
tals and foundling asylums were in existence,
many girls would sootier go through the ordeal of
maternity than risk their lives at the hands of the
criminal quack ; and, if it were generally known
that the State would assist, without trouble and
exposure, unmarried mothers to find homes for
their babes, a large number of valuable lives would
be saved, both adult and infant; the abortionist
and baby-farmer would wilt away for sheer lack
of business, and some of the blood-guiltiness of
N.S.W. would be wiped out.
* ♦ » »
N.S.W. State Attorney-General Wise will in-
troduce a Bill into the local Parliament this session
to give effect to some of the ideas aforementioned.
It is a Bill that will deal somewhat forcibly with
parental rights as they are generally understood ;
but this paper doubts if, even in N.S.W. Upper
House, anyone will be found stupid enough to
champion the cause of the baby-murderer and the
abortionist.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Consolidating the Empire.
This telegram to the Natal Mercury of Sept.
20 shows how the work of “consolidating the
Empire ” is being carried out : —•
Oradock, Sept. 12.—This morning nine girls, aged be-
tween 15 and 20, and one married woman were charged
before Mr. Harrison with harboring the King’s enemies
and supplying food, etc., on the occasion of the Boers
entering into Maraisburg. Two were discharged and the
remainder sentenced to 30 days’ imprisonment.
A fuller report in Cape Argus of Sept. 18
shows that the district was under martial law,
and the military prosecutor sought to prove, among
other things, that the accused girls had actually
kissed some of the Boer raiders. It was suggested
for the defence that some of the girls might have
kissed their relatives in the commando, but this
the court considered a treasonable practice,
tending to make rebellion more popular. Cases
of a similarly trivial nature are filling the Cape
“ Loyalist ” papers, and probably help to explain
why the Boers commandos appear pretty vigorous
in spite of all the reported killings and captures.
The imprisoned girls were (as might be expected)
being supplied with food and comforts by their
relatives and sympathisers ; and no doubt, now
that they are out of gaol, their kisses will be at
a premium—in Afrikander circles anyhow. The
determination and bitterness of the Afrikander
women bodes ill for the “consolidation of the
Empire,” and this many of the more rational men
on active service are not slow to recognise. An
officer of the last Bushmen’s Corps which went
from Sydney writes to a friend: —
God send that when Australia’s hour of trial comes, our
women may be as faithful to their country as are the
Boer women.
And these same women will be the mothers of the
future Afrikanders. When the Empire called the
Soldiers of the Queen, Sons of the Sea, and
so on, few of them bargained on being used as
gaolers for women who had committed the crime of
kissing their lovers in the war. If the Germans,
Russians, or Japanese were to come along here
and imprison the girls who kissed our kharki-clad
heroes who went out to fight against them, the
pacification of Australia would probably be quite
as big a contract as the pacification of South
Africa.
♦
The Bishop and the Black Man.
The Bishop of Carpentaria, pastor of the sheep of
Northern Queensland, has, after a survey of the
remote corners of his fold, come back to civilisa-
tion with views on the colored-labor question.
A perambulating bishop in the back-blocks never
by any chance “puts up ” at the shearers’ camp,
or the boundary-rider’s hut, but usually trusts to
the hospitality of the squatter and the branch
bank-manager. His views when he returns reflect
those of the company that he has kept. This par-
ticular bishop, to quote from the Australian
Churchman
thinks that it will be impossible to keep the Chinese
from spreading in some parts of Australia. They are
large mine-owners and employers of labor, and have
settled in large numbers in the Bishop’s diocese. The
blacks, too, are very numerous, and are a fine
race, and the Japanese also are very energetic
and populous. The Bishop thinks that the solu-
tion of the color difficulty, which must always be
felt in Northern Australia, is to Christianise these races.
They cannot be excluded. They must be reckoned with
as a permanent part of our inheritance—a part of the
white man’s burden. In the Divine Providence it may be
our work to elevate them morally ; to teach them what
true citizenship is, and to bring them into the grace and
purity of the religion of Christ.
The prayer of the cheap-labor-seeking employer
to be left in peace with his niggers never seems
quite so nauseous as when it is voiced by a parson
and urged in the name of the Almighty. The
black races can be excluded : if the Barton Immi-
gration Act is passed and honestly administered
they will be excluded, absolutely and for ever.
To Christianise these races would not in the
slightest degree affect their intrinsic objection-
ableness. No sensible person now has any grudge
against them because they do not believe in dog-
matic Christianity; at least three-fourths of the
white inhabitants of Australia at heart share that
lack of belief; and the Chinese and Hindu has
very often a religion of his own which, to
him, is a far better rule of life than any which
Christian sects could substitute. The objection
to colored immigrants is racial and economic, an
objection founded on the fact that we cannot eat
with them, work with them, marry with them,
without the certainty of national deterioration and
degradation. If, as the Bishop says, Divine Pro-
vidence may have the end in view of getting
Australia to “elevate” the colored races, it is
curious that in the mind of every healthy white
Australian Providence or something has planted
an ineradicable repugnance to associating with
these racial inferiors, a conscience which warns us
against intermarrying with them. And that
conscience is as strong in the Bishop as in any of
us. He would not take to wife a Chinese or
Hindu woman, nor would he give a daughter in
marriage to a colored man ; nor would any other
of those who utter cant talk about brotherhood—
not brother-in-law-hood—with the black man.
+
The Exclusion of Aliens.
“ Viking ” offers a few remarks :
The question seems to be reduced to two considera-
tions : (Ist) Whether we will provoke a serious conflict
with Britain or Japan if w'e exclude the Japs, and (2nd)
whether the exclusion of Kanakas will destroy the N.
Queensland sugar industry. Even if the effect of the Bill
will be the closing of the mills—a remote contingency—
that is not. a conclusive reason for permitting the Kanaka
to be retained. If the evils arising from a low-grade race
establishing itself in our midst are' serious enoug'h to
make it worth our while to avoid them by sacrificing
portion of the sugar industry, bv all means let that
portion go.
W. used to make our roads very cheaply by convict
labor, but when the injury to workmen was considered,
and the degrading effects the sight of the chain-gang had
on the public were appreciated, we cheerfully sacrificed
cheapness for the public good. So with the sugar busi-
ness—if the need arises.
Let us get away from side issues and consider the prin-
ciple of alien exclusion with reference to race preserva-
tion and Australian well-being. We may placate our
religious friends by assuming that the colored man has
the same rights as the white. It does not follow that the
coolie has any right to acquire a domicile here. No man
can be said to have a right to do that. If he is allowed
to enter he is being granted a privilege— not accorded a
right. Does not every landholder practise the principle
of exclusion when he paints, “ trespassers will be prose-
cuted” on his fence? Perhaps Premier Piiilp has been
guilty of that heinous offence. Are not his friendly
visitors exercising a privilege when they tread on our turf ?
Who has a right to traverse another man’s land at will?
So with Australia. We reserve the right to say who shall
come as friends, and who shall not come at all.
It is contended by Mr. Arthur in United Australia
that so long as Kanakas are kept at field work there need
be little fear that they will contaminate the white race
by intermarriage. The logic is hard to follow. The half-
caste element is not largely the result of mixed marriages.
This unsavoury aspect of the matter needs very careful
handling so that, while it may receive due consideration,
its public discussion may not become offensive. 001.
Reay recently touched on the matter when he said that
the proportion of Kanaka women to men in Queensland is
7 per 100. The men are not ascetics, and the large influx
of Jap. women is very significant. Pause here and con-
sider various probabilities ; and the fact that Jap brothels
are maintained in Kanka Cairns to minimise risk to white
women.
In his late letter to Mr. Barton, Consul Eitaki laid
stress on the small number of Japs, that come to N.S.W.
and Victoria, but he did not quote figures for Thursday
Island and Queensland. That would have put a different
complexion on his argument, particularly if he gave
statistics as to their occupations. The threat implied in
his remark that “corresponding rights belong to Japan ”
is not of much concern to us.
Unfortunately, the surplus population of all foreign
States is largely of an undesirable character, and it is that
class which naturally seeks an outlet for its perverted
energy in new lands. It is the interest of the foreign
State to get rid of them ; it is our interest to see that
they don’t come here.
Let us grant that the Jap. is above all colored men in
the social scale. We do not want him, even at that esti-
mate of his value. We require our immigrants to
become Australians, to live here, to intermarry with our
people, to help us to build up a sturdy white race to
develope and defend Australia. No colored race can
comply with these requirements. Europeans can, and
do, become Australians by adoption, arid we welcome
them. The colored man is impossible. Who is there,
however great may be his admiration for Jap energy,
courage and progress, that would pleasurably regard the
prospect of the most enlightened Jap forming a matri-
monial alliance with one of his own family ? That is the
white personal view of the matter, and it is clearly the
white national view.
If it is not to our advantage to blend our race with the
black, brown, and yellow ; if such an idea is repugnant
to our feelings, then it is evident that the colored ele-
ments, if admitted, must form themselves into racial
colonies. In this respect, it is instructive to think of the
Chinese quarters in ’Frisco.
They would be separated from us and from each other
by their different nationalities, modes of thought, lan-
guage, and widely different creeds and social and moral
codes. Then we would be a divided people, and, in addi-
tion to our present political differences, we would be
burdened with bitter racial feud, unrest, and social con-
flict. Prevention is better than such a disease.

The Chinese Cheap-Tripper.
“ H.8.T.” writes :
Premier Barton was recently deputationised by repre-
sentatives of the shipping firms mainly instrumental in
flooding Australia with yellow and brown agony. The
stringent terms of the Alien Restriction Bill have kind
of struck their principals all of a heap. Hence the depu-
tation. Little wonder that these firms are up in arms
against the new alien legislation. Years ago they reaped
huge profits for their shareholders (most of whom are
non-resident in Australia), by carting down the Chow in
his hundreds every voyage, and dumping him in large
consignments at various points along the Australian
coast, from Port Darwin in the north to Adelaide in the
south. The fares charged to the incoming Chow are not
based on what might be termed a moderate scale, 250
Mexican dollars being a common figure. This goes to the
contracting “ compradore,” who makes all arrangements
for the would-be emigrant. It includes the provision of a
permit—a naturalisation paper purchased from a returned
emigrant who has no further use for the same. A selection
of these documents, with photos, attached, is always
available at several compradores’ offices in Hongkong, and
the heap is searched until the one most likely to fit and
correspond with the intending emigrant is selected.
Armed with this he presents himself on board, and is
carried to his required port of destination. En route he
undergoes a sort of coaching from the older hands in the
topography of his desired haven, hence the ludicrous
answers occasionally given to examining customs officials.
Should he be bowled out as an impostor, as occasionally
happens, the expense of his return fare is charge-
able to the compradore who ships him. The menu
furnished for Chinese passengers isn’t extensive.
Rice is provided three times daily, with the
addition of a thin soup at midday, and either boiled
beef or dried fish. They give but little trouble to those
who have the handling of the ship, and growling on the
score of food is seldom or never heard. They mess in
gangs or cliques, according to the port they ship from,
and the doings of one clique have little or no interest for
the rest. Midway between Brisbane and Sydney a Chow
passenger once reported to the quarter-master'that one
of their number was missing. Inquiries revealed the fact
that several other Chinamen had seen the man jump
overboard, but, considering that it was a case of “no
b’long my pidgin,” omitted to report the fact. The same
thing happened on the same voyage, about a week later.
The Chow is an ideal passenger, who pays liberally, eats
cheaply, and falls overboard with less fuss than any other
passenger on this globe. No wonder the companies will
miss him.
There is to be no tram-strike in Sydney, after all,
Parliament having helped the Commissioners to
climb down with some grace. The running staffs
of both railways and trams are to have an 8-hours
day in eight months’ time. As they climb down,
the Commissioners growl that the reform will
cost over £50,000 per annum, and will throw an
appalling burden on the man-on-the-land in par-
ticular. What would have happened had the
Commissioners been asked to extend the principle
to the clerical staff as well, is too dreadful to think
about. The same sort of cry went up in Maori-
land when the Commissioners there were asked
to agree to a maximum of 48 and (50 hours
per week for continuous and intermittent work.
The concession was granted, under threat of a
general strike, but, as they climbed down, the
awful Three growled that it meant the stoppage
of all interest payments, the increase of all rates
and fares, and the general ruin of the country.
But nothing dreadful happened. All rates and
fares have since been further reduced, and the inter-
mittent labor has been lowered from 60 to 54 hours
per week. The head office had, however, to expend
some brain-sweat in arranging new time-tables and
services to make them fit in with the new con-
ditions. They could have done it before, but till
they were driven to it by the financial exigencies
of the new condition they never tried.
