Prince’s Bridge).. Fitzgerald Bros.’ Circus.
Exhibition Buildino.. . The World’s Fair (last week).
In continuance of his Mission to portray a
Great Moral Purpose, and incidentally, various less
moral women, Rev. Wilson Barrett staged at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney, on Saturday, anotherof his plays
“Man and his Makers.” The characters offer
the usual Barrett mixture of cant and concupis-
cence, but there is much more cant than usual, and
a good deal less concupiscence. Indeed, the piece
has occasional flashes of genuine merit; the first
act is more than promising, the third act really
strong—but the influence of the actor manager
wishing to preach a spicy sermon before a vast
suburban congregation must assert itself. So the
second act is mainly Wilson Barrett writing anew
scripture in poem form (copies distributed free by
colporteurs at Her Majesty’s door to the just and
the unjust alike every evening); the fourth act
Wilson Barrett preaching to a judge and jury ;
the fifth act Wilson Barrett preaching to a fallen
woman. Mr. Barrett has set himself up as the
dramatist of the evangelically religious
person with a clean-shaven upper lip, and an
itch for unctuously discussing sex-sins. In
“The Sign of the Cross” robust religiosity was
“relieved” by the portrayal of much heathen
vice, and by much highly-exciting pursuit of a
Christian maiden by a Pagan in short kilts. In
“The Christian King,” there is a vision of the
Master’s Son, a religious ruler of many moral
maxims, and much detailed effort of a Pagan lady
to give or to barter her “body.” In “Man and
His Makers ” the theme is that the Almighty is
not scientific, but evangelical in His methods, and
that salvation is to be got by reading a Wilson
Barrett poem: the inevitable “relief” is pro-
vided by a lady who has been addicted to the ways
of vice, and is converted by Mr. Barrett and his
poem. That plays of this character should suc-
ceed, in spite of turgid treatment and inept
characterisation, is not at all wonderful, for they
appeal alike to the school of Sardou and the school
of General Booth.
* * * »
There are dramatic possibilities in “ Man and
His Makers,” and to a small extent they have been
taken advantage of. A successful bar-
rister is refused the hand of his girl
lover by her scientific father because
of a taint in his blood : his grand-
father had been a six-bottle man,
his father had died of a drug-habit.
The barrister, whose ‘ ‘ foredoom ” is
thus announced, does, in despair,
take to drug-eating—opium, seem-
ingly. But he saves himself and
wins his girl. There is the skeleton
of a fine play. But the idea is
not allowed to grow naturally : all
the flesh has to be put on W. Bar-
rett, and quite extraneous fallen-
woman interest has to be dragged
in. So the drama drivels through
extraordinarily unreal developments,
all apparently designed with the
one object of giving the eminent
author-actor-manager the middle of
the stage, a pious text, and a fallen
woman to moralise over. The Court
scene, for weird improbability, could
not be surpassed many rnelo-drarna.
The thoroughly unscientific treat-
ment of the drug-habit is a minor
fault, since neither the dramatist nor
the general public is expected to be
familiar with the symptoms of mor-
phimania or any of the drug insani-
ties ; but still it is a fault, and Mr.
Barrett, by consulting a physician or
two, might have avoided the mistake
of giving a fortnight of recovery-
horrors to a man who had been eat-
ing opium only a very few weeks.
* * * •
The acting ot “ Man and His
Makers” is pitched in the high melo-
dramatic key. No other course is
possible, since the play is mostly
fierce, turgid declamation. Mr. Bar-
rett is generally successful in his
long, impassioned speeches. In the
second act he is notably good ; but
the quieter exhortations of the first
and last acts occasionally border
perilously close on the ridiculous—an effect partly
of the actor’s extraordinarily juvenile dressing.
Mr. George Barrett plays a real human part with
quiet effectiveness. Miss Lillah M’Carthy occa-
sionally overacts the courtesan, but generally
gives a tragic and impressive picture of the only
other live character in the play. Miss Edyth
Latimer and Miss Daisy Belmore act charmingly
in somewhat colorless parts. Certainly, Mr.
Barrett is to be thanked for bringing out this bevy
of beautiful women. “Man and His Makers”
seemed, on Saturday, to have hit the mark; the
stalls, filled from Suburbia, and the gallery were
roused to something like enthusiasm. “ The Sign
of the Cross ” is to be revived to-night (Saturday).
** # *
Fra Diavolo, at Sydney Criterion last
week, proved a sprightly and pleasant perform-
ance, giving the Josephine Stanton Co. a good
opportunity of showing its value. Auber’s
romantic opera has been so seldom produced of
late in Australia that, except to the oldest of
theatre-goers, it is an unknown work. A public
on which is inflicted so much “Morocco
Bound,” “Casino Girl,” and “Said Pasha”
stuff, might with advantage be allowed a
little Auber more frequently. Diavolo is an
opera hesitating between the grand and the
comic—with a well-defined plot, plentifully be-
sprinkled with picturesque banditti, English
aristocrats of much wealth but little manners, and
graceful village maidens. Likewise the fair
heroine in one scene undresses herself (to a par-
ticularly discreet and modest degree), with charm-
ing incidental music, and pops into bed with far
more clothing on than a man could wear there
without giving rise to grave reflections on his
sobriety. Further, the bandit hero is accus-
tomed to disguise himself nobly as a mar-
quis, and when caught banditting around the
bedrooms of an inn confesses to a little
appointment with a lady, and, so as not to un-
fairly favor or prejudice anyone, with all the
ladies on the premises. Withal there is nothing
suggestive or objectionable in the English version
of the opera, whilst most of the humorous diablerie
of the original is preserved. The Stanton troupe
of Italian bandits was evidently recruited from
Wild West cowboys, who brought their hair and
their accent but they make funny
enough villains nevertheless. The chief burden
of the production was borne by Miss Stanton,
whose voice was the only one fully equal to the
requirements of the opera, but Mr. Hallain gave
some support with a pleasant, if somewhat faded,
tenor ; the orchestra was efficient, and the come-
dians bright and resourceful. Altogether “ Fra
Diavolo ” was a very pleasant production.
*•■ • •
Why it should have been replaced by
“ Wang ” on Saturday night it is difficult to under-
stand. “ Wang” is a piece of the “Said Pasha”
type—certainly a trifle better in plot and brighter
in music, but still an extravaganza rather than
an opera. Except for the opportunity that
“Wang” gives to prove that Miss Stanton
can very satisfactorily fill a midshipman’s
suit, there seems no valid reason why it should
have been allowed to rush a pleasant collection of
bandits off the Criterion stage. The story is of a
pursuit of some hidden treasure by Mataya, Crown
Prince of Siam (Miss Stanton), a white, plump,
and golden-haired lad in ducks who upsets most
popular notions as to the characteristics of Crown
princes in Siam. But perhaps they grow white
princes as well as white elephants in that province
of Asia. Some pretty songs are in “Wang”—
“Another Fellow,” “The Man in the Moon,”
and “ Playing Babies.” A large elephant is very
ably acted by some member or members of the co.
who are not on the bill. The piece was favorably
received on Saturday, but had not so cordial a
DR. KELLY, R.C. Coadjutor-Archbishop of Sydney.
welcome as Diavolo. If the management is wise
it will restore that devilled item to the bill of fare.

A mixture of melodrama and variety still
continues to be popular at Sydney Empire. On
Saturday a new dramatic co. appeared in the
“Octoroon,” which provided more sensation to
the minute than any play previously produced.
** * *
Holloway’s revival of “Dangerous Women”
will endure till the 16th at Theatre Royal, when
will be revived “East Lynne,” the story’ of the
old-fashioned, fallen woman repenting of her one
sin at great length and with much knee drill.
“ Dangerous Women,” as before mentioned, is the
tale of two ladies who sin very greatly, but don’t
make much by it, having the bailiff in their gilded
halls most of the time, and being obliged to hide
murdered gentlemen visitors from the policemen
with the tablecloth, and then stand hastily before
their forgotten feet. There is a young millionaire,
who pretends to be a working man, and a good
girl locked in a garret, hidden in a coffin, and finally
incarcerated in an asylum. Miss Ida Gresham
and Miss Helene Bupdette take the parts of the chief
bad girl, and the good girl respectively, and Miss
Alice Deorwyn gasps and squeaks a good deal as a
guileless servant-maid. George P. Carey enacts
the principal humorous part effectively, and John
P. O’Neill bounds with mdiarubber lightsomeness
through the role of a low-class, humorous villain.
** * *
No change of any importance took place
this week at the Tivoli, where the quicksilver
Craggs still speed gracefully along one another’s
upturned feet, or fall backwards from tall
pyramids made of themselves. James Bain is a
humorist who falls off somewhat in another
sense. A funnier feature is a cleverish
small child who repeats a pathetic verse on
the iniquities of a wife who eats biscuits at incon-
venient times. Prof. De Wynne, with a few new
shadow-pictures, Johnny Coleman, and Will
Whitburn, as a bushranger, help to fill in the
time.

Adorable Dolores’ marvellous voice is gal-
vanising Sydney out of its tired feeling and into
transports of enthusiasm. She seems to have
flutes and birds in her vocal machinery, and her
management of them is mysteriously per-
fect. Her repertory is singularly rare, and she
ranges over Italian, Russian, French, and English
composers—the orchids of the gardens of com-
posers—alike with the same fluency and grace.
Her concerts are nightly crowded.
#* * *
“The Second in Command,” which Brough
staged at Melbourne Princess’s on Saturday (and
which must be his idea of a strong piece, or he
would not have led off with it in Sydney) is to the
present scribe’s way of thinking, a weak, flabby,
sentimental fiction. Stripped of its military
trappings, society graces, incidental music, and
Royal ducal personage it would seem the thinnest
of stories. There is an artificial misunderstanding
between a girl, and a colonel, and the
girl’s young brother, assisted by a major.
The brother opens his mouth too wide in mistake,
whilst the other three people are too reticent,
and the result is a complication of subdued melan-
choly that terminates in the last act, when the
major and the colonel return from S’Africa, the
one in a damaged state, the other in a nice new
kharki suit. Then the girl is at lioerty to hug the
one she loves best, he being the colonel, who is
richly endowed with this world's sugar. A sugary
finish appears inevitable from the start. The
bubble of misconception is bound to burst, and it
is much too polite a bubble to burst with a bang.
* * * *
All the characters of this sad, sweet story
are exceedingly well-behaved, including the
flunkeys appointed to serve refreshments in the
officahs’ quarters. A young man named Hilde-
brand does certainly come forth from the dining-
room with a few vine leaves in his hair, whereat
his noble mother reproves him for vitiating the
atmosphere ; but Hildebrand’s drunk never verges
on vulgarity. The dialogue, though always clever,
lias a tendency to strain after sentimental effect.
Some of Major Brough’s remarks sound labori-
ously pretty. And because the play has
a theatrical style its most convincing inci-
dent is, perhaps, the royal visit in the last
act. Charles Garry, who makes a quick and
heavy appearance as the Jook of Hull, has the
air and straddle of a genuine personage acting the
part that he is supposed to be acting. He looks
as though he were accustomed to accept grovel as
his due simply on account of his relationship to
other people who also sit at the receipt of grovel-
custom. Nothing else in the show seems so
natural as the general uneasiness of the company
in the presence of this appalling Jook, who fires
off “a few well-chosen words,” and retires
hastily, leaving His Majesty’s compliments behind
him. Next to Garry in point of merit comes Miss
Susie Vaughan as an elderly noble matron.
Brough is satisfactory as the Major, and Mrs.
Brough is rather less satisfactory as the misunder-
stood girl. Cecil Ward, Ernest Yere, Leslie
Victor, and Miss Chatteris fill the other principal
places.

Williamson’s comm opera co. are conclud-
ing their six weeks’ innings at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.) with a revival of “The Old Guard.” The
“ 0.G.” will never surrender so long as a first-class
Polydore is available. Lauri, who amounts to
much the same Mayor of Yandrez les Yignes that
Elton used to be, is the main support of Plan-
quette’s opera, and the girl parts get bright treat-
ment from Carrie Moore, Grace Palotta, Maud
Chetwynd, and Lily Everett. Also Kenningham
and Bathurst are more than satisfactory members
of Napoleon’s army, and the ballet and the chorus
will be warmly received up to Friday night.
* * * *
On Saturday, a farewell season of the
estimable Italian opera co. makes a promising
commencement. “Faust,” under any circum-
stances, was always a matter of particular public
interest, and now when it is going to be performed
for the first time in Melbourne under the best musi-
cal circumstances the city has ever known, the in-
terest is strongerthan usual. At time of writing, the
Italian Opera advt. didn’t date anything beyond
“Faust” for the 16th, but it mentioned that 15
operas in all will be produced within the next five
weeks, which, by all accepted rules of arithmetic,
gives an average of three operas per week. More-
over, the management desired to impress upon
economical patrons of music the fact that those 15
operas on a really “ grand ” scale would be avail-
able at ordinary prices.
** * *
This (Thursday) afternoon the managers
of all the shows in Melbourne are furnishing a
vast entertainment at Her Majesty’s, in aid of the
theatrical charities. Any persons who can’t
attend the matinee, owing to being crowded out
or otherwise prevented, are herewith given to
understand that a contribution addressed to the
secretary of the fund will get there just the same.

At the Bijou a few last visits of the brown
Messenger from Mars began to happen on Satur-
day, and to-night (Thursday) brings the conver- -
sion of Horace Parker to a close. The Bijou will
presumably be closed after the departure of the
Hawtrey Comedy Co., re-opening on the 23rd
with the World’s Entertainers’ variety show.
** * *
Touching “ Hearts Are Trumps,” at Mel-
bourne Royal, The Bulletin’s third chapter of
revelations will be held over for a week or two.
Bland Holt’s gigantic drama from Drury Lane
has attained the pinnacle of notoriety in the
politest circles, and, although it might be rather
too full of countesses and millionaires to get the
heartfelt sympathy of the youths who yell,
“Up with the rag,” it seems destined to
run for another month, anyhow. On Tuesday, of
last week, five supernumeraries in Bland Holt’s co.
made a profit of £l2OO apiece on their respective
“ bob ” investments in a Tatt’s. sweep ticket, but
they didn’t haughtily forsake the management on
that account. At latest dates the lucky five were
forming part of the audience in the music-hall
scene as usual, and were only to be distinguished
from other supers by the special earnestness of
their applause when Harrie Ireland makes her
first appearance in brown tights. Enthusiasm
comes easy to the “extra hand” that is about to
get £l2OO for one shilling.
** * *
Except that the Diving Horses have not
yet made their debut, there is nothing to say of
Fitzgeralds’ circus. The iron building in the
Prince’s Bridge district was erected under the
luckiest star in the annals of the Fitz Brothers.
It has held more people within the last few
weeks than their biggest tent ever might,
could or would have accommodated, and
the rush on Saturday nights seems to get
larger instead of lesser. Just now a ballet-master,
with a heathen name, is inviting the attendance
of 100 dancing ladies at the circus, from which one
presumes that the diving gee-gees will be more or
less supported by a ballet in due course.

Edgar Granville, a monologue performer
of inoffensive character, is the latest arrival at the
Opera House, and as he seems to be an addition
to all the people who were there before, the pro-
gramme is all the larger for Edgar’s harmless con-
tributions.
At Poverty Point.
Irene Franklin, the “ dainty Yankee damsel who sing’s
about “ chi-ick-en,” has had a sorrowful time in Aus-
tralia these last few months. Her parents came out with
her from America. The father died, the mother is now
in an asylum, and Irene is left with the care of a baby
brother, born a little while before Mrs. Franklin became
a widow. Manager J. O. Williamson has procured an
engagement for Irene in London, and is shipping her to
her destination. Moreover, he arranged that baby should
have a nurse on the voyage, to save Irene from a good
deal of anxiety and embarrassment. It (jpmes hard on a
grief-stricken girl to discover that she can’t kiss an unfor-
tunate baby brother without causing misunderstandings.
Brough’s mother, Miss Romer, and his step-sister,
Brenda Gibson, are coming out again to Australia. Miss
Gibson, late of Irving’s co., is now married, and it was
for the benefit of her husband’s health that the expedi-
tion, which includes the baby, was suddenly decided
upon. By the way, the hubby of Australia’s original
“ Sweet Lavender ” is a well-known baritone singer.
The trouble from which Wilson Barrett suffered in Ade-
laide, it transpires now, was an acute inflammation some-
where within that noble Roman nose. An operation was
necessary, and it “ had to be conducted from inside the
nose, so that no scar might be left to disfigure the
patient’s face.”
Melba’s advt. cable,announcing her professional engage-
ment to George Musgrove, was thoroughly characteristic
of that distinguished artist. As Manager Williamson
points out, the difficulties which have kept the yearning
prima-donna so long absent from her own naWve land
were altogether of her own making. As far back as ’94
the “ Firm ” thought they had Melba fixed for a visit, but
she changed her mind. More than a year ago, when
Musgrove brought out his opera co., the whisper went
around that he had practically secured the “ Australian
rights ” to a grand production of Melba at the earliest
possible opportunity. But the wily lady didn’t sign a
contract until she had given J. O. Williamson another
chance to bid, and she invited him to name the highest
price he would pay for enabling her “ to add some joy to
the lives of her dear compatriots.” J.O.W. having named
his top figure, the lady persuaded G.M. to go one better
—and that’s all about it.
Cecil Ward, as the Johnnie officer in “Lady Hunt-
worth’s Experiment ” : “ Fighting ain’t work —it’s a plea-
sure.” Then the audience sits perfectly, still and perfectly
quiet, and thinks hard. Two years ago, that remark
might have won appreciative handclaps.
“ King Edward thanks Wilson Barrett for birthday
congratulations ” It ought to be the other way about.
What a king Braggart—that is to say, Barrett—would
make ! How he would strut and pose ! With what an
air carry his crown ! Your mummer is the ideal monarch
—a gilded husk, a sounding empty vessel. The Bulletin
moves that King Edward be at once deposed in favor of
King Wilson Barrett the First.

Is Jane May, the French actress who plays “ L’Enfant
Prodigue,” and similar dumb-show pieces in London,
identical with the Jane Mea formerly of Sarah Bern-
hardt’s travelling company ? She is said to give very
clever imitations of the great woman, which suggests
that she has had a long opportunity for studying the
original. The Jane Mea who figured as second lady in
the co. when Bernhardt came to Australia was a tall and
lovely creature, and seemingly not too old to be only
35 to-day.

was in the chair, and he announced, “De nexta perform-
anci is de part songa, ‘ Salinalari.’” Some of the audi-
ence would have preferred a popular air, but the lovers of
the classical leaned back with a large satisfied expression.
Anyhow, “ Sally in our Alley ” took the whole crowd by
surprise,
Turgenieff on Sarah Bernhardt: “ She is eaten through
and through with chic, reclame and pose. She is
monotonous, cold and dry ; in short, without a single
spark of talent in the highest sense of the word. Her
gait is that of a hen ; she has no play of features ; the
movements of her hands are purposely angular, in order
to be piquant; the whole thing reeks of the boulevards,
of Figaro, and patchouli.”

the wedding,” chuckled the jubilant conspirator.

A very successful trial of the Gardner Electrio
Drill took place at the works of the Austral Otis Com-
pany, South Melbourne, a few days ago. The drill,
although only temporarily fixed, bored 8£ inches in three
minutes through hard basalt. A number of prominent
mining men witnessed the test, and expressed themselves
as greatly pleased with the machine. The Austral Otis
Company have been appointed Sole Agents in Australasia
for the Gardner Electric Drill.*

If further proof is needed that Luhrig Vanners
are the best and most economical machines for dressing
fines and slimes, it will be found in the fact that the Tas-
mania gold mine, Beaconsfield, Tasmania, has decided to
increase the number of these machines in the extension
of the present mill and reduction works. When com-
pleted it will have over 100 head of stampers at work, and
60, Luhrig Yanners, including the additional 24 of the
large type. Before finally adopting plans the Tasmania
board sent its manager to inspect the chief reduction
works in other States. Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne, are the Sole Agents for the Luhrig appli-
ances.*

One thing about the Eastern Extension
Oo.—it is as rich as Croesus and it isn’t a bit
mean. It pays its agents handsomely, and never
refuses them anything reasonable in the way of
expenses. And thus it happens that Federal
Attorney-General Deakin finds on assuming office
that all the agreements made between the Eastern
Extension Co. and the various State Govts, are
utterly indeterminable, except with the consent
of both parties interested ! In a word, the E.E.
Co. has got the States by the wool for ever and
ever, Amen. “I never came across a state of
things like this in my life,” says Deakin, in his
agony. But who can wonder at it? The States
pay their Cabinet politicians (say) £6OO a year or
so in and out of office, and a country gets exactly
the sort of brains, &c., that it pays for. In mak-
ing a bargain, the £6OO-a-year politician is a mere
chopping-block for the ’cute, well-paid agents of
the Eastern Extension Co.
Application of the beneficent Australian
“strike” to S’African warfare has staggered
English military offisahs. Trooper E. E. White,
A Squadron, N.S.W. 3rd Mounted Rifles, reports
two “ strikes ” near Standerton. On the first oc-
casion two blankets and a waterproof sheet were
served out; and later one blanket was taken
away. The men shivered awhile; then rushed
the guard and recaptured the other blankets
The officer chased them, “got a bad buster,” and
returned to his tent discomfited, while the Aus-
tralians “cheered lustily.” The next “strike”
occurred when lib. of flour was served for a day’s
ration; with no wood or time to cook it. “The
whole of the squadron struck ” for biscuits ; and
got them. “The Tommies were amazed at our
temerity.”
They stood open-mouthed as we told the officers we re-
fused to obey duty unless we were given the biscuits. If
they had, they said, been guilty of the same thing 12
months’ imprisonment with hard labor would have been
their lot.
Naturally—for rank mutiny. What more comio
situation can you conceive than the meeting of an
officer “of the old school ” with this new brand of
private ? And how does the incident promise for
Australian Independence ?

Smith seems to be the sort of name to get
mixed up with alleged poisoning cases in N.S.W.
A Smith was the chemist of the Dean business,
Jane Smith was charged in connection with the
Talune case, and now Florence Ellen Smith makes
a sensational statement in the Redfern case.
Over-enthusiastic Sydney tailoresses on
strike assaulted one alleged “sweater,” dislocat-
ing his nose and battering him with specked fruit.
More good would have been done by calling
public attention to the fact that Government
departments patronise some of the worst of the
low-wage firms.
“ Dick" : The B. wanted to see a square-
jawed, not-too-old man sent along to sit on the
city Bench, and the city “push” in succession to
Mr. Mair, retired. Mr. A. N. Barnett, appointed,
answers to some of the specifications. He comes
from Albury (where he succeeded Boldrewood
Browne); is still young, keen, and credited with
firmness.
The luck of some men ! Near Broken
Hill, t’other day, during a blinding dust-storm, a
young man fell into an old mining-shaft, but, at
30ft. down, landed on a couple of planks which
had been placed across the shaft. To this pre-
carious perch he clung for several hours before he
was rescued—none the worse except for a severe
shaking.
Melbourne’s new railway—via Collingwood
to Heidelberg—-will pay well from the start, if
looks are anything. The department began with
a quarter-hourly service, and the workers of
’Wood and the parts beyond speedily made it
difficult to get a seat anywhere on the town side
of Simpson’s Road. The Tramway monopolists
have been hit hard.
The discussion on the Quinn Bill in
N.S.W. Assembly for the signing of every press
article with the real name of its author, was
adjourned—which means that no more will be
heard of it. The pow-wow over the thing
showed its supporters to be desperately lacking in
humor ; the measure was one which could only
have been expected of some dense Scotchman,
like Wully Affleck, of Gundaroo, or old Dr. Ross.
By_ the way, had the Bill passed, all Bulletin
artioles would thereafter have been signed “John
Smith.” 6
How about this for Wilson Barrett’s
Alfred the Great essay-competition? It was
turned out by a small Sydney school-boy in the
ordinary course of history-lesson :
When the Romans went away the Britons were attacked
by some savage people called the Piets and Scots, who
lived in the Northern parts of the Island. They were also
invaded by pirates from Denmark and Germany, called
Danes and Saxons. Then came a good man called Saint
Augustine, who taught the people to know the
true god, and to believe in the Lord. Alfred the
Great was told to look after the cakes, and he was so
taking up with his thoughts that he forgot the cakes,
and the Dane {sic) came in and scolded him, and said go
away you lazzy fellow, and Alfred went away, and he
ruled over some of the saxons, and he said this country
shall be Christian.
Historian Freeman, by the way, exploded the
cake-myth and other Alfred-inyths.
“Miles”: That Pyrrhic victory of the Boers
at Brackenlaagte, where they succeeded in “ re-
moving ” two Imperial guns, rather indicates that
those stubborn patriots are beginning to break
down under the strain of constant pursuit and the
raiding of their country. It’s getting on their
nerves, and in their exasperation they face about,
fight pitched battles, make frontal attacks,
as at Fort Italia and Brackenlaagte—and
lose hundreds of men. A score of such vic-
tories, and the war is over. As long as the Boers
could keep out of the reach of the British columns
by constantly moving, they kept the great, over-
whelming, colossally-expensive British army tied
up in S’Africa. When they begin to seek, instead
of waiting to be sought, Ketohener’s stock com-
mences to rise. Apropos , had the Boers shown
the same splendid vim at the beginning of the
war, Ladysmith might have told another tale.
Then they were afflicted with overmuch caution,
just when reckless daring was wanted. Now,
when every fighting-man is worth his weight in
diamonds, they are reckless to an extreme.
Ed. Bulletin.—“Palman, Q.M.F.” (8.,
2/11/’01) makes half-a-dozen mistakes in 15 lines.
1. Elliot, the defender of Gibraltar, was not an
artillery, but a. cavalry, officer. He for years
commanded a light-dragoon regiment, and was
with it in the abortive expedition to Quiberon
Bay. (See Wilkinson’s recently-published “ Life
of Lord Heathcote,” in N.S.W. Publio Library.)
2. Elliot was not educated at the R.M.A.,
Woolwich. That institution was not established
till after the Siege of Gibraltar. 3. Lawrence
and Wilson did not “ defend Lucknow and Delhi
respectively. ” 4. Sir Henry Lawrence, Chief
Commissioner of Oude, defended the Residency at
Lucknow—his own house and grounds. He was
killed, and Col. Inglis—an infantry man—took
command. He was succeeded by Havelock
and Outram. 5. There is no record
of anybody named “ Wilson ” having
ever, on any occasion, defended Delhi.
6. A certain Brigadier Wilson happened to be
senior officer- present with the besiegers when the
Kashmir Gate and Selimghur Fort of Delhi were
stormed in ’57. The whole story of Delhi and
Lucknow is to be found in Roberts’ “41 years.”
You had better choose someone who knows some-
thing next time you want me called a liar.—J.R.
[We didn’t “want” “J.R.” called a liar. We
merely published a letter written us by an Im-
perial officer, whom we do not personally know,
from London.—Ed. B.]

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
It used to be customary with people who had
small business transactions with N.S.W. Govt. Depart-
ments to pay their dues in stamps. This was of great
convenience in remote districts where banks and post-
offices are inaccessible. Now that the Post Office is run
by the Commonwealth, N.S.W. Treasury is charged If
per cent, discount on the stamps, and so has ukased that
collectors of revenue are not to accept payment in
stamps. This will cause great inconvenience outback. It
seems to be somebody’s business to arrange that stamps
received from the inter-State Govts, as revenue should be
exempt from discount.

Editor Bulletin.— ln re your comments on the
United Insurance Co.’s last annual report, we note your
remarks with regard to decline in receipts from interest
and rents, and, in reply thereto, would desire to say that
the amount has been reduced this year through our
having charged against interest and rent account some
£BOO premium, paid by us for the purchase of a large
parcel of Govt, debentures, the whole amount of premium
having been written off last year’s interest account. We
would further say that this fact was mentioned to the
shareholders at our annual meeting, held last Wednesday
week.—Yours, B. Haigh, secretary. 8/11/01.

* * * *
“ It is indeed a thousand pities that Australian
mining men, when they want English capital, should not
differentiate,” writes a much-travelled correspondent to
The Bulletin. “It should be laid down as a rigid rule
that legitimate propositions should be reserved for finan-
cial houses who genuinely wish to invest money in honest
development-work, and anything that ‘meows’ can be
used on the public speculator at large. He is alwavs a
fool or a knave. But with big financial houses 'like
Rothschilds, who have hitherto steered very clear of
Australia (despite the fact that every field boasts 40
‘ Rothschild’s agents’), it is very different. The other
day, in the city of London, they promised to support a
proposition, provided it was confined to private develop-
ment and would stand inspection. That proposition
hailed from N.S.W. and carried the backing of five excel-
lent reports from five well-known Australian engineers.
Yet when the show was inspected it was found to be very
wild and with a long tail. Such things simply mean the
‘passing’ on sight of many a decent Australian
scheme.”

To an Australian miner, from an old mate in
Alaska:— “ I have this winter been stampeding all
over Alaska, and have had hard times. It has been
a terrible season for storms, and we were lucky in
not being caught in any. We got back home two days
before the big storm, which was a very bad one ; about
30 below zero, and blowing just as it knows how to blow
in this country. A friend of mine got caught in it, and
came near losing his arms. It was on the summit of
the mountain, and the wind picked him up and threw
him down on the ice, injuring him very much. Then the
others broke a trail in the snow, and three of them had
to walk all night to keep circulation going. In Nome all
the cabins were covered over with snow, and when we
got up in the morning the door was blocked, and we had
to wait until the relief-party came round to shovel the
drift from the door. It has been storming for over a
month now. Four days before the big storm it regis-
tered 72 below zero back in the mountains. We were
returning from the Arctic, were out of dog-feed, and had
to travel. I can tell you she was a bad day to
travel. The dogs, poor devils, couldn’t move fast enough
to keep us warm, so we had to cut some of the slow ones
loose and try and get them to follow ; but they crawled
into the snow, and there, I guess, they froze to death.
There have been many men frozen to death, got caught
in blizzards, and, you might say, died standing up ; for
when one of them gets you in a place where there is no
shelter, you are in a bad hole. We got caught in one,
but saw it coming and were well prepared. We got our
sleeping-bags out of the sleigh, crawled into them, and
lay in them for six hours until it blew over. We cut the
dogs loose out of the harness and let them shift for them-
selves. They all came out right, as they were native dogs.
“This has been the worst winter any of the old-
timers have seen in this country. A lot of people came
here from the States, and started out, and have never
been heard from since. I have had a very good fellow
for a partner this winter, but a devil of a fellow
to spring yarns upon you. Don’t matter where
you are, or what you are doing; something you
say always puts him in mind of a little story, and then he
starts. We were going over a divide of the mountains,
and one of our dogs got the greenhide harness in his
mouth and chewed it. It was blowing about 40 miles an
hour, and bitter cold ; but as soon as we stopped to mend
the harness he started with his ‘ little story,’ and I had
to pull out a * gun ’ before he would stop talking. But,
when we moved on a little and got into shelter,
we both had a good laugh over it. Still, it was
the only way I could stop him talking. He is very
near as bad as another partner I had in Australia.
I had no ‘ gun ' over there, or I would have been com-
pelled to pot him! I have started about 20 claims over
in the Arctic, and I think some are very good. Going
back in a few days to prospect them, and have them
ready for the spring. I don’t expect to be in Nome for
about 8 weeks, which will bring us into the spring, when
the ice will be out of the river, and we will have a chance
of prospecting. I have let two lays on tribute on two of
my claims in the Nome district. I get 25 per cent, out of
the total output from the claims, so I won’t be doing bad
out of them."
* * * .
“The ways of mining engineers at the top of
the profession vary much; so does their remuneration.
Hayes Hammond, the great consulting engineer of the
Consolidated Goldfields of S’Africa, makes, say, £30,000 a
year in fees, and recently he said he thought he would
give it up because he didn’t get sufficiently paid. Of
course, he has other interests to look after, but I am cer-
tain he doesn’t work as hard as, say, the average editor.
Dr. Hatch, who should step into Hammond’s shoes, of
course, doesn’t get anything like that, but he is an inter-
esting personality. His greatest joy (which he rarely
sees) is his rose-garden at Addlestone, Surrey. He has
just finished an exhaustive monograph on the goldfields
of India—principally in the Mysore district—for the
Indian Govt., for which he got splendidly paid. Of
another type are the engineers that have been streaming
to the Gold Coast for some time past. Their salaries
range from £l5OO to £4OOO a year. Many of them are,
unfortunately, only mining engineers because they are
perfectly willing to send cables ‘as directed ’ written out
previous to their departure in the London offices of the
companies they represent.”
* * * •
The Broken Hill Municipal Council, five-sixths
Laborite, is fighting desperately for the right to tax the
mining co.’s on the basis of profits earned locally. If the
co.’s should only pay on the basis authorised by N.S.W.
Equity Court, the whole Laborite policy of municipal
socialism becomes impossible ; and so, though the muni-
cipality went down on every point in the Equity Court,
the Labor aldermen accept as their only hope the 100 to 1
chance of success on an appeal to the Privy Council.
Which explains their obstinate perseverance with the
suits in the teeth of overwhelming local opposition.
* 9 * m
British B.H. made a loss of £3360 in the Jan.-
July half-year. Lead averaged then about £l3 10s. —
started at £l6 ss. and closed at £l2 ss. Present average
about £lllos. This looks pretty bad for the co., even
though things underground have improved a good bit.
At present, only prospecting is being done, and that on a
small scale. Further, lead-market reports are not bright;
the German trade-smashes are upsetting things. Just
now, it isn’t nice to turn-up files and find that lead
averaged only £9 14s. in 1894, and to reflect that there is
no obvious reason why it should not go there again,
although, certainly, there is no obvious reason why it
should.
* • * *
The most striking feature of Zeehan (Tas.) is its
tribute system and the cause of it. The slump that fol-
lowed the V.D.L. Bank failure made the payment of any
calls a maniac dream, and it looked as if every mine must
close. But owing to a very liberal land law, every miner
could secure a quarter-acre freehold allotment for a small
sum, payment being extended over three years afterhe had
made improvements on it to the value of £5O. Conse-
quently nearly every miner, especially if married,
had his own house and land without having
to compete with the absentee speculator at a
town-lands auction as at present. The homesteads
being unsaleable after a slump—though realising
largely increased prices during a boom—the majority of
miners were practically tied to the field. Result was they
had to raise galena, or starve, or give away their homes
and emigrate. They preferred the first course, and revivi-
fied the field in an astonishing manner, leading to the
declaration of dividends by three companies alone of
£llO,OOO, and in many cases making good rises
themselves. They are now raising 50 per cent,
of the total output of the district. But such
a resurrection of a dead or moribund field is not
likely to recur, owing to a new Land Act, passed by
Mines Minister Mulcahy. Seeing the high prices ob-
tained in boom-times for some of these miners’ home-
steads, he blocked the facilities for securing them that
the old Act gave, and threw open the door to competition
at public auction. The absentee speculators jumped at
the opening when the Curtin-Davis district township was
offered for sale. The miner failed to secure a home, and,
when the high-flown expectations of the mines failed,
there being no miners fixed on the township, both mines
and township were deserted. That is what will happen
again in every district where exaggerated opinions of
payable, though common-place, mines are exploded.
Minister Mulcahy may, perhaps, grow wiser with the
century, and refrain from tinkering with land and mining
Acts. Likewise, he may not.
* * *
Kalgoorlie mines are threatened with a fuel
shortage. The local supply of firewood is only estimated
to last a few years longer. The consumption of fuel for
ore-roasting, crushing, winding-plants, and water-con-
densation is enormous. The wood has been cut out for a
great distance, and there is but one forest within a
reasonable reach of railway communication remaining.
Oil will probably be the future fuel for these mines. To-
day, oil could be probably landed there for the price of
coal, while its efficiency would be much greater as well as
the economy in handling. Its use would involve many
changes in the system of firing, but these would be small
compared with the money saveable by its use.
* • * *
“Dusty Pat”: The “Carnarvon Castle” (re-
ferred to by B. 5/10/’01) is located at Black fellows’ Gully,
10 miles from Mornish (Q.). The original co. spent thou-
sands in development. The leader is frozen-on to a hard
diorite wall, and is rich in places. The co. had one
crushing and then “ chucked it,” whereupon O’Donoghue
stepped in, and reaped the benefit of the co.’s work. In
same place there is a very curious crushing plant—a
Chilian mill, manufactured locally of granite, the
pan being two slabs cut and fitted neatly to-
gether. The rollers are also granite, and the
plant crushes all the locally-raised stone—giving fair
results. About six miles south of Mornish is another
curiosity locally known as the Crater. It is an old
geyser-opening, about 30ft. deep in the straight, after
which it goes down in an underlie. Surrounding it is a
patch of limestone about 100yds. x 50yds. in area, and
alongside the limestone is a patch of red soil, of about the
same area, which carries a little fine gold from grass roots
to bottom. The country, for a half-mile square, is a net-
work of ironstone and quartz reefs, all poor, although a
lot of loose surface-stone that was bagged and
crushed went as high as soz. Much trenching and pros-
pecting has been done, but where the rich stone came
from, so far no man knoweth.

A. correspondent sends a few lines re mining on
that long-quiescent but promising field, the Hodgkinson
N.Q. The General Grant, belonging to the Cecil Syndi-
cate, a Bull co., with E. D. Miles and Co., of the Towers
as agents, crushed consistently for eight months at local
10-head battery for an average of 22dwt. from a reef Ift
to 4ft. wide for a length of 400 ft. on No. 2 level (depth
400 ft.). Sank another 100 ft. and crosscut the reef, 3ft’
6in. of solid quartz, supposed to be better than at the
other levels. The Cecil Syndicate owns eight shows
within a radius of 12 miles, and now has only the General
Grant working. The rest, comprising 150 acres, has been
under exemption over three years. Directors : Earl of
Warwick, Tom Mills, Ross Robinson, Edward Pope

“ W.J.M.” : Some further particulars re Callide
railway—as an additional refutation of certain statements
made by “ Canip ” (12/10/’01). I have it officially that the
plans, sections and estimates of the first 40 miles of the
trial survey are now complete, and the cost per mile for
the narrow (2ft. 6in.) and broad (3ft. 6in.) gauges are
£2078 and £2550 respectively. The gradients in the 40-
mile section are all easy and well under the gradients
allowed by the co’s Act. In the event of a tunnel bein<>-
constructed, it will not exceed a quarter-mile long. The
interest upon the capital expended, plus maintenance of
permanent way and cost of labor in running the rail-
way, practically means the cost of transport from field to
port, and so is embraced in the cost of the coal delivered
f.o.b. Gladstone. In addition, there is the revenue deriv-
able from passenger-traffic and freights. The competi-
tors—fpswich, Burrum, Clermont, Darling Downs—are
not likely to be of serious moment, because Callide coal is
greatly superior, and Callide coal’s sphere of operations
will be in foreign ports and northern parts of Q As to
the probable competitors, Port Clinton coal is admittedly
a lignite, and therefore of little value for ocean-carrying
Dawson River coal-field is out of the market owing to
remoteness from a shipping port.

tioned specialities are carried.*

King Edward, who seems to take a fresh
title every month (and who, by the way, arranged
at a recent London celebration to be greeted by
flunkeys with “ Hail to our Emperor ! ”), should
think over the tendency of territories to dwindle
as titles stretch out. Julius Cuesar ruled over all
his known world with but one title : when from
the feeble hand of the last of the Caesars fell the
final atom of the Roman power the degenerate
weakling had 26 resplendent words to convey the
idea of his magnificence. (
* * * *
King Ned professes to be compensated
for the anxiety of the Duke of York’s trip to Aus-
tralia by its “complete success.” If he only
knew how Imperial stock has been sent down
here by the visit of the unfortunate little man !
Kings of old had a Jester as a Court functionary ;
the modern King should have at his Court a
Candid Friend. The Bulletin is prepared to
recommend Edward a man for the position.
m m * m
The details of the Ducal expenses in Maori-
land, dragged out during the long discussion
on the Estimates, are amusing reading. Seddon,
on his own responsibility, ordered new liveries for
all the vice-regal flunkeys. On his own initiative,
too, he bought gorgeous sets of harness for the
Ducal carriage. When Gov. Ranfurley saw then,
however, he flatly refused to allow the flaming
gear to be used on his horses, stating that they
had some self-respect. King Seddon will probably
take the lovely gear ’Ome for the Coronation ;
though too loud for a Duke, it should fill the bill
for a Radical “king.”
GOT HIM ON A STRING: A FAVORITE
ROLE.
The endless, squalid squabble over the
bill! Melbourne City Council now declines to pay
its share of the cost of the Ducal decorations
because the Public Works Dept, did not halve the
few shillings got by selling the draggled remnants
of the triumph. The Council reckons it is being
got at for about eighteenpence, and its rage over
this robbery is something frightful. Also,
in the village of Hamilton, a storekeeper sued a
prominent citizen lately for the price of gun-
powder used in tomfoolery on “ Mafeking day.”
Proceedings were suspended “ to allow the towns-
people an opportunity of subscribing the amount.”
Picture the joy of passing round the hat to pay for
the circus business which welcomed the raising of
a “siege” that is now known to have never
existed 1

Maoriland is now suffering the most severe
epidemic of influenza in its history. Many local
medicos assert that the returned S’African troopers
brought it with them. “ Patriotism ” does not
pan out as all loot.
One hundred English school-marms, it is
cabled, are to be sent out to the Boer concentra-
tion camps to teach the little children (how to
die ?) under Lord Ketchener, the finishing school-
master.

A “sword of honor” has been presented
to Baden-Powell as a tribute from “ the people of
New South Wales.” Parliament should intervene
—of course it won’t—in this. “The people,” in
such circumstances, should be the people of the
country as officially represented. A self-consti-
tuted collector of children’s pennies, dubbing
itself and its fowl-yard “ the people of N.S. W.,”
simply noodleises N.S.W., and swells Baden-
Powell’s hairless head.

The irony of things. Two ping-pong
tournaments are being carried on in Adelaide to
raise money for a memorial fund to the dead in
S’Africa. This tippety-tappety game in honor of
men who (no matter how lop-sided their idea of
the Cause) died in their boots, gun in hand !
Advertisements are appearing for articior
of clothing for the Ketchener concentration-camps
in S’Africa. Shrouds (in baby sizes) must be
most particularly needed.

Dear Bulletin. —Touching that Sydney
anti-spitting bylaw. T’other afternoon, walking
down King-street, I noticed a person with his back
towards me besprinkling the pavement with
copious expectorations. A policeman also noticed
and strode up with arm outstretched. On reach-
ing his quarry the “cop” suddenly stopped and
became deeply interested in something else. I
saw the face of the spitter then. He was a
member of the Cabinet. —B.

* * * •
Mayor Graham has hard and thankless
work trying to get Sydney to keep itself clean.
Some old identities make it a matter of personal
quarrel. Sydney was pampered in the early time ;
if anything in the way of cleaning or ornamenting
took place, the charge was booked to the country
at large, not to the property-owners of the
borough. Gov. Gipps labored to break down this
practice, and, like Mayor Graham, provoked
bitter antagonisms among property-owners. For-
merly, the paving, cleaning and lighting of Sydney
were actually set down on the Parliamentary
Estimates.
* ♦ * *
Concerning a possible new daily paper :
In place of the old yarn about Alfred Harmsworth, the
“King of the New Journalism,” coming out to start a
Liberal paper in Melbourne, there is now in circulation a
tale about a new unmitigatedly democratic paper. Some
of the Labor party have got the idea that they can start a
daily on £30,000 and no brains. Just fancy the “ Daily
Tocsin,” run by 6. M. Prendergast, Stephen Barker, Rev.
J. B. Ronald, M.P., and a few others ! No doubt
the office would be paved with sufficient good intentions
to convert it into a commercial hell. The delusive belief
about the ease with which a paper can be started and
conducted isn’t confined to the uneducated, but afflicts
people who might be expected to know better.

The day after he got ten years for holding-
up the White Cliffs mail-coach Tomlinson indi-
cated that he was prepared to “give somebody
away.” Arrangements were made to take down
the confession ; but just then the interesting
prisoner shut up tight. By-the-way, police-officer
Nolan, who had charge of the case, gets his
reward in the shape of a shift from the western
wilderness to Bombala.

“ Cuss ” to The Bulletin :
Serious doubts exist in many minds out here (western
N.S.W.) re the guilt of young Tomlinson, convicted of the
White Cliffs coach robbery. It seems queer justice that
a man should be sent up for ten years for robbery under
arms and receiving (from himself), when the Crown failed
to proceed with the more serious charge of shooting with
intent to kill, on the ground that both Kanter—the man
shot —and the driver absolutely failed to identify accused.
The money was found placed on top of the clothing in
accused’s box, and a £lO note was found between the
blankets of his bed. Now, why should he have planted
that note there? Does it not look as if the money
was so placed in order that, if any sort of a search was
made, it could hardly be missed? Also, at the trial at
Broken Hill, why was not the witness called who was
ready to swear he saw a stranger on a bike leave the town
ahead of the coach ? said stranger being at White Cliffs the
day after the robbery 7. As to the overcoat matter, any
man-of-the-world can readily surmise an excuse for keep-
ing quiet about that: there are no “ benches in the
Park ”at White Cliffs. To conclude : Tomlinson is doing
10 years because the stolen money was found in his box in
a room occupied by other men, and till the day after the
robbery that box had been in a tent which anybody could
enter. After removing the box to the hotel a discussion
arose, and, to settle the matter, Tomlinson went to his
box to get a photo. When he returned to the dis-
putants he was sick and dazed so much that the local
medico had to be sent for, who found him suffering from
symptoms of shock with impaired heart-action. It is
possible that Tomlinson found the planted money and
realised the terrible position he was in—hence his distress.
Undoubtedly, there is not a nigger in the woodpile, but a
woman in the case—also, some most infernal scoundrels.

Brisbane Tram Co. now rules the roost as
regards suburban traffic in that town. The
manager, a shrewd Yank, receives deputations
that apply cap-in-hand for extensions, most of
which are lines that warp the revenues of the
State railway. S’Malcolm M'Eacharn is King-
Pin in this big concern.
The recent case of the s.s. City of Lincoln,
which, after shipping 700 horses in Melbourne
recently, put into Port Adelaide with nearly 200
of the poor brutes dead or dying, is a shocking
example of blind adherence to custom. There
was a time when no one in his senses would ship
horses without making provision for “slinging”
them in bad weather; but now the stalls are
depended on to keep them on their legs, so that
if a horse goes down, he stays down, and rolls
under the feet of those beside him, throwing them
down too. Also, if the stalls are not very strong,
something is sure to give way The momentum
of such a mass of living animals (100 horses weigh
50 tons or over), swaying backward and forward
with the pitching and rolling of the ship is more
than any ordinary timber can stand When a
horse is slung and lifted off his feet he can neither
hurt himself nor rns neighbours, tie swings
with the motion of the ship. It costs a little
more to provide slings, but their use would make
referred to. SU uns P eakable horrors as that

tions) a couple of pages in its current issue to the

stated, generally wear stays now, and the making

lace they run to as much as £5 ss. The future
Listoriaif of the Boer Wat shou.d study that
article hard ; it may put him on the track of many
nngs. „
Interesting to recount that Melbourne’s
first telephone exchange was started by Byron
Moore and a few supporting syndicators a
year or two before the tinkle of a telephone bell
was ever heard m London. Ihe tale of the
telephone as Byron Moore tells it is a convincing
grievance against the .Government Dept., which
eventually got possession of the business it had
been striving to bring into disrepute.
The Manly (Sydney) steamer hands, who
struck for increased wages on Sunday last, showed
a business acumen which is too often lacking in
organised labor efforts. It was a fine Sunday,
and the day’s takings by the co. were prospec-
tively big; the next day was the King’s Birthday
holiday, probable takings £3OO or £4oo—and the
co. was called upon to grant the increase at 30
minutes’ notice, and, with the increase, 12 months’
guaranteed employment. Of course, it could do
nothing else but yield,

Frank Farnell’s Bill to amalgamate the
i ega l professions in N.S.W. reverses a Council
measure of over 70 years ago. Farnell’s notion is
that amalgamation will cheapen law. When the
branches were dissevered in 1829 or thereabouts,
the argument for separation was also that it
would cheapen law.
Flannelette.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ The child was standing in front of the fire when a spark set her flannelette nightgown ablaze.
In spite of prompt assistance, the burns were so severe that, in a few hours, death put an end to her
suffering.”— Any day, any paper.
QH, there’s nothing like cremation
To reduce the population,
And the cheapest way to do it is to let
All the kids you don’t require
Stand abound a blazing fire,
In their little nightgowns made of flannelette.
Flannelette ! Flannelette ! though it’s useless in
the WET,
And in winter time it keeps the kiddies cool,
It’s so very cheap and clean, and it burns like
kerosene,
Does the gaudy cotton stuff that “ wears like
wool.”
There’s nothing now alarming
In the trade of baby-farming—
The police can never get you in their net.

When they’ve dug up all your garden,

N.S.W. Education Minister Perry can-
not affoi'd free Primary schools in N.S.W., but
expresses himself strongly on the suppression of
truancy. Truancy, now to be suppressed by a
costly army of inspectors, is largely the outcome of
the miserable threepennies exacted as fees.
Children given the school-money squander it on
the way, and then avoid school to escape the
consequences. On the other hand, children who
have to plead poverty play truant to avoiu the
humiliation. The fees only bring in £70,000 a
year, which amount has been well-nigh balanced
by the sum spent in giving free-poHage to news-
papers. Better fat newspaper-owners than free
education ! !
» • * »
One of the stupid retrenchments of
N.S.W. Assembly this year was to cut down the
proposed salaries of the medical superintendents
of the Asylums for the Insane. The N.S.W.
public asylums, despite splendid appointments
and humane treatment of the inmates, have been
too long looked upon as mere places of incarcera-
tion. The medical superintendents should be
put in a position to devote themselves to the study
of mental disease. Owing to a cheese-paring
policy, we are already far behind America and
other countries in the medical treatment of the
insane'; and if, in ignorance or in spite, the medical
posts at the asylums are made still more unat-
tractive, further retrogression is certain.

A fine crusted technicality that has
stimulated the legal palate for 40 or 50
years had itsliquox
spilt badly and
fianlly as far as Vic-
toria is concerned
on appeal to the
Full Court in an
“ under 16 ” case
recently. The guilt or
innocence of the Romeo
concerned turned on
the question whether
Juliet was, or was not,
a few days older
than the birth-cer-
tificate attested.
One, Baron
Romilly, had laid
it down long ago
that a birth-
certificate was
only evidence
of the fact that
a child was
born some-
time before
the actual
date of re-
gistration,
but that it
was not evi-
dence of the
exact date of
birth, al-
though that
was specified
in the certifi-
cate. It has
been con-
tended, for
instance,
that in-
terested per-
sons might post-

births to suit
“fair fames”
or rights of
property.
The Vic. Full
Court, how-
ever, without
calling upon
the Crown,
went bald-
headed for
the eom-
monsense
notion
that such
a certificate
is some evidence
of all the facts recorded
in it. It is to be hoped
that the Colonial Office will not reprove the Full
Court for thus contemptuously brushing aside an
honored British precedent.

* * * *
Mr. F. B. Suttor’s tiny Coroners’ Bill in
N.S.W. Leg. Council needs watching. One of
its provisions abolishes, in the case of gaol in-
quests, juries composed of freemen and prisoners.
The necessity for this abolition needs explanation.
Numbers of cases are recorded in which mixed
juries were unmixed blessings.
“ Railway Man ” complains of a genuine
grievance :
A big fashionable bazaar is set going, and the stalls are
divided amongst ladies in groups according to their
spheres of influence. There is (say) a Locomotive Stall,
managed by the wives of the heads of the Railway Dept.
Mrs. Departmental Head gets a list of the subordinate
officers’ names and addresses from hubby, and distributes
among them-raffle tickets and collecting-cards with polite
notes. The officers, to oblige the boss’s lady, push the
sale of these. Result, many of the men spend money
which they can ill spare to advance some charity (?) or
other. A man who does not feel disposed to shell-out may
find it would cause future unpleasantness to use his judg-
ment freely. The subscription-list, charity-bazaar ticket,
and general cadging business is assuming huge propor-
tions, and the railwayman is not the only sufferer.
* » * *
N.S.W. temperance agitators have taken a
new departure that will bring them much support
from moderate drinkers and non-fanatics. The
new league aims at the nationalisation of the
traffic. Apparently, the cold-tea brigade is
beginning to absorb men who have about
them some of the stuff of which statesmen
are made. State control is the only satis-
factory way out of the liquor puzzle. Prohi-
bition leads to illicit stills, sly-grog selling, and
poisoning of the public. Private control has
proved a failure, and in the nationalisation of the
traffic lies the solution of the tied-house trouble,
the peroxide damsel problem, and the adulte-
rated-liquor difficulty.

Is this a solemn truth or a mere ornamen-
tal yell ? A correspondent writes :
Related that, not along ago, while a convivial party
were having a “ good time ” one Saturday at Gosford
(N.S.W.), one member thereof (an M.P.) boasted
he could get a steam-dredger sent to that unim-
portant locality. A dredger is of as much’use there
as a giraffe would be, but, all the same, it made its
appearance. Being there, some semblance of work had
to be found for the thing, and it was accordingly em-
ployed in dredging a channel for the fishing boats, craft
which drew fully six inches of water. When a mud punt
was filled, it was towed about 100yds., and the contents
dumped into Brisbane Water. This valuable work
only cost the country two or three score pounds pet
week, and as the crew spent most of their earnings in
Gosford, the townsfolk “ lay low an’ sed nuffin.”
HIS MASTERPIECE.
ARTIST'S DAUGSTER : “ Now, which do you think
is father’s best work ?”
HE (promptly): “ You!”

“F.C,” : ’Tisn’t the way-back bush-school
only that is a disgrace to the State that runs it.
Richmond (Melb.), central school, in almost the
biggest suburb, is overcrowded. In winter the
ground is a swamp. There is no shelter whatever,
not even a tree. The youngsters used to be let eat
their lunches on the staircases and in the passages,
but, for some reason, they are now put outside
and the doors locked during the dinner hour.
But—they have a flag-pole and one of Sargood’s
gaudy flags to shelter them from sun and rain.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Church of England “official
organ” opens its vox inhurnana stop on the
flogging question :
Some years ago it was considered that those who in-
flicted pain should suffer pain, arid the punishment
awarded as a deterrent was by means ot flogging. This
had a salutary effect, and checked larrikinism. But some
maudlin sympathy in the House, questioning the action
of certain magistrates who ordered this mode ot punish-
ment, had the effect of staying their hands. The law,
though still in force, is a dead letter so far as its adminis-
tration is concerned, and it needs again to be enforced if
ruffianism is to be put down.
The shriek after the lash by the fat Tory whose
stomach gets in the way of his running, and who
is in deadly fear for his gold watch, is explainable,
but a clerical whine, in the name of Christ, for the
triangle is simply loathsome.
• * * *
To “ go through ” a husband’s pockets has
long been recognised as one of woman’s most
sacred privileges. But since the advent of the
franchise agitation, even that fundamental clause
of the Magna Charta of womankind seems to be
threatened. In a recent N.S.W. divorce-case the
wife alleged that simply because she, by chance,
went near her husband’s clothes while he was in
bed, and rattled his watch and chain, he jumped
up, “beat her violently, tore her night-dress off,
made her wipe up the stains of her own gore,
burned her clothes, and kicked her downstairs ! ”
* * * *
Dr. Maloney’s Legitimisation of Innocents
Bill was promptly thrown out by Yic. House of
Landlords. The only observation meriting the
least notice was that of Melville, M.L.C., who
considered “the Bill did not go far enough—the
seduction of a woman should have the effect of
constituting the marriage tie.” Before this bril-
liant idea could be put into force there would
need to be a removal of present restrictions as to
the number of husbands a woman may have —and
the number of wives a man may take to himself.

Victorian Artists’ Society has just had a
meeting, at which Academicians or Associates, or
some other pretty-titled people, were chosen in
close imitation of ’Ome methods. Whilst N.S.W.
art-traditions mostly consist of fight, in Victoria
they are fight and frill. Why should there always
be such a huge amount of the stoush spirit created

* * * *
The law creeps slowly at times. A Sydney
policeman kneeling down peeping through the
keyhole of a shop at 9 p.m. informed writer that
that was the only way of catching offenders
against the Early Closing Act.
At a Sydney Police Court, the other day, a
small boy appeared on bail for breathing too
loudly, in the presence of a policeman, or some
other crime. But when he answered to the name
of Jim Jackson, the policeman remembered that
he wasn’t the Jim Jackson he had arrested and said
so; whereupon the youth tremblingly admitted
that he wasn’t Jim, but only Bill. Jim, he said,
was “took” with the typhoid, and he had come
instead. The pathos of the incident struck the
whole court. One man in particular was so broken-
up that he announced his intention of adopting
the self-sacrificing Bill and bringing him up as his
own younger brother. Whether he thought better
of it afterwards is not known. Most people think
better of their good resolutions, which is one
reason why Bill’s path in life is hard and circuitous.
* * * *
Refusal of Christian burial took on another
aspect in a N.S.W. cemetery the other day, when
a priest refused the “rites” to a corpse which
was represented at the grave by five mourners,
and a parson promptly stepped in, prayed over
the body, and, as a result, enlisted the five as con-
tributors to his collection-plate in future. Can
such an action be regarded as unfair clerical
competition ?
There is one place in Sydney where the
business of money-lending is carried on in an
equitable manner and where those in need of
financial accommodation can rely on being
decently treated. The sensible man who wishes
to get rid of his monetary worries at a minimum
of cost and trouble to himself goes to the offices of
the Australian Equitable Investment Co., Ltd.
The co. has a large capital for investment at low
interest, and is always open to advance money on
risks which the banks would decline; and acts
generally as a medium between the banks and
ordinary money-lenders. Men in business can
obtain assistance for their venture at a figure
which will leave them a fair profit for themselves.
The co. advances money to any extent on deeds of
land, bond warrants, merchandise, stock in trade,
scrip, interests under wills, furniture and all classes
of security. Press notices which have been pub-
lished from time to time express the favorable
opinion which generally exists regarding the co.
Mr. J. E. O’Brien is the Managing Director, and
the co.’s offices are at 45 Elizabeth-street, between
King and Hunter-streets.* < ..

At Flemington on Cup and Oak days the
lawn was alive with P.D. figures, gay dresses, and
lacy loveliness. There never was so much P.D.
corset gathered together at any previous Cup
meeting. And why not? Because the P.D.
increases its popularity every year. Its power to
make an ordinary waist look out-of-the-common,
and a “ good ” figure look better than the best, is
now one of the best known facts in Australia.*
