A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, November 12, 1901.
My Dear Arini,—
Our Cup wanderers fill their niches once more.
All whom I have met don’t look very joyous.
They went away full of the wine of expectation,
and have come home like returned empties.
Horses and weather cause more heartaches than
unrequited calf-love. Sydney-siders say that the
best-looking women at the Carnival were Mrs.
aV GC t\ Antill bride, and a daughter of
Mr. David Anderson. Victorian plebiscite on
the beauty question not yet to hand. By the way,
a Melb. young woman who had helped to fill
the Cup lawn since she wore Kate Greenaway
clothes, fled over here just before the last carnival,
and now gives her opinion of Sydney girls. They
are willow-waily, floppy, weary, pretty, and
tcrriolv given to eating in the afternoons. Their
slenderness is that of a young bamboo, and their
prettiness requires a lot of accessories. Govt,
ought to give every Sydney-side (fortuneless) girl
a good income to secure these accessories until
shes 27, when, if she’s not married, she ought to
be pole-axed, for another year or two sees her
grey at the temples and round the back of the
collar, and her hips and bust develop enormously,
her neck grows wrinkly, and she loses the bend in
her back. Also, she loses her appreciation of sun-
sets, and begins to see a primrose or flannel-flower
as such and nothing more. ?
Not many of us knew that tailoresses were so
numerous. Their striking strength is 850, and
the city is full of short-frocked, sturdy-looking
girls with collecting-boxes. One of them being
asked why they didn’t wait for the Arbitration
Bill to redress their wrongs, replied—“ The men
that made the Bill would be sure to be ‘daddied’
over by the bosses.” At the monster meeting in
the Domain last Sunday the executive of the
strike were raised to eminence on a lorry j the
speakers were most earnest, keen-faced young
men, but the huge Moreton Bay fig, under whose
shadow they orated, rustled louder than they
spoke. “So hot, my little sir,” it seemed to say.
The best place to hold a strike meeting is on an
ugly site amid bricks and mortar, the less nature
about the better. Great trees and sward make
the orator seem small and mean. There were
bigger crowds around the bands placing in the
rotunda.
After seeing Mrs. Roosevelt’s portrait, as pub-
lished by Sydney D.T., I am a quarter inclined
to think that the President’s consort does dress on
£6O a year. With a different neck, and a mane,
and a body with four legs, one at each corner, and
a tail, the picture would pass as that of a Cup-
starter. The lady has a painfully-intellectual
forehead—a desert of forehead—its monotony
unbroken by a wisp of curled hair. It’s the kind
of forehead that is backed up by utilitarian rather
than artistic brains. One of those penny caskets
of literature which come out in London had an
article, recently, entitled “Are clever people
ugly ?” And it triumphantly answered its ques-
tion in the affirmative, saying: “The higher we
rise in intellectuality, the lower we shall fall in
respect to beauty.” Also, “The reason why
women have hitherto been more beautiful than
inen is because they have been less educated.”
Crushing logic ! Yet there’s nothing like ignor-
ance for loosening the jaw and glassing the eye,
but you couldn’t get the penny-paper writer to
believe it. She seeks her type of beauty !«the
East, not the West, end of London.
Mdlle. Antonia Dolores, in a gown that took
the light like an opal, bronze, blue, and grey, and
coral necklace, has, at time of writing, given two
of the four promised concerts at the Town Hall.
Mdlle’s songs are, as usual, dainty and unhack-
neyed. Amongst those of the opening night were
Berlioz’s “Absence,” which, when Dolores sings
it, brings me a vision of seed-pearls, tears, and
moonlight. In Blumenthal’s “Serenade” one
hears to perfection that effect of which only a few
singers are capable—that of checking and toning
a strong, full note down to a very filament of
music. “Bid me Discourse” and “Chanson
Arabe,” with its wailing charm, were also listed.
For thirteen months Mdlle. Dolores, with only
such rests as she got on ship-board, has been sing-
ing. In W.A. she had capital houses and a two-
hours’ camel ride. Commenting upon Melba’s
voice, Mdlle. says it has notes that remind her of
our bell-bird. Of the cities she has visited, she
heap New York a’calling louder than even her
native Paris. Which recalls a storyette : Railing
from Chicago to N’York, Mdlle. and her
chaperone were assiduously waited upon by the
conductor, a big old negro, black as jet. At the
end of the journey he intimated, in answer to
their thanks, that it was a labor of love, “for
Mdlle. Dolores was the image of his sister, and he
would do anything in the world for a girl who
resembled her.” As the soprano is, in coloring, a
mezzo (English complexion, eyes of greyish hazel,
and dark-brown hair), the conductor must have,
indeed, seen her with eyes of fraternal love and
white faith.
When Mayor Graham and some of the Citizens’
Reception Committee went to the barracks to
request the company of Major-General French
and staff at the banquet to Hector Macdonald,
the military potentate who happened to be on
deck at the time said, “Let me glance at the
Army List, and see who is this man, Macdonald ! ”
He glanced, then : “ Ah, yes ! he’s here; but he
has merely the regimental standing of a colonel.”
At the present moment it appears to be uncertain
whether the Commandant and staff will attend
this dinner. The way of the —aw —“rankah” is
still hard in the British Armv.
Josephine Stanton co. scored a galloping success
at Sydney Criterion last week. “ Fra Diavolo ”
went well, and on Saturday night “ Wang” was
presented to a crowded house, dress-circle, stalls,
and gallery being full. “Wang” is a musical
extravaganza in a gorgeous Siamese setting, and
contains several pretty songs which give the vital
Miss Stanton great opportunities. A dainty little
melody about a running brook and a shady nook was
encored several times. Miss Stanton appears in a
boy’s white flannel tennis suit and a midshipman’s
cap, as Mataya, the Crown Prince of Siam, whose
uncle i 3 regent. Wang spends all his time dili-
gently dodging sundry awful-looking creditors
and seeking the hidden treasure of the late king.
The treasure having been tracked to the vicinity
of the widow of the French Consul, Wang makes
love to the lady in a most amazing fashion,
marries her, and staggers beneath the awful strain
of supporting her and her vast previous family. As
Wang, Mr. Geo. Kunkel is a pearl of a humorist.
His beautiful, red legs and long flowing coat,
to say nothing of his Mongolian moustache,
is an overwhelming demonstration that the
western sac suit is a horror, and the ordinary
dress-suit an outrage on manly beauty. But then,
it is not given to the ordinary male thing to have
such lovely flowing outlines on his basement
works, and Wang’s style of nether garment is
only to be worn by Greek statues and demi-gods.
Kilts aren’t a circumstance beside it. The only
other frocking (all the rest was Siamese, or much
abbreviated) was Miss Nilsson’s. One, a dairy-
maid costume with a pink hat, and the other a
white satin, with blush roses across it, moved a
male person close to me to such enthusiasm that
he promised the thin and faded little woman with
him “one just like it, to-morrow.”
Mr. Victor Leslie, of the Brough co., and Miss
Winnie Austin, who is with Hawtrey, are being
congratulated by friends on their engagement.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Spectacularly speaking, the Cup was ruined
by the wet morning. It was, in the end, a beauti-
ful day ;. but ere the clouds rolled by early birds
arrived in storm-plumage, to spoil the pageant
later on, when they took to the lawns and terraces
in company with human parrakeets, king-fishers
and regent-birds, spreading their plumes in the
sunshine. Lady Hopetoun, flinging off pink
riding-coat, lined with black, to display a Cup-
dress of white crepe, topped by toque of ivory
chiffon and pink cabbage-roses, brought along
‘ that little Charlie-thing, ’ her younger son, a frail
nine-year-old in top-hat and 'Chesterfield, with
complexion like porcelain, and a knack of uncon-
sciously modelling his gait on that of the biggest
man in sight at the moment. Stepping at dad’s
heels, the kiddie affects the pater’s willowy stoop
and cat-like, Lord-Chamberlain tread. If the
only jolly member of the attache-group heads the
party, the small-sized mimic solemnly pulls down
his cuffs, squares his elbows in front of him, and
strides along like a pocket bounder, off to knock
’em in the. Old Kent Road ! If everything the
atom does is patterned on the proceedings of his
elders, his lines are evidently laid amongst enthu-
siastic lady-killers. At the garden - party he
wasted few words on mere men, but hung all the
afternoon on the arm of a white muslinite, bandy-
ing words with her girl-chums, pouter-pigeoning
his little white vest, and altogether doing the
heavy. Of course the poor nipper may have been
cold-shouldered by his own sex at the outset of
the garden function. Judging from the brevity
of his interviews with ‘ Hon. This ’ and ‘ Major
That,’ one gathered that, with galaxies of nice
girls on hand, attachedom wasn’t taking any long-
eared little pitcher.
“ One lady stands out as clearly as an aspirate
at all shivoos for sheer untidiness of dress. Her
troubles about furbelows ! She gives one the idea
of slipping into a dress, securing its mainsails, and
then leaving it entirely to the hands of Providence.
Her hair grows unruly, her gloves burst their
buttons, her hat wobbles like George Reid’s
policy, from side to side, but she doesn’t care
a hang. She looked, all through the shifting
scenes of revelry, one of the happiest women
whirling in the smart set. The constant fixings
and pinnings-up and adjustments and repairs in
operation all round made her negligence all the
more a marvel.
“ There was no need to ask one Sydney woman
were there any more at home like her—she had a
chain to which depended all the family portraits
in medallion shape—seven in all. ‘ All her wares
in the window,’ said one rude fellow. ‘On our
Selection,’ suggested another. ‘Now, there you
do know where you are,’ commented a third.
“Advocates of temperance should have taken
up a stand in the refreshment-room on Cup-day,
and reviewed the squash there, where women
battled for their brandy-and-soda like Trojans,
and left the weaker vessels of male humanity to
drink tea with their relatives at the less crowded
counters.
“A very funny incident relieved the monotony
of the Oaks day. Once the Maddens’ box was
cleared for the tea interval. The Countess walk-
ing along the terrace with Miss Rawson, in order
to avoid the stare of the Stand-gapers, kept her
eyes on the tiles, and suddenly turned up the
steps of the Maddens’ box, where she sat down.
Her nervousness took time to dissipate, but
when at length she raised her eyes to view
tilings, she looked round, failed to recog-
nise the cloaks and fans about, searched
for the familiar gold-and-blue background, swal-
lowed down her annoyance, called Miss Rawson’s
attention to the fact they were on the wrong
roost, and stiffer than ever, and blushing furiously,
descended the inferior steps, and found her way
to her own attic. This time, her nervousness was
very much longer disappearing.
“ Aide Richard Nevill, over from S.A. with
the Tennysons, looked very sad when he drove off
with his employers, en route for the city of
churches. He wore a black-and-white tie, and
seemed to have retained his handy little ways of
humping his Principal’s shawls and wraps. Uncle
Tom Brassey, remember, was death on sou’-
westers, comforters, &c.
“Another S.A. identity noted at the festivities
was President Chaffey Baker’s wife, impressive in
bonnet dashed with red and handsome black
mantle. Mrs. Alec. Hay (N.S. W.), who saw the
Cup with the Maddens, looked a typical Syd-
neyite—pale, slim, and decorative, but too fragile
in pearl-grey and ivory-lace.
“ The handsome woman who won a pile on
‘ Revenue ’ queened it at the Oaks in one of the
most sumptuous costumes on view. It was the
favorite cream voile, made in a style that com-
bined classic drapery with rich oriental trimming.
The material was draped around the hips to a few
inches above the knee, where it fell out frillily.
A quantity of oriental applique was laid on the
bolero and pouched blouse.
“ The Oaks gathering was the brightest and
best equipped of the Carnival. One of the hand-
somely-dressed was Mrs. Henry Butler, who
sported one of her elaborate lace dresses and lace
ruffs she has just brought out from England.
“Steeplechase Day (Saturday) was the heel of
the Carnival, and a tired, dilapidated heel it was.
A scorching sun drew only the bravest and the
gamest. The lawn looked parched, the women
red and greasy. Lucky backers got over-excited,
and boiled over with sheer vulgarity. The unfor-
tunate ones drew long faces, and fanned them-
selves dejectedly, finding racing a delusion and a
snare.
“Mrs. Burdekin again kept to the fore of
frockers. She showed the curviest figure on the
Terrace, and her cream frock was braided down
and slashed across to accent the curve in at the
waist, and out again, and in again, the scroll being
the result of the new straight-fronted whalebone
shafts. Mrs. Alec Hay, who has sported
some stunning frocks at all the shows,
and Lady Clarice, shared the honors of the most
cynosured zig-zag forms divine. All the Federal
and Social Somebodies assembled for a strawberry
scramble in the vine avenue at Mrs. W. Leonard’s
invitation. The Maddens, Clarkes, Armytages,
and the rest of the swagger clique sat under the
shade of the Omar Khayyam tent and feasted
with relish. Later, the Waxellency’s girls led the
way into their own tea-sanctum, and took Lady
Downer (the Federal rose) and Dr. Finch-Noyes
in tow. These were the two most fussy adjourn-
ments for the cup that cheers. The Countess,
garbed in a cheap pink muslin, went off with her
aides for an ice to the retreat where the automatic
linnet sings, and returned to tour the bird-cage,
successfully steering clear of womankind.
“Guv’ment House garden party? Nothing
new. Same old faces, same old costly frocks;
same old silk hats, same old ice-creams, same
old tea. No, wrong there; the tea, straw-
berries and other eatables were the worst
of many a cheap Gruv’ment House treat. The
Maddens made an imposing exit from Govern-
ment House with the Hopetoun party, but
separated on the lawn, for fear the guests might
mob two sets of Y.R. luminaries or mistake one for
the other. The Countess wore heliotrope, another
markedly simple turn-out. The most extraordi-
nary figure of the House party was Lady Havelock,
the newly-arrived Tasmanian Governor’s wife ;
she has an air of mystery about her, and wore
white satin, topped with an old-fashioned grey
cloak, and a grey head-piece. The Guv’nor is a
medium-sized, kindly-faced, shrewd-looking Ex-
cellency, with a scrap of goatee beneath his
lower lip.
“An eye-sore at the garden-party gown-show
was a girl in so short an underskirt that an in-
terval of shin showed below her petticoat-hem,
through the costly transparent over-dress.
“At Govt. House, the Ormond Professor of
Music, escorting Mrs. Peterson, in red-and-white
gala rig, seemed more than ever mermanlike,
thanks to weird, long, narrow boots, which had
an uncanny air of no feet inside. The Greek
Patriarch, slim, pale, and youngish, trailed pic-
turesque black - and - purple vestments. Stout
Archbishop Carr, also robed and sashed, wore his
skirts with a difference. If he’d been anyone but
‘th’ Orchbishop,’ as his people call him, one
might have suspected him of regretfully ponder-
ing : ‘ they’ve all got a mate but me ! ’ as he
glanced from buxom brunette ‘ Mrs. Patriarch ’ to
his own single clerical shadow.
“She-guests enjoyed trailing their trains on
velvety Govt. House grass. English visitors
likened mothers and daughters parading their
draperies to a school of peacocks teaching imita-
tive broods to spread their tails in the sun. Same
critics extolled hostess Hopetoun, in simple
though elegant lilac gown and black hat, as an
oasis in a crowd chiefly intent upon topping pre-
vious record miles of bodice tucks, chiffon flounces
and bead necklets.
“Captain Wallington is generally told off to
coach Mrs. Hopie when the stranger, on a large
scale, is present within her gates. The good Man
of the House, unlike nervous wifie, can manage at
a pinch without Mentor. Rather good fun some-
times to see Wally mistaken for the Gov.-General,
and acting the part, too, when there’s no special
reason for oassing on the cup of boredom to his
principal. ‘Better-not,’ who believes in * impress-
ing ’ ’em, donchernow, unbends less than the real
‘ Mackay ’ would do, under the same circum-
stances. He keeps a stiff upper-lip, while the
blundering nobody looks up at the light, address-
ing him every minute as ‘ Your Excellency ; ’ but
he gazes down his nose and turns a deaf ear when
Mrs. Hopie, standing by, is referred to as ‘ your
good lady ’ or ‘ your noble help-meet.’
“The Senate President and Mrs. President’s
party in Parliament House gardens, on Monday
last, was a lively affair—so loud and lively that
the strains of a band could scarcely be heard above
some of the Federal bagpipes. The crowd had
streamed from the Queen’s Hall and circled on
the lawn, when their Exes suddenly appeared
round a corner, and were soon laughing,
bowing, and engaged in many significant
smiles with some person or persons on high.
Everyone strained a neck to see the for-
tunate. They saw him—the photographer of one
of the daily papers. The multitude gasped and
gave a disgusted snort. A camera-fiend to be
smiled upon in this fashion, while they, the pillars
of Sassiety, are apt to be frozen when they ven-
ture a remark over their handshake ! The ele-
phantine John Forrest jolted heavily round
the grounds in the company of some of the
prettiest girls in the crowd. His dame, how-
ever, was dowdy in black. Mrs. Senator
John Downer blossomed pinkishly in handsome
cream. A more extravagant air of courtesy than
Senator Downer’s was Senator Drake’s. He
almost genuflected to their Ex.’s. ‘Do look at
the Postmaster-General’s bob ! ’ exclaimed one
Federal light. ‘Yes,’ replied a neighbor, ‘he is
making a little duck ! ’ Admiral Beaumont, with
brushed-out whiskers and shining eye-glass, carried
his big frame round haughtily; and Lord
Tennyson, also eye-paned, beat him in a peacock-
strut by a neck.
“The Rupertswood dame made the sensation
of the day in smartness. Her cream voile gown
had rashers of lace and ribbon, and ruche frills
and brilliant buttons, and black tabs and gold
belting, and a ruff, brushed back like a
thick white shrub round her neck. Her head-
piece was a thing of chiffon and pink roses.
Lady Tennyson has a primitive idea of dress,
but bright, kittenish little ways that are a positive
relief beside the rigid stolidity of her silent
spouse. Her white embroidered muslin frock
showed arms of lobster hue —their condition
was not the result of one day’s scorching.
Our cool-looking Countess wore, to the
great grief of His Ex., who no doubt
wishes she would put on some style, an
impudently-plain knock-about frock of pink
muslin, almost innocent of a frill-kick or a
festive piping. She carried a stumpy parasol
with a knob-handle and a stout pink butt. The
G.G. hob-nobbed with everyone, and gave a
genial smile with a nod or hand-grip, potential in
its flattery. Wally slid from the side of duty
every now and then, and dipped his beak into
sundry ready ears. He’s such a sly dog.
“Women could scarcely get to the buffet 3
through the reefs of men struck with prodigious
thirst. The V.R. party, with that infinite
capacity for taking pains ascribed to genius,
spooned up ice creams in the swell kiosk, once the
property of the Upper House, now the resort of
the Senators. Lady Baker, who was beautiful
enough to figure in a thrilling romance, has the
soft, sweet, rather coquetting manner of the one-
time beauty. Her extremely eyeful aspect
(her eyes play like her smiles) makes one feel
hoarse and heavy. The President sticks to Apollo
collars and a severe air.
“Grace Palotta, tricked forth in ficelle voile,
many lace hurdles, and a Florodora hat, walked
the Cup-lawn with a fair, frail specimen of the
smoking sex obviously made-in-Germany. Of
other stage-lights Mrs. Bland Holt, with racing-
glasses and leather sachet slung over her shoulder
and her ears weighted with jewelled pendants,
was the most in evidence. Signor Salvi lost
heavily, and sat in sulky reverie under an elm in
the magic ring. Max Eugene lost, but smiled, hut
he had a charmer to cheer him. Maud Clietwynd
lost a handsome turquoise brooch at the Cup, and
Dolly Twinlda’s lip dropped—-like a rain-cloud—
ominously.
“Gov. Tennyson, at the garden-party, wore his
usual grey suitings, single eye-glass, and collar
low enough even for a poet’s child. He’s as baldi
as Gov. Madden, but, unlike that frilly potentate,
leaves his sparse back-hair to its fate, instead of
fluffing it out to the best advantage with
curling-tongs. Lady Tennyson’s Government.
House party - toilette was a scrimpy lilac
blouse with black skirt. A governess dressed
like that would be in request with employers
inclined to keep temptation out of the way of
flirtatious husbands or susceptible grown-up sons.
Adelaide, having no lack of dashing representa-
tives to the fore at the recent gala, could afford to
condone the chastened dress methods of their
amiable pro consuless. The spouse of Senator
Downer did her State credit throughout. Her
Cup and garden-party suits held their own with
the best, and the wearer looked so young and
blithe; also, her genial, humorous-looking hubby
seemed in great spirits. John Forrest, at the
party, had more the air of an elephant, and won a.
wifely sermonette from his liege lady when he
stamped heavily on her black flounces just as she
stepped forward to shake hands with dhe Hope-
touns.
“At the garden-party, Miss Madden, in white,
threaded with blue, monopolised one aide.
S’Rupert’s wife, looking very stagey in white
voile skirt, pale-blue Eton embossed with white,
and enormous ruffle, chained another, to her side.
Host Hopetoun and his bigger half sort of gave
the gathering to understand that they placed
themselves at the disposal of any guest anxious to
pump-handle a Governor-General, or pass the
time of day with a real live Countess. Those
who availed themselves of the privilege a parently
liked it so well that diplomacy had to be brought
to bear to make ’em let go.
“First night of the ‘Old Guard' (Saturday,
Nov. 9) the Gov.-Generaless took off her pink-
embroidered black cloth to show becoming white
satin corsage, cluster of pink roses, and her usual
simple coiffure of Grecian rear-coils, the side-locks
slightly puffed and held with small combs. Black-
gowned Miss Rawson looked older and more sub-
stantial than her hostess, and the Adrniraless,
also in black, got lots of laughter out of
Lauri’s absurdities. In the opposite box,
Mrs. Ernest Raleigh (nee St. Albans Wilson)
wore shimmering black and coiffure crest of pink
roses, almost as substantial in effect as a toque.
When Carrie Moore tripped forward as the
Buonapartist’s bride, a thrill and rustle ran
through the audience at the sight of her big
diamond-pendant. ‘ The star he gave her !’
whispered she-beholders, with twice the enthusi-
asm they would have shown had the bauble been
a medal for saving life, instead of a trophy of the
chased.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

Warner’s Safe Cure.
FEDERAL TARIFF PRICES.
The proprietors of Warner’s Safe Cure beg to
announce that, as they have a branch laboratory
in Australia, Warner’s Safe Remedies will not be
subject to the duty of 25 per cent., which will
have to be paid on all proprietary medicines
.imported into Australia.
The wholesale prices of Warner’s Safe Remedies
have been slightly advanced in New South Wales
so as to make prices uniform throughout Australia,
but this advance is so small that it will not be
necessary for the public to pay more than two
pence per bottle over and above the prices paid
before the Federal tariff came into force.
Warner’s Safe Cure is the only i eme ly known
to science which will unfailingly cure all diseases
of the kidneys and liver. The following diseases
are cured by Warner’s Safe Cure, because they all
all arise from the retention in the system of
urinary and biliary poisons which it is the province
of the kidneys and liver to expel: Rheumatism,
indigestion, gout, neuralgia, biliousness, lumbago,
sick headache, backache, sciatica, gravel, jaundice,
bladder troubles, blood disorders, anaemia, Bright’s
disease.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
It is curious that Governor-General Hope-
toun speaks with a slight English provincial
accent.
The money-testimonial to Dr. O’Haran
amounted to nearly £lO,OOO, which is not at all
bad just for being a Man Who Didn’t.
Sydney has had in succession two Uni-
versity graduates as Mayors—Matthew Harris
and Dr. Graham. And both are knights.
Archbishop O’Reilly (Adelaide) will be 55
on Tuesday next week ; Senator Henry Dobson
60 on Nov. 24; and Senator Tom Playford 64 on
Nov. 25.
Edward YII has passed his 60th year.
Having sampled his heir apparent, Australia
shouts from the bottom of its heart, “ Long Live
the King ! ”
Old-man Booth, of the Salvarmy, is said to
purpose another trip to Australasia. The won-
derful old battler for souls— and “beans”—is
nearly 80, and the last sea-voyage nearly killed
him.
King O’Malley so captivated the esteem of
a widow residing on the King River at Wan-
faratta (Vic.) that she called her pet rooster after
im. It was a rooster to be proud of—but
O’Malley winced!
Generally a man of any importance can
get the newspapers to print his name as he
wishes it. One distinguished visitor should try
and stop the circus-poster business of paragraph-
ing—“ General Sir Hector Macdonald (“ Fighting
Mac”) is booked,” &c.
To the long list of persons permitted to
read their own biographical notices is to be added
the alleged Australian authoress, Mrs. Campbell-
Praed. A cable indicating the demise of the
lady’s husband was mis-interpreted into an an-
nouncement of her death.
Obit. : James Agnevv, One of the fine old
men of Tasmania, aged 87. By profession a
medical man, the sturdy Scotsman took also an
active part in politics, and had been Premier of
the Garden Island. Tasmania is one of the best
places in the world to grow old ; in its placid air
life lengthens out and the twilight is long, Wit-
ness Agnew, Adye Douglas, and Braddon.

Kissy Ware, C.M.G., has been called to account

it be that the fatal kiss infected Mr. Ware with

Former N.S.W.* Chief-Justice Martin re-
ceived three princely gifts from the coffers of his
father-in-law : £20,000 was passed to his credit
when he married Miss Long, £20,000 when he
became Premier, and £20,000 when he became
Chief-Justice. And yet, in a sense, he was always
hard-up.
Judging from the conflicting versions
given, the members of N.S. W. Ministry are not
at all clear as to the exact sum drawn by Judge
Darley for his dual positions as Chief Justice and
Lieut.-Governor. The two salaries come to
£10,500, but it is understood that the recipient
returns a portion to the Treasury.
Bulletin expects to see “ old brass for
sale” on the door of a residence in Liverpool-st.,
Sydney. Once the door-plate was “ Dr. Graham,”
then it was “Sir James Graham,” now it will be
“Sir James Graham, K.C.M.G.”
Australian daily-paper editors are not
usually millionaires. One of the brightest and
best of them (he is dead now) told present writer
not very long ago that his salary—his total
income—was seven pounds a week.
As far as The Bulletin knows, the only
member of an Australian Legislature who has
never visited his electorate, is Mitchell, M.P., for
the Northern Territory. For that reason, possibly,
he fills his speeches about it with poetry.
Dr. Mcßurney, one of the experts who
fought Death for President M‘Kinley, is famous
in the medical profession as the discoverer of
“ Mcßurney’s point ” —the point on the body
which is bound to be painful in a case of
appendicitis.
The long, grizzly arm of coincidence :
When Samuel Bennett, of Sydney Evening News,
died, the editor of the paper followed him within
a week. Editor Dekker was just a week behind
Frank Bennett. All four deaths took place on a
Sunday—the journalist’s dies non.
Archbishop Kelly, Coadjutor to Cardinal
Moran, seemed, in an opening address to his
flock, to be most of all anxious to impress them
that he had to come because the Pope said so. If
rumor speaks correctly, the Cardinal is about as
pleased at his Coadjutor’s arrival (by order of the
Pope) as a man at the visit of his mother-in-law.

land Edition was lately reprinted Wells’s “ Star.”
I wish to point out that the idea was anticipated
by me in “Finis,” which appeared in Adelaide
Observer during first half of ’B4. Ido not chal-
lenge comparison with Wells’s treatment, but do
claim priority of idea. Of course Wells never
heard of “Finis,” but should I ever reprint I will
append date.
N.S.W. Leg. Council, by deaths, resigna-

S. H. Hyam, good fellow, cattle-breeder, all-
round sport, and intermittent legislator, is the
last to go, after long enduring an agonising in-
ternal trouble. The fortune left by Mr. Hyam
would be much more substantial
could his executors recover the
thousands he “lent” to importu-
nate fellow-legislators.

M.L. Judge Cooper and ex-Judge Marlin
(of Maoriland amorous celebrity) have completely
swopped places- Cooper took Martin’s place on
the Bench, and Martin has just joined Cooper’s
old partner, Devore.
Recently dead, aged 90, Captain John
Blackbourn, the first piermaster at Williamstown
(Vic.), and the oldest shipmaster in Australia.
He was in the Australian trade for 64 years. In
’49 he sailed a 570-ton barque, then the largest
Australian-built vessel, from Tasmania to Eng-
land and back ; and at the break-out of the Turon
diggings, N.S.W., in the early Fifties, he brought
the first contingent of miners from California to
Sydney.
Vic. Premier Peacock began life as as-
sistant in a grocer’s store on Simpson’s-road, Mel-
bourne. He rose from that to the secretaryship
of several mines at Creswick. Paying many
miners their wages on Saturdays, he parted with
the dross in such a genial way that they looked
upon him as their personal benefactor. So, when
a vacancy occurred in the Parliamentary repre-
sentation of the district, he easily secured it,
having by this time become legal manager in place
of his employer, deceased.
The will of the late Thomas Hussey Kelly
(“Tin Kelly”), having been sworn at over
£250,000, yields to N.S. W. Treasury probate-duty
exceeding £25,000. Than the deceased there lived
no better-hearted man; and his beneficence was
most unostentatious. The N.S.W. professional
Freetraders covetously eyed the old man’s money
and sought to draw him into the political swim ;
but the successful metal-man, who had a horror of
the limelight, failed to respond. From the time
Burdekin started to fade Sydney Freetrade sorely
needed a partisan with a long purse.
“Sam” M'Caughey, M.L.C., Australia’s
most enterprising and possibly her most pros-
perous squatter, is completing a palatial residence
on his recently-acquired property—North Yanko.
Speculation is ripe as to what it means, for
bachelor “ Sam ” has a really beautiful home at
Coonong, near Narandera. Perhaps it’s an
asylum for some of his ancient aristocratic “ Ver-
monts.” The first owners of North Yanko—the
M‘Neils —lived in a bark “humpy” for years;
the property then went to the Douglas family, one
of whom represented the Mumimbidgee for a
term in Parliament. And now the earth-grabbing
“Sam ” reigns in their stead.
“Melb.”: Speaker Holder has taken to
preaching. I have heard him twice in the pulpit
here. He is not a good preacher. This may be
said with the more freedom because he is an ex-
cellent Speaker, and yet such a financier that, to
my idea, he ought to have been Treasurer. His
preaching is the most absurd Evangelicalism, not
absurd in itself, but through the'contrast between
his preaching and his practice, in regard
to the reckless Gospel views he enunciates.
Besides this, his matter is poor, and utterly con-
ventional ; and his barren style—in the pulpit—
supplies no reason for his being there, except
because he is the Hon. the Speaker, and can draw
bawbees for other persons.
Li Hung Chang is dead, and another of
the past century’s history-makers has gone. And,
of his kind, East or West, none were greater than
he. The story of his life is without parallel in
other lands ; he was not more full of years than of
guile; he has been honored and disgraced
more times than most men can count in
years; his head has been forfeit times enough to
make the ordinary man to lose it actually in very
fear at last; and now dead. Few will be more
sincerely mourned than he, and none will leave a
greater void behind. In his tour of the West in
1896-7, he let nothing pass, and gave forth some
memorable readings of men and things. Of the late
Queen Vic. he said : “ She is a good woman, great
within her limits ; but her limits are very narrow,
she being iron-bound of sex.” Asked his opinion
of Gladstone, and Salisbury: “ Gladstone says
much and means little ; Salisbury says little and
means much; neither does much, being weak. ” All
of which, when you come to think of it, was
tolerably true.

When Dr. SelvTyn,
the first Bishop of
Maoriland, was on his
way from .England to
take charge of his dio-
cese, he had a disagree-
merit with the captain of
the ship, who informed
him that if he said anything
objectionable he would put him in
irons. On the arrival of the vessel at
the M.L, port of debarkation, the bishop
met the skipper on the pier, and, reminding
nim that lie was no longer under his authority,
threw off his coat, engaged the mariner in a
stand-up fight, and administered to him a sound
thrashing. Then the pair shook hands and were
good friends ever afterwards.
Austral Light (R.C., Melb.), for Nov.,
publishes some notes of an interview which one
Craubourne Philpotts, now apparently in Aus-
tralia, declares that he had with the present
German Emperor, not long ago, at the Paris Cafe
des Afcibassadeurs. This is out-of-the-way matter
for a little Australian religious paper to get hold
of. There is room for much difference of opinion
as to whether the Emperor has been in Paris since
he came to the throne.
“Miller” Darling, M.P., the strongest
force in S. A. Toryism, has urged that as the Leg.
Council represents contributors of direct taxation,
and safeguards the interests of the people, it
would be well, now Federation has been es-
tablished, to cheapen the cost of the legislature by
abolishing the Assembly ! “ Payers of customs
duties only,” Darling remarks, “should be con-
tented with the Commonwealth Parliament, for
which every adult has a vote.”
That recent. Melbourne proposal that the
Wages Board should be composed solely of “men
of commercial experience” is very thin. The
“man with commercial experience ” is no different
from any other man, except that he spends most
of his time driving a hard bargain and reaching
out for another 5 per cent, profit. Of course, the
idea is that “men of commercial experience”
would all be drawn from the employing class, and
that it would be a case of the pigeons referring the
question of whether they should be plucked or not
to a court of hawks.
London Morning Post thinks that the
division to abolish the Kanaka will lead to the
same trouble here as between North and South in
the U.S., on the slave question. The Morning
Post is an authority on duchesses’ tea-parties ;
about Australia it evidently knows nothing.
Outside Queensland, Australian public opinion is
unanimous that the Kanaka must go ; in Queens-
land itself the imported black man’s retention is
supported only by a minority so small that it
could never hope to enforce its views even if
merely confronted by the local abolitionists, apart
from the forces of United Australia. What the
Morning Post should have said is that the
Kanaka question, if not decisively settled now,
would probably lead to a civil war. White
Australia clearly sees this, and is resolved to cut
out the nigger-traffic root and branch.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. — ln the case of ethnological and
natural-history items , etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
About Mickey. Mickey was the school’s
best reciter, and even our “ member” prophesied
a great future for him. The action and expression
which he learnt as a reciter stood to him in need.
One day his old man sent him to a farm about
four miles off to bring home a bull. On the way
home, Mickey thought he would give the old
piebald a lesson in jumping. A cockatoo-
fence ran along one side of the road, so
he turned his charger and drove home the
•purs. The moke, not being used to this,
gave a bit of a pig-jump, and landed on one
of the shore-legs of the fence, which penetrated
its chest some half-dozen inches. Mickey led the
mare home, and told his old man how the ferocious
bull charged him time after time, and gored the
mare, while he only escaped with his life by
climbing a tree. To hear him talk of this adven-
ture would cause you to dream of bulls chasing
you. Mickey was told to get the gun and go back
and shoot the bull, which he did with great
enthusiasm. The moke was put in the high-fenced
slaughter-yard, to be away from the other horses
while being doctored. Mickey’s grandmother had
climbed to the top of the fence to have a look at
the mare, when she fell and broke her arm, and
fainted. Mickey ran to the old man and told him
that the mare rushed the old lady and kicked her
through the fence. Granny did not know how it
happened. The gun was again ordered, and
Mickey shot the moke. “By hell! ” said Mickey,
afterwards, “I was wishing the old woman would
do something, so that I would have to shoot her ! ”
He would have done it, too. Mickey is now a
J.P., an alderman, and a member of the show
committee, so the prophecy of “our member”
oame true.

IN SYDNEY.—A SUNDAY ALTERNATIVE.
Old Gentleman (to street-arab) : “Gimme a
paper!”
THE ABAB: “ Which'll y'have, sir—two pennorth
o' weakness or two pennorth o' wickedness? ”
“ Yarrum ” : Riding over a bridge, t’other
day, I saw an excited man diving intotheriver. He
had a shirt round his middle, and seemed terribly in
earnest. Asked him what he was after. “Lars’
Sunday,” said he, “I was crossin’ the bridge
here with me twenty-guinea ’ammerless gun,
when I spotted a bobby cornin’. Didn’t want to
be copped for shootin’ on a Sunday, so I slung ’er
into the river.” When the bobby came up, he
said: What’s that yer threw over ? Told ’im a
stick. ‘Looked very like a gun,’ ’e said, an’ rode
on. I slung ’er as near the bank as I could, so’s I
could dive and recover ’er, and I’ve been divin’
ever since. She’s a beauty—a real daisy,
anomistake ! ” Then he dived again, and came
up exhausted. “Can’t fetch bottom,” he said.
Ain’t got the wind I ’ad 20 year ago. Asked him
to lend me the shirt, and I’d try. I peeled off,
and took a header. Came back with a handful of
weeds and a couple of jam-tins. Down I went
again, and came up with a knotty blue-gum stick,
worn smooth with handling. “ That’s er ! ” cried
he. “ That’s ’er ! Aint she a beauty ? Kill an y-
thin’ comes in range! ” Then hugging the thing
closely, he sat on the bank, and wept for joy. I
kicked him in the back, donned my garments,
and left. A mile further on, I met a policeman
who inquired if I had come across a man behaving
strangely. He was an escaped lunatic.

“ Karch ” : Re “ footing it ” (“ Vril,” B.
8/6/’01). The tramp he records was a good per-
formance for a party, and wonderful for a man of
78 ; but I know of a better. An old ex-soldier,
named Michael Peyton, was for some time men’s
cook at Bowman Bros. ’ station, near Deloraine,
Tas. Michael got stranded down in Hobart once,
his ticket for free Govt, lodgings having given
out; so he pawned his boots for a tot of rum,
started to walk home barefoot, and did the 140
miles in 10 days. No warm heart beat for Michael
throughout that awful macadamised journey ; but
at Deloraine a storekeeper gave him a pair of
boots and a feed. Michael cheerfully got the
latter into his stomach and the former on his feet,
and tramped 10 miles further to Bowman’s. The
old chap was 73. He had been through the
Crimean war, and told the writer that during that
time he had for three weeks gone with grass tied
round his feet, and had nothing but “living”
biscuits. Writer himself once tramped from Comet
mine, Tas. West Coast, to Both well, 150 miles of
difficult going, in five days, with a 301 b. swag.

When the item in N.S.W. Estimates for
an Aboriginal Home at Grafton was under dis-
cussion, Sleath, M.P., said the Clarence was so
closely settled by whites that there was no room
for blacks. Much Sleath knows about the Clar-
ence 1 There’s country there which shelters big
herds of brumbies and wild cattle that the cham-
pion station riders can’t capture. The river banks
alone are closely settled, and only as far as the
Orara, a little above Grafton. From old Yulgil-
bah Castle to Tabulam, and beyond to the border
mountains, is all virgin bush —as rough and wild
and thickly timbered as any in Australia, and
likely to remain so for years to come—which
would be a veritable paradise to the aborigine.
“Prospector”: A case of economy of
time. Here at Oroville (Great Barrier) mining-
camp settlers bring fruit (strawberries, etc.)
from farms about 10 miles away, generally
getting here about 11 a.m. Lately one
rolled up at 8 a.m. Asked why he was
early, he explained : ‘ ‘ Oh, me brother
died at 2 this mornin’, so we had to
get up, and dad said we might as
well start pickin’ strawberries, as it
looked like rain.”

“A.P.R.”: Marvellous
how far a stone can be propelled
by tying it to the fall of a bul-
lock-whip and cracking same.
Writer once thus hit a China-
man 150yds. away. The
Chink spent 10 minutes
examining the drains along-
side the road, and looking
behind trees, but it remains
a mystery to him to this day
what struck him.
“ R. 8.” : Re luck of
druuks. Was stoking in a
gasworks. Steam began to
rise, so I went round and
fixed the engine, and then
started looking for the driver.
Knew he had a few drinks in and
would be asleep somewhere.
Looked all over the show and
couldn’t find him, until, when
passing the fly-wheel, half of
which ran in a pit, I caught
sight of a boot sticking out.
There he was—fast asleep, the
join in the driving-belt just touch-
ing him as it went round. It must
have dragged him in, but he didn’t
remember anything about it.
In the Senate, last week, Senator Sargood
said he had seen women ploughing in S. Australia.
Senator Playford hotly doubted this, but Sargood
added that, in Playford’s State, he once saw a
horse pulling on one side of a plough and a
woman on the other. Senator Pearce deposed
that he also had seen S. A. women ploughing. So
has present scribe, often. The last occasion was
near Mt. Gambier. The plough-lady was attired
mainly in a very short petticoat and Wellington
boots, and her husband, a fat German gentleman,
sat on a fence near by complacently smoking.

“Bob R.” : Re “Wallaroo’s” heavy-
booted female (B. 26/10/’01). There’s a woman
who has a clearing contract on a station near
Temora, and is doing well at it. Her husband is
with her, but she says she can “run rings round
him.” She pegs away the whole week, wet or
dry, and turns out on Sunday spick-and-span.
She told me that she could not live in a house-
housework would drive her “barmy.” Must have
out-door graft.

“ M.K.” : Re that farmer’s lift of a bag
of wheat to top of load (“Bilby,” 8., 26/10/’01).
I have done the same thing myself, and I weigh
but list. It is more knack than strength. Have
loaded 20 bags of wheat upon a waggon from the
ground without help. Wonderful examples of
streng h can be found outside the ranks of pro-
fessional “Samsons.” At Beulah (Vic.) lives an
18st. German, who; when he wants to get the rear
of his pole-waggon against the railway-platform,
drives close up, turns the waggon upon the
lock, and then lifts the rear carriage of
the waggon round with apparent ease. At

“Jaf.” : That feat of lifting a bag of
wheat to top of load (“Bilby,” 8., 26/10/’01) is not
uncommon in farming districts. There is, how-
ever, a farmer at Berry Jerry (N.S.W.) who can
lift from the ground, one under each arm, two
2401 b. bags of wheat and carry them 80 or 100yds.
with ease. Scores of local residents can verify
this. I also knew a farmer who could carry three
coils of wire, lcwt. each, from one straining-post
to another —100yds. or thereabouts. One coil
would be round his neck, and the other two on his
arms.

with 4cwt. of wire up—a lcwt. coil under eacli
arm and two round his neck. This you can take
from me, because my money was on the metal.

“Thomed” : Re stampeding of cattle :
It is well known that cattle will not camp on old native
burial-grounds, and it must be apparent to those ac-
quainted with the bush, that animals possess the power
of psychic seeing. The investigations of Prof. Crookes
(radiometer man and real discoverer of the X Rays), and
other eminent scientists in the realm of spiritism, have
proved, beyond a doubt [to some people—Ed. B.] the con-
tinuity of life after the death of the physical body. Ac-
cording to Crookes, the vibrations of light tend to disinte-
grate the “ etheric ” body, hence the necessity of dark-
ness for manifestations. In his “ Where the dead men
lie” Boake merely described what he really saw. The
old idea of waiting some feet underground for the
last trump is getting played out. Cremation —apart
from its sanitary advantages—has always been practised
in India as the only means of freeing the “etheric” body
from its physical counterpart.

“ Jack-a-Clubs ” writes :
This may throw light on recent Bulletin pars re stock
stampeding at certain places. About six miles from
Yeppoon.on the old road leading to Rockhampton, there
once was a slime moving slowly across a short-cut bridle-
track. It appeared to the casual rider-by to be the tail-
end of a gully. But when a horse’s foot steps in, there is
a mad jump across the remainder, and a struggle for
ownership between man and beast. Once I dismounted
and put my finger in the slime. Nothing happened. I
rubbed some on my horse’s neck, and instantly he pawed
me. Another time I left my horse tied to a sapling, and
tried to discover the source of this “ animal slime-devil,”
but gave up the search after following the slime for about
three miles, without finding a growing stream. Came back
and found my horse gone ; a broken bridle and a patch of
slime where he stood told the tale. For curiosity I sat
down and saw—first, two cows with calves, then a stock-
man leading a pack; and, lastly, three “swells” from
Rockhampton riding for the sea-side. One calf stumbled,
got up in a hurry, and limped off. The stockman stuck
to the saddle, but his “ pack ” lost its load. Two of the
three “ swells ” buried their beautiful white helmets in
the dust. Although the recent drought dried the slime
up, still the place has the same influence on animals.

“ Gowrie ” writes
Queensland Hansard reports the examination of Arch.
Meston re aboriginal kidnapping. He denied that “ boys ”
were ever forcibly removed from their camps. Writer
knew a man in a back-block township who, to please a
big official, did a bit of kidnapping publicly. He rode
out to the camp, brushed the father aside with a revolver,
and annexed the pickaninny from the gin in the gunyah.
The gin, who was lying on the youngster, bit and clawed
like a tigress. The kidnapper threw a handful of silver
and coppers, with a pound of tobacco, to the old man,
who, with a pitiful grin, had sworn that the “ little
pheller ” was dead and buried long ago. Old grey pate
danced in a rage on the coppers and tobacco ; but the
white man smiled and brought in his prize. By the way,
Meston informed the House that he can supply the place
of Tommy Tanna in the canefields with his old friends,
the aborigines!

netting as regards bunny. At
the big Government-tanks near

places on the stock-route, near

amongst the wheat on t’other

“Yarrum”: After all, bringing the
Kanaka to Queensland is rather like taking coal
to Newcastle. Why don’t Queensland sugar-
growers, if they must have black labor, round-up
what darkies are left in Australia ? The different
States could well spare them, as it costs them
some thousands yearly to keep ’em in tucker,
blankets, railway-passes, and religion. Queens-
land, by nourishing, and otherwise looking after
its black brudder, could breed enough labor to cut
its cane for hundreds of years to come. It would
be just as cheap as Tanna, and not nearly so
objectionable. It is all bunkum to say our darkies
can’t graft. I’ve seen ’em, in N.S.W. quarries,
knock white men under. Feed your darkie well—
not too much, as he doesn’t know when to stop—and
he’ll work all right. Hitherto, the Queensland
nigger has only been used for show purposes, and,
far as writer has sden, he is a dismal failure at the
game.—[Protector-of-aborigines Meston suggests
the utilisation of aboriginal labor in the canefield,
but it’s better in the interests of Australia to keep
them where they are.—Ed. B,]

“Bob R.” : A few of the P.U. slieds,
which are working with full boards, were fortunate
in possessing astute managers. When the men
refused to sign the P.U. agreement the manager
quietly interviewed the ringleader, and a “tenner”
or two soon altered his opinion as regards
union rules, and with him went in most of the
men. This accounts for the presence in P.U.
sheds of “ big-gun ” shearers who had sworn un-
dying loyalty to the Workers’ Union !

“Shaker” : Re that pack horse-drover’s
cook (“ R. 8.”, B. 21/9/’01). Sorensen knew what
he was talking about. “R. 8.” confuses pack-
horse-drovers with packers. Di’overs of small
mobs of cattle often carry their swags and tucker
on packhorses, which the cook packs, and drives
just as he would the cart, if there were one. Even
if there is no regular cook, the man who packs-up,
shifts camp, and boils the billy is called the cook.

“ Wurrym ” : A dozen
years ago a party of men were
standing on the verandah of the
head station on Auburn, Upper
Burnett (Q.). Three hundred
yards away,' on a stump, sat a
crow. A Winchester stood
handy, and one of the party
fired at Satan. He flew off un-
harmed as everyone anticipated,
but something they did not
dream of happened. Five or six
hundred yards off, far away to
the right, a horse dropped,
struggled, and lay still. At the
same moment the man bringing
up the milkers —he was away to
the left—waving his arm wildly,
galloped through his mob and
up to the house. What sort of
a gory joke was it to fire at a
cerulean man, was what he
wanted to know. He’d have
bis money in the morning, and
so on. All hands went to the
stump to see if the bullet was
embedded there, and thus con-
vince the wrathful cow-man.
The bullet wasn’t there, but in
the top of the stump was stuck
a shear blade which plainly
showed where the bullet had
s' ruck it. The fallen horse was
then visited. It was helpless,
with a wound through the spine,
j ust back of the wither. Another
shot finished it, and then from
the wither was cut half a bullet.
It was the other half that had
grazed the cow-man. There
was a parson in the crowd. He
registered a vow never to tell
of that occurrence lest his re-
putation should suffer.

“Copa” tells about a
bung-boom-township:—
Somewhere back in the ’Bo’s, at the junction of four
roads in one of the wildest parts of Gippsland, there
existed a little township named Thorpdale. In due tune
the land-fever spread itself over the State. The gentle
speculator arrived at Thorpdale and bought up a few
h undred acres of virgin soil in the neighborhood. Next
the Railway Commissioners considerately built a line
from Moe in the direction of the peaceful hamlet, and
d imped the terminus down on the guileless boomster’s
property, about two miles from the nearest road, and
from the township. The line was the most expensive per
mile ever built in Victoria. Following the course of the
Narracan Creek, which it crossed 18 times in 11 miles,
it consisted almost entirely of bridges, cutting's and
embankments. New roads had to be cleared to the
station, a new town-site laid out, and new public and
private buildings erected. In fact, Thorpdale had prac-
tically to be shifted. The boomster cut up his land into
small' blocks and sold them at fancy prices to the local
innocents. The Railway Department provided extensive
storage accommodation for all local products, and Thorp-
dale loomed large on the horizon as a centre of great
potentialities. Then the boom exploded with a bang. A
nastv gloom hung over the district, broken only by the
shrill shriek of the departmental locomotive as it charged
into the station with its burden of empty carriages.
Among other buildings erected in the good days was a
commodious cool-stcrage chamber, costing £2OO or £3OO.
Finding this little used, except to shelter an occasional
(verv occasional) passenger it did that well enough the
Department sold it for the magnificent sum of £9. Then
it built a decrepit-looking shed of galvanized iron and
woodatacost of £l5 for the occasional passenger toslielter
in instead. A truly great institution is the Victorian
Railway Department.
About the safest place in a thunderstorm
would seem to be the bowels of the earth, but
three men sinking the Long Tunnel shaft at M al-
halla (Vic.) were badly knocked about during a
storm by lightning entering the tunnel, playing
about the plat-sheet, and then running down the
shaft by the steel rope.
There was in San Francisco, years ago, a
Hebrew named Leon Moses, who became a Wes-
leyan parson, and the local Rabbi used to ask as a
riddle: “ Where was Moses when his light went
out?” The answer was, of course —“In the
Wesleyan church.”
Yankee jock Ames, now in Sydney, tells how
the so-called Tod Sloan style was extensively practised in
America long“ before Tod was heard of. It consists of
using short stirrup-leathers and placing the weight on the
shoulders, where it can be borne easiest, so that the
prod runs more freely and can be kept together better.
The seat on the back is more comfortable for the rider,
but the success of the new style all over the world seems
to indicate that it is best for the horse. Leading Austra-
lian jockeys, Kuhn, Turner, Barden and co., all adopt
some modification of it, which may account for then
success.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice ivill be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any State) accompany.
B.D. : If Australia were invaded by a decent-
sized army, it would be a moral certainty that the big,
practically-undefended cities, would fall into the hands of
the enemy. Would you then advise Australians to cave
in, or to hang on the rest of their country and fight the
matter out to the bitter end ? Would you be prouder of
them if they “ listened to reason,” and accepted the rule
of the brigand invader than if they comported themselves
like bull-pups? Tell us—we’d like to hear. So far, the
Boers seem to have only definitely lost the three big towns
of their territory ; the rest of it, and a large share of Cape
Colony, is as much in their hands as in anybody’s
Love is Blind: Story leaves a horrible taste in the
mouth Delphis : A very trite idea. Ever since the
first gate was built some woman has been waiting beside
it for the man who never came Wurrym :It is truly
wonderful how you can get 20,000 or 200,000 reformers to
enthuse over a new voting system which, after all, will
leave things much as they were, and you can’t get two to
vote for freeing this continent from its abject servitude
to the British money-lender, making it self-dependent
and self-reliant. What a readiness there is to tackle the
reform that requires no money and no big effort!

sidering “ 8.L.”.. ..Jack Clubs: Lie back for a year (or

The New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
00., Limited, report .—Wheat: Transactions during the
week have been few and small, and in anticipation of the
arrival of the new crops buyers are not disposed to
operate beyond actual immediate requirements. Our
representative in the Riverina district reports that the
crops there are in splendid condition, and it is expected
the yield this season will be a heavy one. To-day’s value
here of prime grain is 2s. 10£d., but the feeling is towards
lower levels, and no doubt when the new crop com-
mences to arrive a decline in prices will take place.*
Gay sports who didn’t take down Ikey Mo.
at Flemington last week were deprived of none of
the joy of life so long as they took their D.C.L.
whisky in moderation. After an appeal to that
health-restoring beverage there is no difference
between the glowing feelings of the man who has
just won and the man who has just lost. D.C.L.
■spells fortitude, cheerfulness, hope, and wisdom
Anyway, it is the whisky of the wise.*
Are Opals Unlucky?
The good or bad luck pertaining to thav
beautiful stone —the opal—has furnished the
Eapers with many items of interest, but pro-
ably few ladies would decline a gift of jewel-
lery set with this iridescent gem.
When the writer was on the White Cliffs
opal fields he found the man adjudged the
most lucky there was he who owned the largest
quantity of this stone. But on further inquiry
he brought to light it remarkable but true story
of the persistent ill-luck which for years attended
a lady resident —a JkL’s. Jane Wigney. ‘’When I
was seventeen,” she exclaimed, “ I used to go out
sewing, and owing to wet weather I got my feet
damp and caught a severe cold. From that time
until lately, I seemed the unluckiest mortal alive,
for my health began to fail, and I suffered from
headaches, terrible pains at the pit of my stomach,
under my shoulder blades, and across the small
of my back. Food became tasteless, and I had
to force myself to eat. My blood grew impov-
erished and my nerves unstrung. Then the
sharp burning pains of sciatica came in my legs
and thighs. Despite medical treatment I got
worse, and pneumonia set in. One doctor said I
had chronic indigestion, another said my liver
was out of order, and still another told me my
lungs were affected, but their prescriptions only
gave me partial and temporary relief and the
patent medicines I afterwards tried were also
useless. My husband being impressed by the
character of the people who had testified
to their cure by Dr. Wiliams’ pink pills, per-
suaded me to take them. I was pleasurably
surprised when, after using them for a fortnight,
I felt improved. As I continued taking them
my blood gradually became enriched and I
obtained sound, refreshing sleep. My nerves
were steadied and I relished my food. lam now
strong and well through the use of Dr. Williams’
pink pills, which cured me after medical treat-
ment and patent medicines had failed.”
There is no element of luck or chance in using
Dr. Williams’ pink pills; they cannot fail to do
good because they enrich the blood, tone up the
nerves, and so remove ailments such as aneemia,
debility, nervous exhaustion, neuralgia, insomnia,
skin diseases, indigestion, liver and kidney
troubles, dysentery, rheumatism, consumption of
the bowels and lungs, paralysis, etc. From
chemists and storekeepers and the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co., Queen’s Place. Sydney, two and
nine per box, six boxes fifteen and three, post free.
These pills regulate health, and ladies will find
that graceful development and a complexion of
roses and lilies follow their use.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Lee is expected to retire from the leader-
ship of N.S.W. Opposition inside of a month.
Carruthers climbs up.
When Kruger said that “the cost of sub-
jugating his country would stagger humanity,”
the Jingoes laughed derisively. And now !
An all-Australian stamp will be valuable,
even if only as a sentimental suggestion of unity.
Until its arrival, all State stamps should be inter-
changeable.
In the critical division on the Kanaka Bill
in committee of the House of Reps., the colored
man had only four friends, one from Queensland,
two from Victoria and one from S.A.
Already the Barton tariff begins to work,
increases in wages having been secured (amongst
others) by glass-workers and tailoresses in Syd-
ney, largely because of the Protection afforded to
the manufacturers by the new duties.
The assassin-anarchist is the male counter-
part of the outrage-story female—a being whom
the dulness of life has driven across the borders of
sense, and who is mad for a riot of excitement at
any cost. The sensational newspapers are mainly
responsible for his crimes, and supply the prompt-
ing to kill by shrieksome diatribes against politi-
cal opponents.
THE REAL ANARCHIST.
“ The murderer of President McKinley was electrocuted to-day at New York." —CABLE.
A point for the White Australian party
in the Senate. If the white man can’t stand
the stress of climate in the open fields when
cutting cane in N.Q., how does he manage to
stow cargo into the stifling lower holds of vessels
at Cairns, Cooktown, Port Douglas and Towns-
ville wharves ? Longshore Jack tucks the stuff
away in an atmosphere more humid and trying
than that of any canefield—does it right through
the season and comes again for more. Why ?
Because lie gets a living-wage.
“ I cannot stand a wobbler.”—G. H. Reid
in the Federal Parliament.
His grave is made, his doom is sealed—
The arrogant, but luckless elf
Who has unconsciously revealed
Himself unto himself !
Maoriland has begun reprisals, in response
to the Commonwealth tariff, with an export tax
on undressed timber, which will raise the price of
white pine to Australian butter-box makers.
Since the avowed object of the tax is to keep the
work of cutting the timber in the country, no one
on this side can growl at its imposition.
Trenwith definitely left the Radical camp
when he recorded his vote in Vic. Assembly for
the reduction of the old-age-pension rate to 7s. per
week. The one-time shoemaker has for long had
little of his own opinions left; now you can only
recognise the ex-Labor advocate in the slight
awkwardness with which he wears his belltopper.
The consolidation of the N.S.W. statutes
will cost a huge sum. Mr. C. G. Heydon under-
took the work as a labor of love, asking only the
assistance of one draughtsman. The “one
draughtsman ” has increased to ten, and the cost
so far has exceeded £14,000. Now an additional
£2OOO is on the estimates. But the junior Bar
must live.
Rep. Thomas, who created a slight
diversion in the last days of the tariff debate by
his direct-tax amendment, and upon whom the
Argus used several pounds of buttered linotype,
is the same Thomas whom the same Argus de-
nounced with others in ’92 as madmen and
murderers. But Thomas was then a Broken Hill
strike-leader.
A peculiar combination has lately been
entered into between opposition leader Darling
and the most extreme conservatives, and the
labor members of S. A. Parliament. The result
is that the moderate men have been concentrated
round the Govt, standard, and Premier Jenkins
smiles blandly when condemned by the Tories or
reviled by the Radicals.
David Storey has brought in a private Bill
to reduce N.S.W. Assembly to 78 members. As
a private Bill, the measure has no chance of pass-
ing, and for that reason it will probably be enthu-
siastically approved of in its earlier stages. Most
members are anxious to pose as in favor of a
reduction of the House in numbers ; and, even
though their own seats are safe, still more
anxious to prevent any reduction, on the general
principle that an insecticide is bad for the flea
race generally.
THE KID AND HIS
CAMERA AT THE
RACES.
Printed by London Daily News that
Ketchener threatens to resign his command in
S. Africa, if not allowed a freer hand to deal with
the Boers. Possibly the sudden stop put to
hangings was in consequence of orders from
London, and vexed the soul of the Soudan
warrior !
What really are trips round Sydney
harbor with liberal liquors, provided for members
of N.S.W. Parliament, but the Dalley-Stuart
system of influencing members revived ? No
Minister has a right to bolster up his power with a
jolly-good-fellow reputation at the expense of the
public purse.
Sydney Telegraph now pins its faith to
the Senate, and urges that body to kill the tariff
in the interests of the Chinese egg and the Asiatic
textile industry. But a few months ago the
Senate was to be a hideous thing, the robber
stronghold of necessitous States preying on
N.S.W. Now it is the citadel of Freedom and
the fortress of Liberty. Yah !
According to Minister Forrest, to place all
the Commonwealth rifle clubs on the favorable
terms enjoyed by the Vic. clubs will cost £75,000
a year in ammunition alone. When the other
States cultivate rifle-clubs. as Victoria has done—
she now possesses quite half the total number
(142,000) of rifle-club members—Australia will
shoot away fully £lOO,OOO a year at targets.
The Sydney dailies, which continually
wail that had the tariff been anticipated “we
would never have passed the Bill,” attempted
during the referendum campaign to make the
public believe that a very much higher range of
duties would be imposed. Either, then, the people
did anticipate the tariff, or they had a very poor
opinion of the veracity of the Freetrade news-
papers.
The final result of Senate and House of
Reps, discussions on the Postal Bill is that no
power is given to the postal officials to summarily
stop a newspaper circulating for either “ blas-
phemy ”or “ sedition.” The Senate was willing
enough to abandon the Almighty by allowing the
word “blasphemy” to be omitted, but made a
stubborn fight on behalf of King Edward for
“sedition.”
Pointed out in Q. Parliament that, at
date of last prisons report, 24 Kanakas were in
gaol—s per cent, of the total prisoners—though
the race constitutes less than 2 per cent, of the
population. Altogether, 103 Kanakas (6 per cent,
of the total prisoners) were imprisoned during
1900. In same year, 460 colored aliens —gentle,
docile, harmless, law-abiding, &c.—found their
way into gaol.
Another British war-loan is rumored. So
far, the cost of trying to subdue the Boer republics
borders on £200,000,000 or so, and before Cham-
berlain has the title-deeds of Naboth’s vineyard
safe, it seems as if he would have to pay for it at
the rate per foot frontage of a block in the heart
of London. Moral—When you want your neigh-
bor’s property, always find out beforehand what
kind of a gun he keeps.
The Chamberlain agitation to increase
Governor-General Hopetoun’s salary is expected
to burst out afresh at a near date. A rule of the
Colonial Office often quoted for the benefit of
Australians is that a Governor’s salary shall not
be reduced during his tenure of office. In
Barkly’s time the British Government vetoed an
Act of Vic. Parliament which cut Barkly from
£lO,OOO to £7OOO. And it’s a poor rule which can’t
work both ways.
The laundry rates increase which is being
so widely advertised in Sydney is mostly “ fake.”
There has been no advance in starch or soap
prices to warrant a 25 per cent, rise in the rates
for laundry work, and those who resist the im-
position will find that it is soon abandoned. The
white grabster, by the way, is simply playing into
the hands Of the Chinese washerman, now becom-
ing very numerous in Sydney, who may be relied
on to avail himself of his opportunity.
A sum-
ming-up of
the argu-
ments for
extension of
pastoral
leases in
Q u e e n s-
land :
The pastoral
indu s t r y
neverdid pay.
It took all the
good seasons
to stock up
and improve
on borrowed
money. The
bad seasons
swallowed the
stock and
loaded the
mortgages
with arrears
of interest.
The price of
wool is too
low. The rent
and taxes are
■ too high.
Freights are *■
extortionate.
Large areas
don’t pay,
small areas
can’t pay. Ir-
rigation is im-
possi b 1 e.
Tanks are
only expedi-
ents. The rab-
bits cannotbe
coped with. Nobody ever made money at bona-fide
squatting ; only by dealing in squattages. But give us a
40 or 50 years’ lease and all will be well. With long leases,
we will be able to borrow more money at a cheaper rate
on the securities which we have already damned beyond
redemption, and we might, if good seasons or something
or other happens, redeem all our past irretrievable loss-
and make a .profit where profit has never been made
before.
Discussion of the “ ifs ” of the House of
Reps, censure debate still meander wearisomely
along. Bruce Smith, for instance, remarks that
if all members had voted according to their fiscal
opinions the Govt, would have had a majority of
four only ; which, if true, only shows that some
members were wise enough to be Australian
Nationalists rather than British Cobdenites. One-
other authority talks of what would have hap-
pened “ had all the N.S.W. members voted true
to the policy of their State”—a State which abso-
lutely smashed up the Lee foreign-trade party at
last general election !
At the instance of Meagher, N.S.W.
Assembly omitted this year the sum of £l5O for the
Anthropological Society. In addition to studying
and preserving records of our fast-decaying
aborigines, the Anthropological Society deals with
human degeneration, bodily and mental, crimin-
ology and penology. In connection with the
society are associations devoted to child-study
and anthropometrioal measurements. A grant of
£l5O to the society would seem to most people a
splendid investment, though there possibly are
reasons whyM’s.P. should not think so.
Rep. Spence was true to N.S.W. Laborite-
traditions in the vote on the no-confidence motion
of Reid. On the other hand, Thomas, repre-
sentative of the Broken Hill miners, sold his
con tituents badly. Returned pledged to judge
every Govt, according to its programme of social-
reform legislation, Thomas, a hard-shell Free-
trader, on the very first opportunity conveniently
forgot the Govt.’s social policy and voted as a
Freetrader and not as a Laborite at all. That
means the end of the farce of “sinking the fiscal
issue ” at Broken Hill.
Macdonald-Paterson, in the House of
Reps., on the Kanaka Bill
He did not know what would happen in Queensland.
There was seething discontent there, bordering on revo-
lution, because of the intention of the Government to-
force the Bill through.
The discontent of a few bank-shareholders and
other broad-clothed gentlemen can be safely left to-
seethe without fear of its boiling over. The humor-
ous spectacle of about one-tenth of the people of
Queensland rising in revolution against the other
nine-tenths, plus the whole of Australia, in
defence of its right to import cheap niggers, is not.
likely to be witnessed —worse luck.
A curious bit of N.S.W. Constitutional
law is that a member of the Leg. Council may-
interview the Governor without an intermediary,,
while a member of the Leg. Assembly is only
entitled to an interview through the Speaker.
Sydney Telegraph interviews George
Reid’s Foreign-trade brother, Hugh R. Reid,
Melbourne merchant :
While in London Mr. Reid was assured that if tne Com-
monwealth Government ran its own line of steamers to-
England with mails and passengers, a saving to the people-
of something like £150,000 per annum could be effected,
and fares and freights reduced by one-third. At present
a great English shipping ring controls the Australian
trade. They pay 20s. per ton to the shipowners from
whom they charter, and then fix the rates so as to bring
them in 40s. per ton.
A ring or trust that has put up prices 100 per cent,
isn’t quite the advertisement for Freetrade that
it might be. Apparently rings and trusts can
flourish in any soil and under any system —if th©
people are foolish enough to let them do so.


The /EOLIAN
THE /EOLIAN is really a solo
orchestra brought within the
home and can be played by
every member of the family. It
opens up the rich field of orchestral
music to every one.
As its technical capacity is un-
limited, it enables the player to
execute every note of the score —
an achievement which it is next to
impossible for two hands to ac-
complish.
While the /Eolian has techni-
cal powers immeasurably beyond
those of the most skilful techni-
cian, the performer is complete
master of the expression.
This is the secret of the/Eolian’s
fascination. It is responsive to
the player’s every feeling.
The great popularity of the JE olian in Country homes is a
pertinent illustration of their practical value. In these retreats
away from musical entertainment, an .dEolian provides the
pleasure of hearing any composition one’s mood may call for
a.7iy time , with the added pleasure of producing the’ music
one’s self.
The iEolian is sold at no other
address in Sydney.

Ulcers on Both Legs.
HE HAD TO BE ASSISTED IN AND OUT OF BED.
Cured by Vitadatio.
To Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir, —About two years afro I fell from a ladder
and burst a vein in my leg. A little later on I slipped
off the shaft of a cart, and grazed the other leg on the
step, the consequence of this was that ulcers broke out
on both legs, and for a considerable time I suffered great
pain, and could not bend either of my knees. I could
neither get in or out of bed without assistance. At this
time I spoke to Mr. Palmer, and asked him his advice,
■and he advised me to try Vitadatio, with the result that
in a very short time I was completely cured. The pain
died out, and I got the use of my legs back again, thanks
to Vitadatio. I can highly recommend it to any person
suffering from any form of blood disease.
Yours faithfully,
JOHN CHARLTON.

LIVER
LOGIC.
The Liver is the main spring of the system.
When active it is the great purifying organ.
When sluggish it easily develops a poisonous
tendency, contaminating all the great
organs.
The Liver both secretes and excretes.
It secretes about three pounds of bile daily.
It excretes impurities and waste materials.
The Liver is the sewage farm of the body, and
since it is the sieve it must perforce be kept
clean, otherwise it becomes sluggish.
If the Liver is sluggish it cannot expel its refuse,
and as it must go somewhere it is absorbed
back into the blood, causing biliousness and
often jaundice.
The Liver also makes from the blood a kind of
Sugar, which, if healthy, is used up as
nourishment.
If the sugar is excessive or inferior it cannot be
so used; it then escapes into the urine,
causing diabetes.
A Torpid Liver develops uric acid, gout and
rheumatism in predisposed subjects.
The absolute, incontestible remedy for all this,
and the one which never fails, is Nature’s
Great Restorative, Kutnow’s Improved Effer-
vescent Carlsbad Powder.
This Powder is made by an ingenious expedient
from the health-giving medicinal springs of
the Continental Spas.
It is improved by removing all drastic or lowering
properties.
It is gentle and pleasant, but the greatest of all
Stimulators of the Liver.
K UTNO W’S PO WD ER may
be obtained at any chemist or
medicine vendor in capsuled
bottles, but care must be taken
to see that it is Kutnow’s.
Don’t accept suggested substi-
tutes, they are worthless.

There is a ludicrous “derangement of
epitaphs ” about some of the utterances of Austra-
lia’s recent visitor, teetotal apostle G reenough.
Lecturing recently on the founders of Methodism,
he said, according to the report—
When John Wesley commenced to preach, the quantity
of trin consumed in'England amounted to four f ; al 1 1( ? l ? s
for every man, woman, and child, and if they added the
Xe and beer that were drunk, they would get a
stupendous drink-bill. It was into this valley of dry
bones that the winds of God commenced to blow.
Dry bones, indeed !
“Red Tape, jun.,'’writes
One item on N.S.W. Public Works Estimates was £3OB,
to recoup the Superannuation Fund, for abatements to
officers retired in 1895-6. The “officers” is Joseph
Barling. His “ retirement ” consisted in being promoted
from Under-Secretary for Works at £920 to preside over
the Public Service at £lOOO a year. In response to a
strong protest the O’Sullivan temporarily withdrew the
item ■" Members should ask for a lot of information
before they consent to the vote. This matter of recoups
from the decaying Superannuation Fund is the burning
question in the Service. When notice was given a few
y ears a g o that public servants could withdraw' from
contributing ” to the account, the great majority did
so. Discontinuing, they, of course, forfeited their pen-
sion rights, and, to their dismay, they were not allowed
to get the money, already paid in, hack. It had been
collared by the Treasury for 15 years. Yet Mr. Barling was
allowed to withdraw his little bit. Is there one law for
the magnate at £lOOO and another for the clerk at £lOO
in the Public Service?
