SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Melbourne Oup race was a disappointment
for the g.p., notwithstanding- the tact that the first and
second favorites fought out the finish, and the great
monte ’ did actually do the trick. Wakeful’s hundreds
of backers had expected that the mare would give them
some excitement for their money. Also the hundreds
who followed Hymettus felt confident that the big chest-
nut would be seen battling for the lead half-a-mile from
home. Opinions differed as to which of the two would
wear the other down, but the partisans of both held tight
to the idea that the history of their Caulfield Cup struggle
would, in some measure, repeat itself. The result falsified
all predictions re those old antagonists. Neither of them
ever looked like getting away with the two miles’ race.
Wakeful, although she led in the first furlong, was content
to let something else (Rockleigh) make the pace as they
swept past the grand-stand, and for the rest of the journey
she ran third, fourth, or fifth. Hymettus, alwaj T s behind
the mare, was beaten at 1} miles, what time Flagship was
sailing away in front—a reversal of the Caulfield order of
things.
And the victory of Revenue was a quite ortho-
dox achievement, after all. There was no taking the lead
a mile from home and winning by “ half a furlong.”
Revenue’s friends and enemies confined themselves to two
rival predictions—the dark horse was either going to
romp away from the field, or prove an utter fraud. In
the end, he neither romped nor failed. He ran like an
ordinary “ good thing,” which gets ordinary good luck.
Had he started at an ordinary short price for a fancied
Oup horse—say 6to I—his win would have seemed fairly
“ solid,” but, under the circumstances, it looked to be
drawn rather fine.
Revenue was the best animal in the race at the
weights, but the advantage that the handicappergave him
was probably of less consequence to the big, steady-going
gelding than it would have been in the case of his stable
companion, Wakeful. One would pick Revenue for a
weight-carrier rather than a flyer.
By the way, Flagship’s forward running in
Melbourne Oup for miles was a contrast to his tame
performance in Hotham handicap three days previously.
The 4-year-old (a 5 to 2 chance) held fourth place from
end to end of the Hotham gallop, and looked very
distressed when he came back to the birdcage. But in
the Oup he was leading the field at a mile and a half.
The lengthening of the price against Hymettus
for Melb. Oup meant that the Caulfield winner had been
beaten three lengths by George Frederick in a trial over
the Oup distance. Also, the big chestnut showed by his
running fourth to Wakeful in Melb. Stakes, that he had,
perhaps, lost a little of his dash, or was not so well dis-
posed to struggle at Flemington as at Caulfield, where
all his best performances have been scored. In 22 years
only one Oaulfield Cup winner (Grace Darling) has gone
very near to capturing the “ double.” Hymettus is not
a better stayer than was Paris, who won two
Caulfield Cups and a Metrop. (after the Metrop. dis-
tance had been reduced to 12fur.), but could never
go two miles. As a four-year-old, Paris was given
a chance to win Melb. Cup with 8.2, but he finished
nowhere. Next year, after he had won his first Caulfield
Cup carrying 8.8, he failed to get placed in the Sydney
Oup with 9st. up. What Paris was Hymettus is—a
speedy horse with just enough stamina to support him
through a long final sprint up to the 12fur. limit. Had
he been asked to make pace for Wakeful in the Caulfield
Cup the mare would doubtless have finished ahead of
him.
Both of the Melbourne dailies on Melb. Cup
morning referred to Wakeful as a possible non-starter, but
neither touched upon the fact that an eleventh hour
scratching of Wakeful would have been an outrage. It
goes without saying that Wakeful was always a certain
starter. The stable’s preference for Revenue didn’t alter
the conditions of the race. Melbourne Cup is worth
£lOOO and £5OO to the owners of the second and third
horses ; and the stable, apart from the question of bets
about Wakeful’s winning the race outright, could have no
reason for sacrificing the chance to pick up £lOOO or £5OO.
Therefore the “ ifs ” and “ buts ” of the Melb. dailies were
simple darned foolishness, or were intended to put the
general punting public “ off ” Wakeful, in the interests of
Ikey Mo, who wanted to save the mare for himself as much
as possible whilst laying shortened prices against all the
others.
Melb. Argus, whose horse-reporters seem to
write everything that the most influential horse-owners
could desire of them, was particularly trucklesome in the
matter of the 3-mile Hurdle race on Cup-day. Said the
Argus : “ The winner (Arcadia) is owned by Mr. S. Miller,
chairman of the Y.R.0., who bought him recently for
£3OO ; but he thought so little of his chance on this occa-
sion that he didn’t even go near the ring to inquire his
price. It is not often that- a horse wins a hurdle race
after jumping steeplechase fences, but Arcadia is
evidently the exception which proves the rule." Now, as
a matter of fact, Mr. S. Miller’s horse was so favorably
handicapped for the Hurdle race that he couldn’t have been
blind to its chance, and if he didn’t inquire the price there
was no reason why somebody else shouldn’t make an
investment for him. Arcadia was a hurdle-racer before
he became a brilliant steeplechaser. He beat a big field
of hurdlers as recently as June 22, and was then handi-
capped at lOst. On July 6th he was second favorite for
V.R.O. G.N. Hurdle race, in which he carried 9.9. Sub-
sequently he won the G.N. Steeple, at Oaulfield, and
again on Guineas day he won the Steeple with 12.7 up.
On these good performances he was somewhat “thrown
into” the hurdle race aforesaid with only lOst. 71b.
Arcadia is a far better horse than the Argus line of com-
ment would lead one to suppose.
• • * •
The Wallace filly Beanba secured V.R.C. Oaks
(ljm.) with great ease, in record-time of 2.37}. Best pre-
vious performance was Haulette’s (2}sec. more), last year.
Veteran Stakes, run immediately after over same distance
as Oaks, occupied £min. longer than Beanba’s time. Both
Beanba and Haulette were prepared by the same trainer
—Earnshaw.
* • * •
The Oaks was but fseo. slower than the Derby
gallop—which is to say that, barring this year’s Derby
time, Beanba smashed the record for the mile-and-a-half
by a three-year-old at w.f.a. As the bard observes—
“ Time’s not ‘ form ’ in itself, you see ;
And don’t you be forgetting it.
Always bet your money on the best gee-gee,
But don’t take Time for betting it."
When Sequence was seen to go clean away from Wakeful
in the Flying Stakes last Thursday, a man situated at the
back of the members’-stand set up a vociferous chuckling
and crovving—“ Ho !ho ! nice chance they’ve got now ! A
nice chance—against Sequence. Hee 1 hee 1 Ho ! ho ! ” &c.
The “ they” whom this offensive rooster was crowing at
were presumably Wakeful’s backers, who laid 2 to 1 on
the winner of the Melbourne Stakes and Caulfield Stakes
because she had left Sequence all behind in those events.
But if the little mare had not been out of sorts, the crow
of the Sequence party would have been a sad moan as
before. Sequence is very fast, but he doesn’t fly off the
mark or seem equal to making his invincible run if
another fast one has got away with a big break. In the
so-called Flying Stakes the Epsom handicap phenomenon
had “ nothing,” bar Wakeful, to beat, and she didn’t put
his courage to the test. Old Paul Pry, who appeared
broken down on a hind leg, finished third.
Australian Colors and Grasspan were about
the moat conspicuous disappointments of Caulfield-
rlemington Carnival season. Mr. Raby's colt certainly
scored second place for the Derby, and was unlucky, per-
haps, in missing the blue ribbon, but, with that one
exception, he ran like a very over-rated colt. Re the
Derby joetle, there were some who declare that Rock Gun
must have got a place under fair conditions. Any way, his
backers for the classic race acted up to their opinion by
taking 3 and 4 to 1 that the colt would down Sequence
m the O. B. Fisher Plate. Thanks to Corsican making
a merry pace, the downing of Sequence duly happened,
xtock Gun collared the brilliant non-stajer in the
straight, bumped him, and beat him home by two
ooea 8 m khe exceptionally fast time (for Flemington) of
Z.dbjSec. One of the best races of the meeting was this
struggle between the “ Gun ” and Sequence, after Corsican
had spun himself out. As a sporting spectacle, it was
worth 10 of the minor handicaps, in which half of the
blocked, and the result would almost invariably
be different if they had to run the jostle again.

The Haut Brion colt and dual Derby winner,

Australasian mile and a-half record (2.35), re-
ferred to so much since Hymettus’ recent Caulfield Cup
feat, was first set up by the late W. Kelso’s Survivor, in

• * • •
It often happens that a man who knows nothing,
and cares less, about the ins and outs of horse-racing is
apt to find winners at Flemington by the simple plan of
following his racial fancies. John Brunton, the scenic
artist, went to the Derby as a matter of custom, and,
being a verra leal Scot, he was tempted to put a sovereign
on the tartan jacket of Ohio’s jockey in the Hotham
Handicap. The odds of 10 to 1 against the old gelding
having been duly landed, Brunton’s national spirit
prompted him to risk £1 on Caledonia for the Coburg
Stakes, and he went home a winner of £l7 on his braw
Scottish luck.
• * • »
St. Albans’ nag, Pigeontoe, cast and died in his
box recently, put up some marvellous gallops prior to
Malvoho’s Cup, and started a four to one favorite for the
glided mug himself, after winning Hotham Handicap at
the same assembly.
Queensland form of Boreas II is widely different
from that shown in Sydney. He got away with Q.T.C.
King’s Cup on Saturday last—humping the top stao-gerer
of 9.9, and covering l}m. in 2.38 J. Inference is that he
is a much better prad at home than away, or that the
quality of the other Bananalanders is inferior. Fashion
in Sydney has been to discount performances of Queens-
land nags in Queensland, for they have never run up to
that form in the Cornstalk capital. Ohio was a big
offender this way, yet his doings at recent V.R.C. meeting
showed that he is on a level with the best.

Air Motor (winner of Rosehill Maiden Stakes,
Saturday) started favorite and snapped the money from
the fire —through Childers, after getting a big break, stop-
ping palpably towards the end. Air Motor (bred by Mr.
H. L. Mackellar) looks and promises well. She is a three-
year-old, by the Merton stallion, Ayr Laddie (imp.), from
the Goldsbrough mare, Windmill, who went to M.L in
foal to Gossoon.

The Bulletin wouldn’t very much mind wager-
ing a little that Tattersall’s sweeps won’t be interfered
with after all !
* * * *
“ Maclaren’s team,” writes an Adelaide corres-
pondent to The Bulletin, “ looks, at first sight, a likely
lot. A preponderance of clean-shaven men—M’Gahey
Braund, Jones, Garnett, Jessop, Gunn, Blythe—adds to
the youthful appearance of the crowd. With a few excep-
tions, the visitors are undersized—Jessop, Quaife Tyldes-
ley, and Gunn being all midgets. The giant of the team
is Barnes, who stands over 6ft., and is heavily built—a
kind of improved Bill Howell.
“Maclaren, despite the fact that his team left
amid a chorus of English disapproval, is sanguine and
many of his team are even confident. ‘We have an all-
round team,’ he says, 1 but we are admittedly weak in
bowling. England has been weak in bowling ‘for years
The only bowler who should have come is Rhodes ’
Maclaren evidently remembers Hirst’s failure. At prac-
tice on Adelaide Oval, Barnes stands out above his con-
freres. He has a good length, and worked an off-break
on a billiard-table wicket. He is between medium and
fast. Braund, a leg-break man, gets some work on, and
Blythe shapes excellently ; but Clem Hill, Trumper
Noble and co. are bound to trounce what looks like
mediocre stuff.
“ The batting will have to be the Britishers’
mainstay. Jessop, who believes the fast wickets will suit
him, is a nuggetty-built little man, with a powerfully-
developed forearm, and gets his strength in hitting from
the elbow. His stand at the crease is the ugliest writer
has seen, and his crouch reduces his height to about 4ft.
But he gets great power, all the same.
“Apparently, M‘Laren has brought a good
fielding team. Jones and Jessop have done fine work at
practice. The latter is the best cover-point in England.
Garnett, who is only 20, is a left-handed bat, who seems
to be Jessop’s understudy as a hitter. Tyldesley, Quaife,
Gunn, and Hayward are the stonewallers. Summed-up,
M'Laren’s team must make heavy scores against Aus-
tralia in order to win.
» « * *
The cantankerous N.S.W. Cricket Association
now has a dispute with Maclaren on the subject of
umpires. The Association wants to appoint both ; Mac.
demands to choose one for his team. Some vague “ prin-
ciple ” is at stake, and at ’Ome the visiting Australian
teams never can select an umpire. It certainly appears
more graceful and sportsmanlike to allow each side to
appoint one man, and as S. A. has given way on the point
the other States might follow suit.
* * * »
At moment of writing, England v. S.A. match
looks good for the latter, bar some of those possible
peculiar happenings so frequently evident at cricket.
Home team made 230 first and 207 next. Visi-
tors started badly, and had a disastrous time of it
right through the innings, notching only 118. Apparent
crumbling appearance of the wicket scared them a heap.
Veteran Giffen—rated very much of a has-been by self-
constituted judges—did great things, getting 7 for 46.
Wicket-fright of batting side helped him a deal, for they
played back at well-placed balls which called for forward
work. Doings of the S.A. men demonstrated that there
wasn’t much wrong with the pitch, as they hit freely all
round. One looked for the Englishman’s talked-of mar-
vellous fielding in vain. Beyond picking up the balls and
returning smartfy, there was nothing to rave about in it.
* * * *
Clem. Hill’s innings for S.A. against the Eng-
lishmen, at Adelaide on Saturday, was exceptionally
brilliant. Throughout over three hours batting hie
never gave the semblance of a chance, and notched
107 with great freedom, sending ten to the boun-
dary. English team’s trundling—all things weighed—
was high-class, but they grievously lacked a swift bowler.
Brunt of the work was borne by Gunn, Braund, Blythe,
and Barnes. Last-named (the “ fast ” bowler of the side)
shows little better than medium pace. Clem. Hill’s cen-
tury in this match is the fourth compiled by him for the
wheat State against an English Eleven. Previous figures
were 150, 200, and 124. In S.A. second innings he scored
80.
* • * •
“Adelaide”: First impressions of Maclaren’s
team show that they “ run in all shapes.” Garnett is
built very much on the lines of Clem. Hill. M’Gahey
(already known in Australia by a private visit) is tall and
slim ; might be a good-looking actor. The great Jessop,
small but nuggety ; particularly gentle in his manners.
Robson, a jolly, brown-faced typical Englishman, of 40 or
thereabouts. A. O. Jones, a younger and handsomer
Bilmartin—tremendous Roman nose, and same deter-
mined expression. The captain himself is voted much
improved by responsibility ; his speeches have shown him
anxious to convince that he has a strong and representa-
tiveteam. At the nets most of them have shown themselves
bats of the dashing order, Jones especially. Tom Hayward
is unaltered. Quaife and Tyldesley are very small men, and
so is Gunn, who has a remarkable crooked run (just like
Hirst’s) in bowling. Barnes is no colt, but a big, burly
man, who sends them down at a great pace. Blythe is a
delicate-looking boy ; the others think his left-hand stuff
with a spin just suited to these wickets. The rest of the
bowling (Jessop and Hayward, for various reasons, don’t
do much now) is mostly leg-breaks. Braund makes his
do the most astonishing things, and is likely to be unex-
pectedly dangerous. He is a humorous person, and much
resembles Horace Hodges, in Wilson Barrett’s co. Lilley
is a well-built fellow, of no particular style (till he gets
the gloves on). Nine of the team took part in a ping-
pong tournament the evening before the S.A. match
began.
* * * *
Fourth round of Sydney first-grade crioket com-
petitions ended Saturday. Paddington squelched North
Sydney by eight wickets. Losers declared their second
innings closed with three for 137. Paddington played up
in fine style, and notched the 121 necessary to win with
the loss of two wickets only. Leichhardt-Balmain
accounted for Redfern by a margin of 89. Waverley,
against Central Cumberland, stopped at five for 459, and
latter accumulated 393. Expiration of time prevented
Burwood from beating Glebe. A draw resulted. Sydney
and Cricket Ground team saw former credited with 571
for seven wickets.
* • * *
Scraps from Saturday’s Sydney first - grade
cricket. Hopkins got 76 (not out) of North Sydney’s 137
against Paddington, and Victor Trumper 64 of latter’s
121. Pye and Wilson made a big stand for Central
Cumberland, opposed to Waverley. Former scored 178,
and his mate 140. Nothing but a pronounced tail pre-
vented the fruitgrowing centre’s reps, from winning. The
other nine and “ Mr.” sundries only aggregated 75. This
was Pye’s second century in first-grade cricket present
year. Previous one (154) was hit up against Glebe.
Wilson’s feat was a surprise. It’s long since he did so
well. Sydney’s 571 (seven- wickets down) against the
Cricket Ground side included 220 (not out) from Pite.
Lowest contribution to the total was 18. So far, Leich-
hardt-Balmain and Paddington have come out best in all
their matches.
• • * *
Match between East Maitland and Albion clubs,
at Maitland (N.S.W.), Saturday, produced a new Aus-
tralian record for first wicket down. Lindsay and Onus
knocked up 444 before Onus’ stumps were scattered for
192. Day’s play ended with one for 469. Lindsay accu-
mulated 242 (not out). Previous Australian best was 318,
but the world’s record stands at 605, scored by Trevor
and Vernon for Orleans against Rickling Green, in Eng-
land 19 years back. First-class cricket best is 554, made
by Tunnicliffe and Brown (Yorkshire against Derbyshire)
in ’9B.
* * * *
One-time Sydney newsboy, Cocker Tweedie,
(10st.), late of The Rocks, and Broken Hill 11.4 man, Jack
Dunne, thumped and sprawled over each other, Saturday
night, for the middle-weight supremacy of the wheat
State. Tweedie, certain of victory, despite tissue dis-
parity, made the pace from “time” and got all over
Dunne without doing him much harm. In the fourth
set Dunne dropped Tweedie with a flukey one on the point,
and when he staggered into an un-upright position on his
unsteady hind legs another on the same spot sent him to
sleep. Pugilism has been dead in S.A. for years, but if
pugs would only go straight (which they can’t), they could
work up a boom in the Holy City.
• * » *
Ex-novice pug. Peter Toohey brought bantam
champ. Bert Weekes down with a crash at Sydney
Golden Gate A. 0., Saturday night. Bert, over-confident,
lacked preparation. Weekes punched cleverly and often,
but Toohey only shook himself like a bull-pup, and came
along for more, till an opening gave his sinister mauley a
chance ; then home it went, and lean, lank, boyish peic
went staggering. Weekes operated briskly, using right
and left, but most blows were ineffective. Toohey' omy
brought one hand into action (the other might have been
left at home for all he did with it), though always
to some purpose. He clinched and clung occasionally',
when things didn’t look too inviting, and thus avoided
one or two dangerous-looking swipes, but it is question-
able if they carried enough power to affect Tooheys
blow-proof hide. It was one on the mark in the seventh
round that spread-eagled Weekes beyond hope. A man
who “ knew something” could have picked up shekels
backing Toohey', against whom odds of 3 to 1 were plen-
tiful. Weekes should not fight ; he is a capable boxer,
and that’s all. Take away legs and wings and there s
nothing left; besides which he isn’t wicked enough for
the willing game.
* * • *
M.C.C.’s bowling tourney for Australian supre-
macy didn’t catch on so well this year as last, but play
was good throughout. Of 96 entrants, Saturday saw
eight still going—all Victorians. Sims, Wartman, Wax-
man, and Ellis went to the semi-final. Sims finished 7,
and Waxman 6, better than Wartman and Ellis respec-
tively. The final was exciting enough (as far as bowls
can be) for anything, and, during the time being, stopped
all biz. at the bar. The 18th “head” scores were “12
all.” Waxman only notched two more before Sims
reached the journey’s end—2l. Sims belongs to Mel-
bourne club and Waxman to Armadale. Wartman (Mel-
bourne) got third prize, and Ellis (St. Hilda) fourth.
* * * *
Premier swimmer, Fred. Lane, t’other day won
a 90 yards handicap in S.vdney', doing exactly the same
time (54 2-5) in his heat and the final. Six months spell
on Mark Fo.v’s station in the Parkes (N.S.W.) district has
done him a heap of good, and if he goes down this season
it won’t be through ill health or lack of wonted power.

Sydney is just now suffering from a high-
way-robbery boom. A couple of weeks ago, a
" u %gy loaded with many golden sovereigns was
stuck-up near Clyde, but the occupants had pluck
(and a ‘ gun”), and the highwayman was baffled.
This week a couple in a sulky were held up,
pounded into insensibility, and robbed of about
£4O within the very boundaries of the city.
The gang (there is probably only one gang)
seems to operate with singular coolness and
forethought. Success at Clyde would have
meant a bag of many hundreds of pounds,
and the people held up last Saturday night
would have panned out a good return on that
night only. Given a certain amount of audacity
and the city highwayman seems to carry on his
operations with a reasonable degree of safety : so
far there have been several crimes of this sort, but
no criminals have been caught.

The Fair Rent Bill and the State Fire
Insurance Bill have both been dropped by the
M.L. Government. The Seddon Ministry in its
old age developes a capacity for dropping things
which is only equalled by the untidy woman with
all the little parcels who gets into the tram just
after it has started. And its economical prin-
ciples, its self-reliant policy, and its objection to
floating big loans are among the things that the
Dropping Ministry has dropped.

M.L. cargoes to Australia have shrunk
tremendously since the new tariff. The Mokoia,
Wellington to Sydney, Nov. 9, carried only 30
tons; the trip’s expenses reaching £4OO, freight
and fares only £l5O. The Zealandia, from Auck-
land, Dec. 9, brings only 102 tons; former average,
560 tons. Of course, trade has not settled yet.
Wired that Gen. Hector Macdonald, dur-
ing his M.L. visit, confined his speeches wholly
to urging “ military organisation for the defence
of the colony.” In other words, Macdonald is a
recruiting-officer pure and simple. For who
doubts that, once “ the military organisation ” is
established, no time will be lost in perverting it
to the base uses of Imperialism—with the aid of
nobbled—ennobbled—politicians ?

Sydney Telegraph declares it a good
omen that Salisbury’s Guildhall speech was
“ comparatively barren. ” It is the worst possible
omen ; for when a politician has good news he
doesn’t lose a moment in telling it. Africa is
seething with hostility ; Britain is muttering dis-
content ; another increase of taxation is fore-
shadowed ; and Parliament is further pro-
rogued because Govt dares not meet it
at present. Meanwhile, fresh reinforcements
are being sent to Ketchener—nearly 1000
Guards last week, 1000 recruits this week ; and
the war is farther from an end than ever. Prac-
tically the whole Dutch population has been made
hostile ; and the British military will not be out of
S’Africa for many years to come. So much for
prostituting an Empire to Chamberlain and
Rhodes !

COMMONWEALTH*


A travelled American writes, on his return
to TJ.9.A. recently :
Keep your eye on Sydney I . . . We Americans pride
ourselves on having money to burn. The Austra-
lians make more on the average than we do, and their
living expenses are higher than ours. . . . You see
the signs of wealth on the streets. There are no
patched clothes, and no sun-bonnets. The average
working-man is better clad than his brother of the
U.S. Many of the women wear tailor-made gowns,
and the clerks all have creased pantaloons. . . . The
crowds in a Sydney street are like those of Chicago or
New York, save that the German, Swedish, and Italian
faces are absent. Here, the faces are all English, Irish,
and Scotch ; or, what is better, pure Australian. The
Australians are finer-looking than the people of Great
Britain. They are taller, straighter, and better formed.
Six feet is not an uncommon height for either men or
women. The latter are Amazons; and, owing to the
climate and their sedentary life, they are raw-boned and
ungainly (! 1 !).
As others see us I

ANOTHER
CONCLUSIVE REPORT.
Th® Case ofMr, C. D. BROWN.
A RECORD FROM WATERLOO.
In course of his duty a reporter knocked
at the door of No. 74 Garden Street, Alex-
andria, which was promptly opened by Mr.
Charles Douglas Brown. Inviting his visitor
into the parlour, Mr. Brown intimated him-
self at leisure for a chat.
“Will you permit me to report your case?”
queried the scribe.
“ Most willingly,” was the response.
“ But I have two experiences to relate, and
you may as well take them dowr in the order
that I had them. When I was a lad of about
sixteen, great anxiety was occasioned amongst
my relatives through the miserable way I
went about. Without any warning I com-
menced to lose flesh until my bones were vis-
ible through my skin. I had not had any
illness to bring about this state of affairs,
and the sudden change was unaccountable.
My appetite was very uncertain. Sometimes
I had a loathing of food, and at others I ate
voraciously. Nothing that was given to me
prevented my flesh falling off, and people
thought I was going into consumption. I
often had a feeling of chilliness running
through my body, my feet and knees some-
times being very cold, even when sitting in
a room where other people complained about
the heat. Then a bad, hacking cough came
on, which strained my enfeebled constitu-
tion greatly. I was troubled with difficulty
in breathing, very little exertion causing me
to sit down and gasp for breath.”
“Were there any painful symptoms?”
“ Yes ; a number of them, not the least of
which were those than attacked me in the
shoulder and bowels. When I raised my re-
latives hopes by eating heartily after hardly
tasting a bite for several days, they thought
I was on the mend ; but shortly afterwards
the severe pains in the chest and stomach
told a different tale. I became afraid to eat,
and so got weaker and weaker, till I had not
enough vitality to take interest in anjthing.
I was very fond of doing little jobs at home
connected with my trade, such as polishing
pieces of marble and making frames and imi-
tation books ; but these were all set aside,
lliere was al ways a bitter taste in my mouth,
lhe bloom of youth faded .from my cheeks,
and I wore a pallid, unwholesome colour in-
stead. I looked like a walking ghost. I re-
sembled one, at all events, in respect to feel-
ing just about half dead. Dull frontal head-
aches tortured me persistently, and the
excruciating pain took all the *go ’ out of
me. I thought my life was destined to be
nipped in the bud, and I would prefer death
to a continuance of such suffering.”
“Surely you could obtain some relief?”
Not until I was given Clements Tonic,
i don’t remember now how many different
remedies I did take, but I do remember
quite clearly that I did not get the slightest
relief from any of them. On the other
hand, when it looked as though my case was
past the aid of medicine, Clements Tonic
came to my rescue, and as soon as I com-
menced taking it I found myself breathing
more freely. A feeling of great comfort was
experienced after taking a couple of doses,
aS i, m £ P ains started easing off, and I slept
well Die first night—a luxury that had been
denied me for some time. In the morning
i felt a freedom from the aches in my limbs,
fu spirits were bright and cheerful,
the. feelings of despondency and grief
having passed away with my night’s slum-
ber. The fretful irritability of temper which
had marked my sickness gave way to an
evenly-balanced mind, and I was soon a
legu.ai attendant at the table, my meals
econnng larger with a daily-increasing
appetite. I soon made up for lost time
under the influence of C’ements Tonic, my
cheeks filling out and displaying a tinge of
re< rr‘ t I mentioned that I
Buffered from costiveness during my illness ;
but that trouble, like all my other ailments,
was soon set right by Clements Tonic, which
thoroughly restored my health a- d strength,
and gave my youthful constitution a solid
foundation to work on.”
«< ?> ave y ou . been fluite well ever since ? ”
1 have, with the exception of a severe
attack of neuralgia. That forms the second
experience which I have to relate. This
attack was more torturing than my first
sickness. The exposure to all weathers,
which we are subject to in our trade, must
have brought it on, combined with over-
work. lam never satisfied unless I am
tinkering at something. AH this has been
done in my spare time, so you see I am never
idle. I took several remedies I had read
about, but they only made me worse. lam
quite sure of this, because the shooting pains
travelled, to my ears, and interfered with
my hearing. My health was undermined,
and I thought I should never get rid of my
complaint.” J
But y°u did succeed in doing so ?”
Oh, yes, after a lot of unnecessary
suffering. A friend one day told me that
Clements Tonic was a sure cure for
neuralgia. I regret not having known this
fact before. My recollection of the wonder-
ful curative piopcrties of Clements Tonic
made persuasion unnecessary, and I bought
three bottles right away. The first couple
of doses lessened the severity of the pains
in my head and face, and the ear-ache be-
came less acute and died out altogether. I
was thus enabled to obtain some refreshing
sleep, and felt much better on arising.
When the second bottle of Clements Tonic
was finished my neuralgia was con pletely
cured, and I continued with? the third
bottle for the purpose of invigorating my
debilitated system. The excellent appetite
I gained enabled me to fill out again, and I
was soon as strong and stout as before my
dreadful attack. It is a long time since
Clements Tonic effected a thorough cure,
and my old comp’aint has never since
troubled me, in consideration of which you
are at liberty to fully publish my statements
any how you like.”

CAUTION.

gfg
A while back, when meat-prices rose high
in Melbourne, the dozen or so freetraders in that
city loudly demanded that the stock tax should be
taken off forthwith. This iniquitous thing, they
said, was the prime cause of the then dearness of
food, and was a grievous burden on the poor
(always “ the poor ”). It is now about four weeks
since the tr.x was lifted, and the retail butcher
hasn’t lowered his figures by a farthing.
In Feb., 188], David Buchanan moved, in
N.S.W. Assembly, that taxation should be levied
so as to compel all work that could be done to be
done in the State. W. ,J. Lyne supported him.
G. H. Reid, emphatic even in that stage of his
political infancy, pronounced Protection “dead.”
Buchanan angrily prophesied in reply: “Protec-
tion is not dead, but its truths are forcing them-
selves on a Free trade Government and a
Freetrade Parliament, and will vet prevail.” The
stand in the last ditch now made by the
Freetrade party proves the dead David a better
prophet than the live and roaring George.
N.S.W. State Coast Hospital, Little Bay,
is likely to supply some more interesting items
shortly. The drivers, nurses, and employes gene-
rally are complaining of long hours, and request-
ing that they be shortened. Also, most of the
male employes at the Coast Hospital are married.
They are always single when they go there, but
they get married as soon as they can find a
suitable girl. The hospital doesn’t supply
quarters, and the men are asking that such
quarters be supplied, for which they are quite
willing to pay rent. Meanwhile, the wives live at
Botany, four or five miles away, and the majority
of husbands sleep at Botany, leaving the
hospital with about three able-bodied men to pro-
tect it, in case of emergencies. Some night there’ll
be a grass fire, or the connecting verandah will
catch alight, and there’ll be such a burning of sick
and helpless patients that N.S.W. will demand
that someone in a high place be hanged, but will
forget all about it before the rope can be got
ready.

Mr. W. G. Hearne.—Dear Sir,—l am writing to tell you
a l,out the wonderful cure your medicine has effected in
my ease. About three years ago I began to cough. At
first the cough was not severe, but it gradually got worse,
and I became very weak and, troubled with night sweats,
pain in my chest, and great quantities of phlegm. On
several occasions there was blood in the expectorated
matter. I had been treated by a doctor, who pronounced
my case to be Consumption, and various other treatments
had been tried, but without benefit. It was at this stage
that I heard of your Bronchitis Cure, and sent to you for
a course of the medicine. When it arrived I was too ill to
leave my bed, but I commenced taking it at once, and
gradually improved. lam glad to say that the two lots
of medicine you sent have effected a complete cure, for
which accept my very best thanks.—Yours gratefully,
J. BLAIR.

The extract runs as follows :—“ As for myself, thank
the Lord, lam feeling stronger than I have for years. I
had an attack of Bronchitis in November, but Hearne’s
Bronchitis Cure was again successful. I feel quite well,
and walk into town feeling quite strong.
“ I must ask you to send me six bottles more of the
medicine, as I wish to have a supply in the house. I
have tried to get it made up here, apd let my chemist
have a bottle to see what he could do. He tells me this
week he can make nothing out of it, he never saw any-
thing like it before, so there is only one thing for me to
do —send for more. I have never kept in bed one day
since I commenced to take it. I used to be in bed a fort-
night at a time always, and after that for months I was
as weak as I could possibly be, and was always taking
cod liver oil, so you will see at once it is quite worth
while sending for it such a long distance.
“ Something more I must tell you. Charlotte has been
very ill since I wrote you. Her cough was so bad she
never had a night’s rest, and was spitting up blood
very much. The doctor told her husband that there was
nothing more he could do for her. So on the Sunday I
sent, her half a bottle of the Bronchitis Cure, and told
her to try it, and if she did not use it, not to waste it,
but send it back again. She had such confidence in her
doctor that I thought she would not try it. On the
Wednesday I sent over again, and she was much better,
the night’s rest was very good, and cough and bleeding

A Prayer-Cure.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dbewer had not tasted liquor for months, and
lately the uselessness and emptiness of existence
had been worrying him. When he reached home
he kicked open the gate, smashed-in the door,
broke some crockery, and nearly killed the cat.
Then he went to bed with his boots on. Mean-
while, his wife had made her escape and taken
refuge in a neighbour’s house. About two hours
afterwards she ventured back, and when she saw
the battered and disorderly condition of every-
thing, she almost wept with vexation.
Mrs. Roober promptly visited Mrs. Drewer
next day to offer her sympathy—and obtain full
particulars. Mrs. Roober was quite sure such
occurrences were most distressing, but at the
same time strongly advised Mrs. Drewer
to bear-up and not give way to despondency.
As an infallible cure for drunkenness, however,
she patronisingly suggested Prayer, and testified
to several blessed changes that had taken place in
the most hopeless cases. She also dilated on the
necessity of Abundant Faith, whereby we could
always cheerfully bear the burdens of our tribula-
tions. Apropos, she recalled a saying of that dear
old man, Dr. Howlmore, that there was nothing
at all extraordinary about walking on the water —
provided one had sufficient trust in God. As she
was only too anxious to do good when the oppor-
tunity presented itself, and as she was not particu-
larly busy on Wednesday, she would bring a
couple of friends with her to engage in prayer.
Meanwhile, for the present edification and spiri-
tual consolation of Mrs. Drewer, she read and
commented upon the 12th chapter of the Epistle
of Paul to the Hebrews (verses 1 to 6), and, after
graciously accepting another cup of tea, took her
departure.
On the appointed day, Mrs. Roober brought
two of her friends —Mrs. Trowney and Mrs.
Noles. A more, objectionable selection, however,
could not have been made, since Mrs. Drewer in
her heart hated the sight of Mrs. Trowney, and
mortally detested Mrs. Noles. Nobody knew this
better than the same women, and that, no doubt,
was why they had come. After the visitors had
solemnly kissed Mrs. Drewer, and had piously ex-
changed greetings, Mrs. Roober formally opened
ths proceedings.
“Dear sisters, we have come this afternoon to
perform a serious duty. In this world of sin and
sorrow it often happens that a sister, or a brother,
is weak and wayward, and departs from God’s
grace. In this instance it is a brother—the
husband of our dear and afflicted sister who is
among us to-day. Let us pray ! ”
Mrs. Roober then prayed with exceeding fervor
and familiarity for the space of twenty minutes,
and concluded her appeal by informing the Lord
that she could plainly trace His Finger in their
dearly beloved sister’s present trouble (which was
doubtless for some very Wise Purpose), and
earnestly hoped and desired that it might be the
means of bringing both husband and wife into
closer communion with Heaven.
Mrs. Trowney then started off by pointing out
to the Lord this other mournful instance of the evils
of the unrestricted sale of liquor, emphasising at
considerable length theurgent and immediate need
of total prohibition, and, at the same time,
endeavored to influence the Lord in that com-
mendable direction by reverently relating to Him
several shocking cases that had come under her
special notice this last month, and which were all
attributable to the infernal agency of the Accursed
Drink. After twenty-five minutes’ rather effec-
tive oration, Mrs. Trowney finished up by tear-
fully demanding, “ ’Ow long, oh Lord, ’ow long ? ”
in a style carefully calculated to impress both the
Almighty and the company present.
Then Mrs. Noles, who, if anything, was of the
opinion that Mr. Drewer was a very bad man, and
that what he really needed was a sudden check
Eut on his career, opened her mouth and uplifted
er voice—
“ Oh, long-sufferin’ Lord ! (Amen !) Has we
come before Thy footstool to-day, Thou knowest
as ’ow we ’ave much to thank Thee for,
and more particularly and especially for Thy
loving kindness and great mercy to hus, oo are
Thy children. And now, oh Lord, to-day, oh
Lord, as I say, oh Lord, we come before Thee on
our bended knees with our ’arts full of trouble—
and woe—and— ‘ a-choo !’ ” Solemnly wiped her
beak and proceeded—“and oh Lord, as I remarked
before, we ’ave come to Thee this day with our
arts filled with ’umbleness, and thankfulness, and
contrition, and devotion as Thou and Thy far-
seeing wisdom knowest, for to supplicate and to
pray, Oh Lord, because, as Thou ’ast said in the
Axe of the Aposels, ‘ Hask and ye shall receive,’
w’ich we now do, oh Lord, to save that unfortunit
man, our pore unsaved brother ”
“ A-men ! ” murmured Mrs. Trowney.
“Ah, yes ! ’’groaned Mrs. Roober.
} “ our pore unsaved brother, the unfortunit
usband of this our dear sister, oo is in our midst
to-day at the present time.”
“ And, oh Lord, we, oo are Thy children, and
hairs to Thy kingdom (though desprit sinners and
undeserving as Thou, in Thy hinfinite wisdom,
knowest), are ’ere to-day, being specially aware of
Thy merciful dealings with us in the past, and
thankful for Thy many favors, and hax of saving
grace, to beg and pray Thee to extend Thy great
mercy and some saving grace to our dear and way-
ward brother, and exert Thy loving influence so
as to bring ’im back to the fold from whence ’e ’as
wandered. For we know, oh Lord, wot the End
must be if Thy saving grace is not extended to
im has it ’as been to hus. (Groans.) For we
know, oh Lord, as ’ow no drunkard can enter the
Kingdom of ’Eaving (‘Amen!’ from Mrs.
Trowney); for we know becors even Thou ’ast
said so, that ’oosoever departs from Thee, oh Lord,
and from the pleasant paths of Thy wonderful
mercy and infinite love, is on the downward
track that leadethtoEverlastin’destruction and un-
speakable torment, into that houter darkness
where there ain’t nothin’but weeping, and wailing,
and gnashing of teeth.” (Heavy groaning, which
encouraged Mrs. Noles to grind out further
details.) “ For we know, Oh Lord, as ’ow there is
to be a Judgment Day set apart, when the just
shall be separated from the unjust, and the ’oly
from the un’oly ; that we ’oo are Thy children,
Oh Lord, shall be placed at Thy right ’and to
enter into the eternal bliss of the fulfilment of
thy promises to hus ; but that they ’oo were
offensive in Thy sight shall be placed on Thy
left ’and to be cast into the flaming lake of fire
and brimstone, which burneth day and night for
ever and ever. So that we can easily understand
Oh Lord, that our dearly beloved sister naturally
feels very hanxious to rescue ’im as from a yawn-
ing gulf into w’ich ’e is about to fall. And itis becors
we are aware of the terrible danger wi’ch ’e is in
that we would specially supplicate Thee, oh Lord,
to take away ’is ’orrible craving w’ich will surely
lead ’im to eternal damnation and everlasting
torment (groans). But, oh Lord (hysterically
punching the chair), save ’im (■ Amen ’ and
groans), save ’im, we beseech Thee ! (‘ Bah !’
muttered Mrs. Drewer.) Show ’im the herror of
his ways, and make ’im one of hus, Thy children,
oh Lord (appreciative groans). Show ’im the
wrong ’e is doing, and oh Lord, give the
dissipated man a new ’art. Show ’im the way to
Thy grace, and make ’im a better man and a
’appy ’usband ’ (more groans). Show ’im that
the ’eavenly light of Thy countenance aint upon
’im (lamentable groans). But, oh dear Lord,
save ’im, save ’im, we beseech Thee. Save
’im from the Evil One, ’oo is the Devil, w’ich is
the Serpent. Put the hobstacle of righteousness
acrost his path on the broad and downward track,
and show ’im that the red glare of ’ell, w’ich he
sees in the distance, is for ’im alongside of the
Seven ’Orned Beast and his Scarlet Woman.”
“ That’s where you’re going to, you wretch ! ”
Mrs. Roober rose horrified.
“Mrs. Drewer!!!” in various tones of excla-
mation.
“Oh, let me hout !” exclaimed Mrs. Trowney,
as though momentarily expecting a judgment, also
rising as hastily as her recent attack of rheumatism
in the legs would allow her.
“ The Lord preserve hus ! ” piously murmured
Mrs. Noles, thereby meaning Mrs. Roober, Mrs.
Trowney, and herself.
“ It is not,” said Mrs. Roober, in tones of mag-
nificent reproach, “the insult to us. That is
nothing. But to impiously make a mockery of
God—”
Mrs. Drewer defiantly pointed to Mrs. Noles,
and hotly asked : “ How can the Lord listen to a
woman like that, who ought to be at home praying
for her own worthless husband, who’s a hundred
times worse ?”
“You’re a liar ! ”
“ And you’re another ! ”
“Really,” remarked Mrs. Trowney, somewhat
apprehensively, “ I don’t feel safe in this ’ouse,
after what’s passed this day ”
But Mrs. Roober, with that beautiful dignity
and simplicity of faith in the divine majesty of
her belief, which is characteristic of the truly
Christian woman, calmly said :
‘‘ My dear woman ! Do not alarm yourself.
This house is quite safe while we are here —but
let us go ! ”

H.S.R. : He dirty Boers. At Ahmedna-
gar, in the Deccan (whither a lot of Boers have
been exiled), they are allowed one bar of soap for
20 men per month, and as this place was con-
demned as insanitary for British troops, and has

First catch your Boer —then cook him !
A Liverpool parson’s son, a soldier, but
evidently not an arsonist by nature, writing from
S’Africa :
After looting the waggons, the General gave the order
to burn the lot, and at 8 o’clock all the waggons were set
alight. I thought this one of the hardest acts I had ever
seen—burning all food-stuffs and waggons, and leaving all
these women on the veldt. All the women could speak
English, and were of the better class of Boers, who had
been reared in good homes, making it all the harder for
them. When the fire started all the women sang one of
the Psalms, asking God to give them victory over their
enemies. It was grand to hear those women singing ; all
had good voices, and took the different parts. I felt a
peculiar sensation run through me, and all the men were
very indignant at the step taken.
Northern black-labor papers begin to com-
plain about the aggressiveness of the Jap. as com-
Eared with the Kanaka. Not only is the small
rown man inclined to go into trade himself and
become a business oppositionist, but even as a
laborer he has turned out “unreliable.” He has
learned to strike. At Thursday Island, recently,
a Jap. crew gave the pearl-shelling co. notice that
it could either remove a certain captain or fight a
general strike. The captain was said to make the
men work too hard. The co. tried to replace the
men, but all the other sailor men thereabouts
shook their heads, and pointed out that the men who
black-legged in such a case got knifed-off gradu-
ally and unaccountably on dark nights, and the
thing wasn’t good enough. So the co. climbed
down and sacked the captain for looking after its
interests too energetically. Talk about the arro-
gance of the white worker!
Mark Twain (who becomes serious in his
twilight days):
Extending the Blessings of Civilisation to our Brother
who Sits in Darkness has been a good trade, and has paid
well, on the whole. There is more money in it, more
sovereignty and other kinds of emolument, than there is
in any other game that is played. But Christendom has
been playing it badly of late years, and must certainly
suffer. She has been so eager to get every stake that
appeared on the green cloth, that the People who Sit in
Darkness have noticed it. They have become suspicious
of the Blessings of Civilisation. More—they have begun
to examine them. This is not well. The Blessings of
Civilisation are all right, and a good commercial property ;
there could not be a better, in a dim light. In the right
kind of light, and at a proper distance, with the goods a
little out of focus, they furnish this desirable exhibit to
the Gentlemen who Sit in Darkness
“Love, Justice, Gentleness, Christianity, Protection to
the Weak, Temperance, Law and Order, Liberty,
Equality, Honorable Dealing, Mercy, Education—and so
on.”
There. Is it good ? Sir, it is pie. It will bring into
camp any idiot that Sits in Darkness anywhere. But not
if we adulterate it. It is proper to be emphatic on that
point. This brand is strictly for Export—apparently.
Apparently. Privately and confidentially, it is nothing
of the kind. Privately and confidentially, it is merely an
outside cover, gay and pretty and attractive, displaying
the special patterns of our Civilisation which we reserve
for Home Consumption, while inside the bale is the
Actual Thing that the Customer Sitting in Darkness buys
with his blood and tears and land and liberty.
We all know that the Business is being ruined. The
reason is not far to seek. It is because our Mr. M’Kinley,
and Mr. Chamberlain, and the Kaiser, and the Czar, and
the French have been exporting the Actual Thine- with
the outside cover left off.

Message.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Long is the journey from man to worm, and full
of trouble and pain,
But short and swift is the journey from man to the
worm again.
We walk erect for a random year,—make love,
make war, make woe,
And where is the God to hold our hands ? Then
down to the dust we go.
What does it matter, when all is said, the lot of
our living here ?
Death will deliver us at the end, and what is there
left to fear ?
What is there here for man to fear who draws
not coward’s breath,
For what can fright the heart of a man whose
dearest friend is Death ?
Take ye no heed of the Future—let Hell and
Heaven go;
Be brave, be true, be tender, be just—and God
will know.
There is no possible happiness, there is no possible
bliss,
Nor wisdom known to sons of men so sure and
true as this.
Live richly while your life-days last, and let your
heart keep young,
God will remember the generous hand before the
praying tongue.
This knowledge comes to the dying man who turns
him to the wall,
That genius counts for less than nothing, and
Goodness counts for all.
N.S.W.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
The Saving of John Sandilands.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It cost Jack Sandilands something to write that
letter to Damaris, telling her that the break had
come at last, and he was leaving by mid-day train
to-morrow.
That sort of thing brings its own punishment;
whether because it is intrinsically wrong or
because most socially-unlawful things are uncom-
fortable, is a still unsettled question. The sense
of wrong-doing or the sense of the uncomfortable-
ness of it all had been growing upon him for some
time. Damaris demanded a certain amount of
public attention, for instance, at the mixed affairs
to which she and her doubtful mother and
divorced sister —who was beyond a doubt —
gained access at times, and it is hard for a man
with a certain social position to keep it and pay
attention to a woman who has none. So the letter
was written. Yet it cost him something to do it,
for “ under the rose ” has fascinations which need
no expounding, and Damaris was one of those
women who bring the wine of life, if not peace
and prosperity, along with them.
And yet he felt a new sense of freedom when it
was posted. Fate had taken upon herself to ruth-
lessly snap the threads that the girl was slowly
gathering into her slim, brown hands, and he was
going away beyond reach of her strange power —
the power to rouse in him that surging wild-
beast jealousy which transformed him even to
himself. His common-sense told him no good
would come of a life ruled by passion and by
Damaris, and fate had done for him what he had
been too weak to do for himself.
And, still, when her answer came he was
disappointed. Reproach or pleading there was
none—she congratulated him upon his good
fortune. “It is time you and I dropped all
nonsense,”she wrote. “Perhaps lam as glad of it
as you are. Good-bye and good luck, if I don’t see
you again. Of course if you have time to drop in
between eleven and twelve —we shall all be at
home, and would like to see you, but do not put
yourself out, for I know you must be busy. lam
glad we are parting—friends.” There was a P.B,
informing him that Charlie Thorn was coming to
take her for a ride in the afternoon, so that there
would be an early lunch if he cared to stay for it.
He had quite decided not to say good-bye until
that letter came. He was strong at least in know-
ing his own weakness—but it was so cool and
sensible —and they were “all” to be there. Rather
a different message from the kind Damaris had
been accustomed to send him. Besides it
would be just as well to warn her to be careful
with Thorn. As a friend he would do that, and
so he went.
Damaris was waiting for him in the tangled
rose-scented garden, that gave an air of past glory
to the creeper-covered “rookery’’she called her
home. She was shelling peas, and the other
voices could be heard distinctly from the back
region. She wore a big, brown hat and a holland
apron, and laughed a little as she noted how care-
ful he was not to touch her when he was obliged to
pass close to her to get a seat on the stone steps.
“ Don’t be afraid ! ” she said.
“It is best to be afraid,” he answered. “I
have only a few moments to stay, you know. Igo
at one o’clock.”
“ And have other friends to say good-bye to. I
suppose you will go on to the Cedars from
here ? _ Don’t forget to say good-bye to your chief
and his family. Miss Reed is at home. I saw
her pass half-an-hour ago.”
“ Yes, I am going to see them, of course.”
“ And to ask Nellie Reed to marry you ? ”
“Yes,” he answered, boldly. “I am going to
try my luck. ”
“She is a nice girl, and a pretty one. Well,
shall I wish you good-bye and good luck again ? ”
She spoke carelessly, and leant back against the
French window. Like most windows in the old
house, it was minus a clasp, and flew open, dis-
closing the dim, flower-scented room beyond—a
tiny drawing-room, full of shabby, comfortable
chairs, a sofa with many cushions, curtains that
were inured to cigar-smoke, and. beyond all—full
of memories. The girl’s big hat tumbled forward,
and the sunlight falling through banksia boughs
touched her dusky hair to bronze, and for the
first time that morning she raised her big mys-
terious eyes to his.
And then there were her lips curved and red—
“ Better not, Jack.”
But the voiceless sentence the bright eyes and
the red lips spoke was a different one.
“ You are a dear, clever girl, Damaris, not to
make a fuss when a thing is inevitable and for the
best, but as it is for the last time I suppose I may
kiss you ? . ,
“ Well—yes, if you promise to go at once, ana
—I suppose you don’t want all the passers-by to

sunned itself on the stone steps, and an adventu-
rous king-parrot made havoc amongst the scattered
pea-pods. No one came to disturb them. The
minutes sped—it would soon be too late for Jack’s
visit to the Cedars too late for wM.

The Syrian gave a pleasant and knowing chuckle,
“Go at once—do you hear me?” cried the girl,

In after life Damaris could never bear the sight
of Syrian hawkers—and sometimes, when the
gilded chains of respectability, monotony, and
law-abidingness weighed heavily, Jack Sandilands

ETHEL MILLS.
Lord Salisbury, the hopeless pessimist of
British politics, apologises for his subserviency to
the Chamberlain-Kynoch gang of pro-Gores :
Fifty or sixty years ago, the leaders whom the public
followed, and who governed the formation and guidance
of political parties, were all men who, like Sir Robert Peel,
valued the advantages of peace, and the economy it
brought, above everything else. But the tide has turned,
and who are we that we should attempt to stem it ? If
the tide has turned we have to go with it! ”
Consider again the magnitude of things
American ! New York municipal improvements
—projected and in progress—are to cost nearly
700,000,000 dollars ! —just about double the
N.S.W. State debt. Rapid transit facilities
account for the biggest items ; a four-track tunnel
contract is for £7,000,000; and an extension
beneath the East River is to cost £3,000,000 more,
F ive new bridges are to cost £26,000,000, and im-
provements of parks and streets the enormous
sum of £17,000,000.

The new and really fine block which this poverty-
stricken organisation, so “ urgently in need of funds,” has

the social and missionary operations of the Army.” They
will be taught the correct smile to accompany the infor-
mation that “another 3d. will make a shilling, God
bless you,” and other qualifications for the field. It is
related that some time ago the then training garrison
was controlled by an individual who had considerable
tal ® nt and a sense of humor, and was not half
a bad fellow all round. He thought he would inculcate
a knowledge 0 f logic and debate by a mock “ trial of the
Devil.” The defendant, who was charged with “obtain
ing money and time under false pretences,” failed to
appear, being engaged elsewhere, but was represented by
counsel—the officer above-mentioned. Cadets were cast
for judge, jury, police, witnesses, reporters and officials,
and things were conducted on Supreme Court lines.
